Defining Ayat and Bayyinat: Linguistic and Theological Analysis
Expansion of the matn's opening definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of al-bayyinat as defined in Hanafi usul and explain the ontological distinction between Allah's haqiqah and creation's majaz.

Core Idea
Al-bayyinat encompasses Quranic verses, manifest sunnah, and clear miracles—all decisive proofs that admit no doubt.

This passage opens the commentary on Muslim al-Thubut with a word-by-word analysis of the matn's first lines. The commentator defines key terms: 'ayat' as Quranic verses, 'bayyinat' as clear proofs comprising three categories, and 'haqiqah' as real existence belonging only to Allah. Understanding these definitions is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text phrases
Locate the parenthesized words in the Arabic passage—these are the matn phrases being commented upon. This shows which parts of the original text the commentator is expanding.
Parentheses indicate matn extracts.
2
Define 'ayat' and 'bayyinat'
Note how the commentator defines 'ayat' as a piece of Allah's speech (Quran) and 'bayyinat' as clear and evident words. Understand that 'bayyinat' is broader than just Quran.
Bayyinat includes three types.
3
List the three components of bayyinat
From the commentary, extract the three categories: al-ayat al-muhkamat (firm verses), al-sunan al-jaliyyah (manifest sunnah), and al-mu'jizat al-bayyinah (evident miracles).
These are the sources of decisive proof.
4
Analyze the grammatical notes
The commentator parses 'fatala'a' (so rose) and discusses possible readings of 'al-din' (raf' or nasb). This shows the importance of linguistic analysis in usul.
Grammar affects meaning, but here the focus is on definitions.
5
Explain the ontological distinction
Examine the phrase 'Rabbana laka al-haqiqah'—haqiqah means actuality, which belongs to Allah alone because He is self-existing. Everything else exists metaphorically (majaz).
This theological point underpins how proofs are categorized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(المقدمة في حدّ أصول الفقه وموضوعه وغايته) (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم أبتدىء (الحمد لله الذي نزل على ما تقتضيه الحكمة (الآيات) قطعة وهي من كلام الله تعالى (وأرسل (البينات أي الكلمات البينة الواضحة، وهي الآيات المحكمة، والسنن الجلية والمعجزات البينة الظاهرة، لا تحتمل الريب والارتياب (فطلع) من الطلوع أو التطليع (الدين) بالرفع أو النصب وطبع اليقين يحتمل الوجهين (ربنا لك الحقيقة) أي الواقعية (حقاً) لأنك الكائن بنفسك (وكل) ممن سواك (مجاز) في الواقعية إذ لا وجود لهم
(The introduction concerning the definition of Usul al-Fiqh, its subject, and its goal.) (In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, I begin.) (Praise be to Allah who revealed according to wisdom) (the verses) a piece of the speech of Allah (and He sent) (the clear proofs) i.e., the clear and evident words, and they are the firm verses, the manifest sunan, and the evident miracles that do not admit doubt or suspicion. (So rose) from rising or causing to rise. (the religion) with raf' or nasb. (and imprinted certainty) accepts both aspects. (Our Lord, to You is the reality) i.e., the actuality (truly) because You are the self-existing (and everything) other than You is metaphorical in actuality, as they have no existence by themselves.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the introduction (muqaddimah) to the commentary on Muslim al-Thubut. The commentator takes the opening words of the matn and explains each one. The matn begins with 'Bismillah' and 'Alhamdulillah', then mentions that Allah revealed 'ayat' (verses) and sent 'bayyinat' (clear proofs). The commentator defines 'ayat' as pieces of Allah's speech (i.e., the Quran). He then defines 'bayyinat' as clear and evident words, which include three categories: the firm (muhkam) verses of the Quran, the manifest sunan (prophetic traditions), and the clear miracles (mujizat). He emphasizes that these proofs admit no doubt or suspicion. Then he parses the word 'fatala'a' (so rose) and discusses its possible derivations. He also discusses the parsing of 'al-din' (the religion) and 'taba'a al-yaqin' (imprinted certainty), noting that both raf' and nasb are possible. Finally, he explains the phrase 'Rabbana laka al-haqiqah' (Our Lord, to You is the reality), interpreting 'haqiqah' as actuality or real existence, which applies to Allah because He is self-existing, whereas everything else exists only metaphorically. This commentary demonstrates the author's method of combining linguistic analysis with theological depth.

Key Terms
الآيات (al-ayat): قطعة من كلام الله تعالى (a piece of Allah's speech) – the Quranic verses.
البينات (al-bayyinat): الكلمات البينة الواضحة (clear and evident words) – including muhkam verses, manifest sunnah, and clear miracles.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): الواقعية (actuality/real existence) – applies to Allah as self-existing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does 'al-bayyinat' refer to?
A) Only the Quran
B) Clear verses of the Quran
C) Clear verses, manifest sunnah, and clear miracles
D) Only miracles

Key Takeaway
Al-bayyinat are the clear textual proofs comprising the firm verses of the Quran, the manifest sunnah, and the evident miracles—all decisive and free from doubt.

The Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh
Defining the subject of usul as the four proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the subject matter of usul al-fiqh according to the Hanafi school and explain why rulings are not part of it.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh studies the four proofs as they lead to rulings; rulings themselves are not its true subject.

This passage defines the scope of usul al-fiqh by declaring its subject: the four proofs. The author clarifies that rulings (ahkam) are only mentioned by way of digression, not as an integral part of the science. Understanding this boundary is essential for distinguishing between the tools of derivation and the derived rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the declared subject
The passage states that the subject of usul al-fiqh is the four proofs (Qur'an, Sunna, Ijma', Qiyas) considered in a general way, not absolutely, but as they share the characteristic of conveying a legal ruling.
Shared characteristic prevents the science from fragmenting.
2
Note the condition
The proofs are studied 'in the state of being shared in conveying to a legal ruling.' This means usul focuses on how proofs lead to rulings, not on the proofs themselves in isolation.
3
Recognize the dispute about including rulings
Some scholars (like Sadr al-Shari'a and the author of al-Ahkam) held that rulings are also part of the subject. The author rejects this, arguing that rulings are only mentioned for depiction and diversification.
The well-known (mashhur) position is that only proofs are the subject.
4
Understand the author's conclusion
Rulings are discussed in usul only by digression (istitrad) for completion and repair, not as an essential subject. This is a standard practice in all sciences.
The author uses the phrase 'tatmiman wa tarmiman' (for completion and repair).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ عن رسم العلم شرع في بيان الموضوع فقال (موضوعه الأدلة الأربعة إجمالاً لا مطلقاً بل حال كونها مشتركة في الإيصال إلى حكم شرعي) ولأجل هذا الاشتراك لم يتعدد علم الأصول بتعدد الموضوع، ثم لما كان موضوع «الأصول» الأدلة لم تكن حجية هذه الحجج من الأصول»، لكن من أي علم هو فيه خلاف فمن زاعم زعم الفقه وأشار إليه المصنف بقوله (وما قيل إن البحث عن حجية الإجماع والقياس من الفقه إذ المعنى من حجيتهما أنه يجب العمل بمقتضاهما) فقد أثبت الوجوب للعمل الذي هو فعل المكلف فدخلت في الفقه ففيه أن هذا أي وجوب العمل (فرع الحجية) لا نفسها، وكان الكلام في أن إثبات الحجية من أي علم هو وليس من الفقه البتة على أن جواز العمل أيضاً من ثمرات الحجية فلا يصح دعوى وجوب العمل عموماً، ولعله إنما ذكر وجوب العمل مثلاً، ولا يضر هذا أصل المقصود للقائل كما لا يخفى ومن زاعم زعم أنها ليست من علم، وإليه أشار بقوله (ومن قال ليست مسألة أصلاً لأنها ضرورية وبينة والضروريات لا تثبت في أصل) فقد بعد عن الحق لأنه وإن سلم أنها ضرورية (إنا فلا يسلم أنها ضرورية (لما) فلا بد من البحث عن لميتها قال واقف «أسرار الأصول والفروع أن في نقل المصنف اضطراباً، فإنه نقل في كتاب آخر له أن القياس على تقدير كونه فعلاً من الفقه، وأما إن كان عبارة عن المساواة المعتبرة شرعاً فحجيته ضرورية وبينة كما سيصرح في السنة أن حجيتها ضرورية وبينة، وأيضاً لا وجه يظهر للمنع المرموز بقوله : وإن سلم إنا ومن ذاهب ذهب إلى أنها من الكلام وهو المختار وأشار إليه بقوله بل الحق أنه من الكلام كحجية الكتاب والسنة) فإن قلت : فلماذا تذكر في (الأصول) أجاب (لكن) تعرض الأصولي لحجيتهما فقط) دون أخويهما (لأنهما كثر فيهما الشغب من الحمقى من الخوارج والروافض خذلهم الله تعالى : (وأما حجيتهما) أي الكتاب والسنة فمتفق عليها عند الأمة ممن يدعي التدين كافة فلا حاجة إلى الذكر (وفي موضوعية الأحكام مع الأدلة (اختلاف) فذهب صاحب الأحكام من الشافعية وصدر الشريعة من الحنفية إلى أنهما موضوعان لأنه يبحث عن أحوالهما ولا إلجاء إلى الاستطراد والمشهور أن الموضوع الأدلة فحسب، والأحكام خارجة واختاره المصنف رحمه الله، وقال (والحق لا تؤخذ موضوعاً وإنما الغرض من البحث عن الأحكام التصوير والتنويع فقط لا بيان عوارضه الذاتية بالذات ليثبت أنواعها أي أنواع الأحكام بأنواع الأدلة، وما من علم إلا ويذكر فيه الأشياء استطراداً تتميماً وترميماً فلا بأس بكون مباحث الأحكام كذلك، ولما فرغ عن بيان الموضوع شرع في الأمر الثالث الذي هو الغاية، وقال (وفائدته معرفة الأحكام الشرعية عن الأدلة على الوجه الذي بينا (وهي) أي معرفة الأحكام (وسيلة إلى الفوز بالسعادة الأبدية).
And when he finished with the definition of knowledge, he began to explain the subject, saying: 'Its subject is the four proofs in a general way, not absolutely, but rather in the state of being shared in conveying to a legal ruling.' And because of this sharing, the science of usul is not multiplied by the multiplicity of the subject. Then, since the subject of usul is the proofs, the authority (hujjiyya) of these proofs is not from usul, but from which science it is a matter of dispute. Some claim it is from fiqh, and the author referred to that with his saying: 'And what was said that the investigation into the authority of ijma' and qiyas is from fiqh, because the meaning of their authority is that action must be taken in accordance with them'—so he affirmed the obligation for action, which is an act of the legally responsible person, so it enters into fiqh. In it, this, i.e., the obligation of action, is a branch of authority, not itself. And the discussion was about the proof of authority being from which science, and it is not from fiqh at all, even though permissibility of action is also a result of authority. So the claim of obligation of action in general is not correct. Perhaps he only mentioned the obligation of action as an example, and this does not harm the original intent of the speaker, as is clear. And some claim that it is not from any science, and to that the author referred with his saying: 'And whoever said that it is not a matter at all because it is necessary and self-evident, and self-evident things are not established in a science'—he has strayed from the truth. Because even if it is conceded that it is necessary, it is not conceded that it is necessary for a reason, so there must be an investigation into its cause. The author of 'Asrar al-Usul wa al-Furu' said that there is inconsistency in the author's transmission, for he transmitted in another book of his: 'If qiyas is considered an act, it is from fiqh; but if it is an expression of the legally considered equality, then its authority is necessary and self-evident'—just as he will clarify concerning the sunna that its authority is necessary and self-evident. Also, there is no apparent justification for the refusal indicated by his saying: 'And if we concede that...' And the one who went went to that it is from kalam, and it is the chosen position, and he indicated to that with his saying: 'Rather the truth is that it is from kalam, like the authority of the Book and the Sunna.' If you say: So why is it mentioned in usul? He answered: 'But the usuli deals only with their authority'—without their two counterparts—'because there has been much disturbance about them from the fools among the Khawarij and the Rawafid, may Allah forsake them. As for their authority (the Book and Sunna), it is agreed upon by the whole community of those who claim religiosity, so there is no need to mention them.' (And concerning the inclusion of rulings with the proofs, there is disagreement). The author of al-Ahkam among the Shafi'is and Sadr al-Shari'a among the Hanafis went to that both are the subject, because he investigates their states and there is no need for digression. The well-known is that the subject is only the proofs, and rulings are external. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) chose this, and said: 'The truth is that rulings are not taken as subject; rather the purpose of investigating rulings is only for depiction and diversification, not to demonstrate their essential properties per se, to establish the types of rulings by the types of proofs. There is no science except that things are mentioned in it by digression for completion and repair, so there is no harm in the topics of rulings being thus.' And when he finished with the subject, he began the third matter, which is the goal, and said: 'Its benefit is the knowledge of legal rulings from the proofs in the manner we have explained. And it (i.e., knowledge of rulings) is a means to attaining eternal happiness.'
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage addresses two major issues in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the subject matter of the discipline and the nature of the authority (hujjiyya) of the proofs. The commentator informs us that the subject of usul is the four proofs (Qur'an, Sunna, Ijma', Qiyas) insofar as they share the characteristic of leading to a legal ruling. This shared aspect prevents the science from fragmenting into multiple subjects. Then the discussion shifts to which science investigates the hujjiyya (binding authority) of these proofs. Three positions are mentioned: it belongs to fiqh (because hujjiyya entails obligation of action), it is not part of any science (because it is self-evident), or it belongs to kalam (theology). The commentator adopts the view that it is from kalam, as the authority of the Book and Sunna is necessary and self-evident, but the authority of ijma' and qiyas has been contested, so they are treated in usul to counter the objections of heretical groups. The passage also clarifies that rulings (ahkam) are not part of the subject matter of usul; they are discussed only by digression for illustrative purposes. Finally, the benefit of usul is stated as attaining knowledge of legal rulings, which leads to eternal happiness.

Key Terms
موضوع الأصول (mawdu' al-usul): The subject matter of usul al-fiqh: the four proofs (Qur'an, Sunna, Ijma', Qiyas) as they convey legal rulings.
استطراد (istitrad): Digression; mentioning something not part of the primary subject for completion or clarification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's chosen view, what is the subject matter of usul al-fiqh?
A) The four proofs and the rulings they generate
B) Only the four proofs, considered in their function of conveying rulings
C) The authority (hujjiyya) of the four proofs
D) The benefit of achieving eternal happiness

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh centers on the four proofs as they lead to rulings; rulings themselves are discussed only by digression.

The Scientific Home of Hujjiyya: Kalam, Fiqh, or Self-Evident?
Debating the scientific home of hujjiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the scientific home of hujjiyya and explain why the Hanafi school assigns it to kalam.

Core Idea
The authority of proofs is a kalam issue, not fiqh or self-evident; usul only addresses it for contested proofs.

This passage engages a fundamental debate: to which science does the investigation of hujjiyya (the binding authority of proofs) belong? The author presents three views—fiqh, self-evident (not part of any science), and kalam—and argues for the kalam position. Understanding this allocation clarifies the boundary between usul and theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three positions
The passage lists three contenders: (1) Fiqh: because hujjiyya entails obligation of action, which is a fiqh matter. (2) Self-evident: hujjiyya is so obvious it needs no proof, hence not part of any science. (3) Kalam: the chosen view, that hujjiyya is a theological inquiry.
The author uses 'mukhtar' (chosen) for the kalam view.
2
Analyze the refutation of the fiqh position
The fiqh claim is that hujjiyya leads to wajib al-'amal (obligation of action). But the author responds that the obligation of action is a branch (far') of hujjiyya, not the essence. The essence is the authority itself, which is a kalam question.
The phrase 'far' al-hujjiyya' is key.
3
Analyze the refutation of the self-evident position
The self-evident view claims hujjiyya is necessary (daruri) and self-evident (bayyina). The author concedes it may be necessary but argues that it is not necessary for a reason (li-ma); there must be an investigation into its cause ('illah). This investigation belongs to kalam.
The objection uses the concept of 'illah (cause) to require scientific inquiry.
4
Understand why hujjiyya is discussed in usul despite being a kalam issue
The author explains that the authority of the Book and Sunna is universally accepted, so usul does not need to discuss it. But for ijma' and qiyas, their authority was contested by heretical groups (Khawarij, Rawafid), so usul scholars must address it to defend the proofs.
This shows usul's role in polemics against deviant groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

موضوع «الأصول» الأدلة لم تكن حجية هذه الحجج من الأصول»، لكن من أي علم هو فيه خلاف فمن زاعم زعم الفقه وأشار إليه المصنف بقوله (وما قيل إن البحث عن حجية الإجماع والقياس من الفقه إذ المعنى من حجيتهما أنه يجب العمل بمقتضاهما) فقد أثبت الوجوب للعمل الذي هو فعل المكلف فدخلت في الفقه ففيه أن هذا أي وجوب العمل (فرع الحجية) لا نفسها، وكان الكلام في أن إثبات الحجية من أي علم هو وليس من الفقه البتة على أن جواز العمل أيضاً من ثمرات الحجية فلا يصح دعوى وجوب العمل عموماً، ولعله إنما ذكر وجوب العمل مثلاً، ولا يضر هذا أصل المقصود للقائل كما لا يخفى ومن زاعم زعم أنها ليست من علم، وإليه أشار بقوله (ومن قال لا وجه يظهر للمنع المرموز بقوله : وإن سلم إنا ومن ذاهب ذهب إلى أنها من الكلام وهو المختار وأشار إليه بقوله بل الحق أنه من الكلام كحجية الكتاب والسنة) فإن قلت : فلماذا تذكر في (الأصول) أجاب (لكن) تعرض الأصولي لحجيتهما فقط) دون أخويهما (لأنهما كثر فيهما الشغب من الحمقى من الخوارج والروافض خذلهم الله تعالى : (وأما حجيتهما) أي الكتاب والسنة فمتفق عليها عند الأمة ممن يدعي التدين كافة فلا حاجة إلى الذكر (وفي موضوعية الأحكام مع الأدلة (اختلاف) فذهب صاحب الأحكام من الشافعية وصدر الشريعة من الحنفية إلى أنهما موضوعان لأنه يبحث عن أحوالهما ولا إلجاء إلى الاستطراد والمشهور أن الموضوع الأدلة فحسب، والأحكام خارجة واختاره المصنف رحمه الله، وقال (والحق لا تؤخذ موضوعاً وإنما الغرض من البحث عن الأحكام التصوير والتنويع فقط لا بيان عوارضه الذاتية بالذات ليثبت أنواعها أي أنواع الأحكام بأنواع الأدلة، وما من علم إلا ويذكر فيه الأشياء استطراداً تتميماً وترميماً فلا بأس بكون مباحث الأحكام كذلك، ولما فرغ عن بيان الموضوع شرع في الأمر الثالث الذي هو الغاية، وقال (وفائدته معرفة الأحكام الشرعية عن الأدلة على الوجه الذي بينا (وهي) أي معرفة الأحكام (وسيلة إلى الفوز بالسعادة الأبدية).
And when he finished with the definition of knowledge, he began to explain the subject, saying: 'Its subject is the four proofs in a general way, not absolutely, but rather in the state of being shared in conveying to a legal ruling.' And because of this sharing, the science of usul is not multiplied by the multiplicity of the subject. Then, since the subject of usul is the proofs, the authority (hujjiyya) of these proofs is not from usul, but from which science it is a matter of dispute. Some claim it is from fiqh, and the author referred to that with his saying: 'And what was said that the investigation into the authority of ijma' and qiyas is from fiqh, because the meaning of their authority is that action must be taken in accordance with them'—so he affirmed the obligation for action, which is an act of the legally responsible person, so it enters into fiqh. In it, this, i.e., the obligation of action, is a branch of authority, not itself. And the discussion was about the proof of authority being from which science, and it is not from fiqh at all, even though permissibility of action is also a result of authority. So the claim of obligation of action in general is not correct. Perhaps he only mentioned the obligation of action as an example, and this does not harm the original intent of the speaker, as is clear. And some claim that it is not from any science, and to that the author referred with his saying: 'And whoever said that it is not a matter at all because it is necessary and self-evident, and self-evident things are not established in a science'—he has strayed from the truth. Because even if it is conceded that it is necessary, it is not conceded that it is necessary for a reason, so there must be an investigation into its cause. The author of 'Asrar al-Usul wa al-Furu' said that there is inconsistency in the author's transmission, for he transmitted in another book of his: 'If qiyas is considered an act, it is from fiqh; but if it is an expression of the legally considered equality, then its authority is necessary and self-evident'—just as he will clarify concerning the sunna that its authority is necessary and self-evident. Also, there is no apparent justification for the refusal indicated by his saying: 'And if we concede that...' And the one who went went to that it is from kalam, and it is the chosen position, and he indicated to that with his saying: 'Rather the truth is that it is from kalam, like the authority of the Book and the Sunna.' If you say: So why is it mentioned in usul? He answered: 'But the usuli deals only with their authority'—without their two counterparts—'because there has been much disturbance about them from the fools among the Khawarij and the Rawafid, may Allah forsake them. As for their authority (the Book and Sunna), it is agreed upon by the whole community of those who claim religiosity, so there is no need to mention them.' (And concerning the inclusion of rulings with the proofs, there is disagreement). The author of al-Ahkam among the Shafi'is and Sadr al-Shari'a among the Hanafis went to that both are the subject, because he investigates their states and there is no need for digression. The well-known is that the subject is only the proofs, and rulings are external. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) chose this, and said: 'The truth is that rulings are not taken as subject; rather the purpose of investigating rulings is only for depiction and diversification, not to demonstrate their essential properties per se, to establish the types of rulings by the types of proofs. There is no science except that things are mentioned in it by digression for completion and repair, so there is no harm in the topics of rulings being thus.' And when he finished with the subject, he began the third matter, which is the goal, and said: 'Its benefit is the knowledge of legal rulings from the proofs in the manner we have explained. And it (i.e., knowledge of rulings) is a means to attaining eternal happiness.'
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage addresses two major issues in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the subject matter of the discipline and the nature of the authority (hujjiyya) of the proofs. The commentator informs us that the subject of usul is the four proofs (Qur'an, Sunna, Ijma', Qiyas) insofar as they share the characteristic of leading to a legal ruling. This shared aspect prevents the science from fragmenting into multiple subjects. Then the discussion shifts to which science investigates the hujjiyya (binding authority) of these proofs. Three positions are mentioned: it belongs to fiqh (because hujjiyya entails obligation of action), it is not part of any science (because it is self-evident), or it belongs to kalam (theology). The commentator adopts the view that it is from kalam, as the authority of the Book and Sunna is necessary and self-evident, but the authority of ijma' and qiyas has been contested, so they are treated in usul to counter the objections of heretical groups. The passage also clarifies that rulings (ahkam) are not part of the subject matter of usul; they are discussed only by digression for illustrative purposes. Finally, the benefit of usul is stated as attaining knowledge of legal rulings, which leads to eternal happiness.

Key Terms
الحجية (al-hujjiyya): The quality of being a valid proof that requires compliance; the authority of a source of law.
الضرورية (al-daruriyya): Necessary knowledge; truths that are self-evident and require no proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen Hanafi view, to which science does the essential investigation of hujjiyya belong?
A) Fiqh
B) Usul al-fiqh
C) 'Ilm al-kalam
D) Logic

Key Takeaway
The nature of proof authority is a kalam question; usul only addresses it for contested proofs to defend against heresy.

Speculative Reasoning as a Source of Certain Knowledge
Debates whether speculative reasoning yields certain knowledge, refuting skeptics and discussing Ash'ari and Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that speculative reasoning (nazar) yields certain knowledge and identify the Hanafi response to the skeptic challenge.

Core Idea
Sound speculative reasoning yields certain knowledge; the skeptic's doubt fails because intuition distinguishes knowledge from ignorance.

This passage addresses a foundational question in usul al-fiqh: can rational reasoning produce certain knowledge? The Hanafi school affirms that it can, refuting skeptics who restrict knowledge to sense perception. The argument is crucial because the validity of qiyas (analogical reasoning) – a key source of law – depends on the reliability of nazar. Understanding this epistemological defense strengthens your appreciation of how Hanafi usul establishes rational proof as a legitimate source of legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
State the Hanafi position: speculative reasoning (nazar), when correctly performed, necessarily produces certain knowledge ('ilm). The text says 'النظر مفيد للعلم بالضرورة' (speculative reasoning is beneficial for knowledge necessarily).
This claim is directed against the Sumaniyya skeptics who deny any knowledge beyond sense perception.
2
Analyze the Skeptic's Objection
The skeptic argues that certainty after reasoning may actually be ignorance, because ignorance can resemble knowledge. Since both appear similar, how can we distinguish them? The text states: 'الجزم قد يكون جهلاً وهو مثل العلم' (certainty may be ignorance, and it is like knowledge).
This is a classic skeptical challenge that targets the very possibility of rational certainty.
3
Examine the First Response: Parity with Sense Perception
The Hanafi response first argues by parity: the same problem applies to sense perception – we may be certain about a sense perception that later turns out to be false (e.g., a mirage). So if the skeptic rejects reasoning on this ground, they must also reject sense perception, which is absurd.
This rebuttal shows that the skeptic's criterion is self-defeating.
4
Examine the Second Response: Intuitive Judgment (Badiha)
The second response appeals to innate intuition (al-badīha) which judges without doubt that correct reasoning yields knowledge, not ignorance. The text says: 'البديهة الغير المكذوبة حاكمة بأن الحاصل بعد النظر الصحيح علم لا جهل' (undisputed intuition judges that what results after correct reasoning is knowledge, not ignorance).
This move grounds certainty in a self-evident, foundational cognitive faculty.
5
Address the Further Objection and the Author's Resolution
A further objection asks: how do we know that a particular instance of reasoning is 'correct'? The possibility of error is always present. The author counters that one can know the correctness of reasoning either by necessity (badiha again) or by deduction from necessary premises. Ultimately, the strongest answer is to deny any real similarity between knowledge and ignorance – they are two distinct types (type-distinction). The text concludes: 'الحق منع التماثل' (the truth is preventing similarity).
The type-distinction argument resolves the skeptic's appeal to resemblance: even if they appear similar, they are categorically different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الأولى في المبادىء الكلامية

مسألة [فائدة النظر في العلم]
النظر مفيد للعلم بالضرورة الغير المكذوبة السمنية نفوا إفادة النظر العلم مطلقاً قائلين بأن لا علم إلا بالحس وهذا زيادة في حماقتهم وشبهتهم هذه لأن الجزم قد يكون جهلاً وهو) أي الجهل (مثل العلم، فبماذا يعلم أن الحاصل بعده أي النظر (علم) لا جهل (ويجاب) أولاً بأن هذا جار في المحسوسات أيضاً، فإن الجزم الحاصل بعد الحس قد يكون جهلاً، فلا يفيد الحس العلم أيضاً، وثانياً بأنه يتميز بالعوارض، فإن البديهة الغير المكذوبة (حاكمة بأن الحاصل بعد النظر الصحيح علم لا جهل أقول : وفيه أنه بماذا يعلم أن أي هذا النظر الذي ادعيتم صحته نظر صحيح فإن (الاحتمال أي احتمال عدم الصحة (قائم من المبادىء إلى المقاطع مثلاً بمثل فلا يعلم صحة هذا النظر أبداً، فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يعلم صحة النظر بالحس أو بمقدمات تعلم به قال والحس لا يفيد إلا علماً جزئياً وهو) أي العلم الجزئي (لا يكون كاسباً فلا يحصل به علم أصلاً واعلم أن هذا الإيراد ليس بشيء، فإن للمجيب أن يقول : يجوز أن يحصل العلم بصحة النظر بالضرورة أو بالكسب عن مقدمات معلومة بالضرورة، وقيام الاحتمال بعد حكم الضرورة ممنوع ، فتدبر وأنصف، والحق لا يتجاوز عنه بل الحق) في الجواب (منع التماثل أي تماثل العلم والجهل، بل هما نوعان متباينان (كما هو مذهبنا فتدبر) وهذا أيضاً غير واف فإن مقصود صاحب الشبهة من التماثل التشابه بحيث لا يتميزان في أول الأمر، وهما كذلك، لأن الجزم ربما يكون علماً، وربما يكون جهلاً، فلا يتميزان في أول الأمر، فتعود الشبهة كما كانت فافهم وأنصف.

مسألة
،
(قال) الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري) رحمه الله إن الإفادة أي إفادة النظر الصحيح العلم (بالعادة) أي بجري عادة الله تعالى بأن يحدث العلم عقيب النظر إذ لا مؤثر) في الوجود عنده إلا الله) كما نطقت به الشريعة الحقة، بحيث لا مساغ للارتياب فيه فالمؤثر في وجود العلم الله تعالى : (بلا) وجوب منه تعالى على زعم الأشعري ولا عليه فبالعادة ولم يدر الوجود من غير وجوب ترجيح بل ترجح من غير مرجح (و) قال (المعتزلة إنه) أي حصول
The First Article on Theological Principles

Issue [Benefit of Speculative Reasoning for Knowledge]
Speculative reasoning is beneficial for knowledge necessarily, not falsified. The Sumaniyya denied the benefit of reasoning for knowledge absolutely, saying there is no knowledge except through sense perception, and this is an excess in their foolishness. Their skeptic argument is: because certainty may be ignorance, and it (i.e., ignorance) is like knowledge, so how does one know that what results after it (i.e., reasoning) is knowledge and not ignorance? (And it is answered) first by saying that this applies also to sense perceptions, for the certainty obtained after sense perception may be ignorance, so sense perception also does not benefit knowledge; and secondly by saying that it is distinguished by accidents, for undisputed intuition judges that what results after correct reasoning is knowledge, not ignorance. I say: In it (the answer) there is that how does one know that this reasoning which you claim to be correct is correct reasoning? For (the possibility, i.e., the possibility of incorrectness, is present from the premises to the conclusions equally, so the correctness of this reasoning is never known. If it is said: why is it not permissible that the correctness of reasoning be known by sense perception or by premises known through it? He said: sense perception only yields particular knowledge, and it (i.e., particular knowledge) is not acquisitive, so no knowledge is obtained through it at all. Know that this objection is nothing, for the responder can say: it is permissible that knowledge of the correctness of reasoning be obtained necessarily or by acquisition from premises known necessarily, and the existence of possibility after the judgment of necessity is prevented, so ponder and be just. The truth is not beyond it, rather the truth) in the answer is (preventing similarity, i.e., similarity of knowledge and ignorance, rather they are two distinct types (as is our doctrine, so ponder). But this is also insufficient, because the intention of the author of the skeptic argument from similarity is the resemblance such that they are not distinguished at first, and they are indeed such, because certainty may be knowledge and may be ignorance, so they are not distinguished at first, so the skeptic argument returns as it was, so understand and be just.

Issue
(Al-Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) said, may Allah have mercy on him: The benefit, i.e., the benefit of correct reasoning for knowledge, is by habit, i.e., by the continuous practice of Allah Almighty of creating knowledge subsequent to reasoning, since there is no effect in existence with Him except Allah, as the true Shari'a has spoken, such that there is no room for doubt in it. So the effecter in the existence of knowledge is Allah Almighty, without obligation from Him Almighty according to al-Ash'ari's claim, nor upon Him, so by habit. And he did not know existence without obligation of preference, rather it is preferred without a preferrer. (And) the Mu'tazila said: It (i.e., its occurrence)
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage deals with the fundamental usul principle that speculative reasoning (nazar) yields certain knowledge. The author refutes the skeptic position (attributed to the Sumaniyya) who claim knowledge only from sense perception. The skeptic objection is that certainty (al-jazm) after reasoning could be ignorance, so how can we know it is knowledge? The response first analogizes to sense perception (which also can produce false certainty), then appeals to intuitive judgment (badiha) that distinguishes correct reasoning. A further objection is raised: how do we know reasoning is correct? Possibility of error is always present. The author counters that knowledge of correctness can be obtained necessarily or through necessary premises. He then presents al-Ash'ari's view that knowledge is created by habit ('adah) of Allah following reasoning, and the Mu'tazila view that it is obligatory. This discussion is important for establishing the authority of rational proof (dalil 'aqli) in Hanafi usul, which relies on the validity of nazar to derive rulings from textual evidence.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar): Speculative reasoning; the intellectual process of deriving knowledge from premises.
البديهة (al-badīha): Natural intuition or immediate judgment, not dependent on reasoning.
العادة (al-‘āda): Habit; the regular practice of Allah in creating effects after causes, as per Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By sense perception.
B) By the judgment of undisputed intuition (badiha).
C) By consensus of scholars.
D) By the impossibility of error in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Correct speculative reasoning (nazar) yields certain knowledge, and the Hanafi defense of this principle is essential for the validity of qiyas as a source of law.

Interpreting Quranic Threats: Literal or Deterrent?
Debate on whether threatening verses apply literally to all sinners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of deviating from the apparent meaning (ظاهر) when a certain proof (قطعي جلي) exists, using the example of Quranic threats for non-polytheist believers.

Core Idea
Certain proof of forgiveness requires reinterpreting Quranic threats as deterrents, not literal promises of punishment.

In this passage, the author resolves a tension between the apparent meaning of Quranic threat verses and the certain principle of divine forgiveness for believers. The Hanafi school argues that the apparent meaning must be departed from when there is a certain proof that contradicts it. This lesson walks through the reasoning and the practical implications for interpreting verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognise the apparent meaning (ظاهر) of Quranic threats (وعيد) and why it seems to conflict with the possibility of forgiveness for believers.
The threat is general; the issue is whether it must be taken literally.
2
Recognise the opposing argument
Some scholars argue that restriction (تقييد) or reinterpretation is a deviation without a valid cause, and that the same reasoning would apply to promises (وعد), leading to absurd consequences.
The counterargument is that if we reinterpret threats, we might also reinterpret promises, undermining the certainty of reward.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the cause for deviation exists—a certain proof (قطعي جلي) that forgiveness is permissible for non-polytheist major sinners. This necessitates departing from the literal threat, either by restricting its application or treating it as a deterrent (إنشاء التخويف).
Certainty here is compared to the sun at midday—indisputable.
4
Apply the distinction
Promises remain literal because no such certain proof contradicts them. Threats specific to polytheists also remain literal because the cause for deviation does not apply.
Thus, a single verse can be a deterrent for believers and a literal threat for polytheists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إيراد، ولك أن تقلب عليه بأن التقييد عدول عن الحقيقة بلا موجب ومثله يجري في الوعد أيضاً، فيلزم جواز تعذيب الموعود بالجنة بغير حساب جوازاً وقوعياً، فالحق أن الموجب للعدول متحقق، وهو ثبوت جواز العفو لأهل الكبائر الغير المشركين ثبوتاً قطعياً جلياً، مثل الشمس على نصف النهار، فلا بد من العدول عن الظاهر في الوعيدات التي لغير الكفرة، فإما بالتقييد أو جعله لإنشاء التخويف، وأما الوعد فلا موجب فيه فيبقى على الحقيقة، وما قال : فليس بشيء، لأن التخويف لا يكون إلا على فعل قبيح موجب استحقاق الذم، فالتجاوز عن مستحق الذم والعقاب هو العفو، قيل في ترجيح الإضمار على التخويف بأن التخويف مخصوص بعصاة المؤمنين المغفورين ونص الوعيد شامل لغيرهم وليس في حقهم تخويف، يصح في كلام واحد أن يكون تخويفاً في حق البعض وخبراً في حق الآخرين، ولا يبعد أن يجاب بأنه هناك تخويف في حق الكل، إلا أنه ربما يؤاخذ، فإن مؤاخذة المخوف جائزة، وربما يعفو، نعم الآيات والأحاديث المخصوصة بأهل الشرك لا تحمل على إنشاء التخويف لعدم الموجب هناك، ولذا وقع في كلام الشيخ الأكبر خليفة الله في الأرضين قدس سره أن لا وعيد حقيقة إلا النصوص الواردة في حق المشركين
Objection: And you can counter him by saying that restriction is a deviation from reality without cause, and the same applies to promises also, so it would necessitate the permissibility of punishing the one promised Paradise without account, as a real occurrence. But the truth is that the cause for deviation is realized, which is the certainty of the permissibility of forgiveness for the major sinners who are not polytheists, a clear certainty like the sun at midday. So one must deviate from the apparent meaning in the threats that are for other than disbelievers, either by restriction or by making it for the creation of deterrence. As for promises, there is no cause for deviation, so they remain on their reality. And what he said is nothing, because deterrence only occurs for an ugly act that necessitates deserving blame. So overlooking the one deserving blame and punishment is forgiveness. It was said in preferring implication over deterrence that deterrence is specific to the sinners of the believing who are forgiven, while the text of threat includes others, and there is no deterrence regarding them. It is valid for one statement to be a deterrent for some and a report for others. And it is not unlikely that the answer is that there is deterrence for all, except that sometimes he may punish, for punishing the deterred is permissible, and sometimes he may forgive. Yes, the verses and hadiths specific to polytheists are not interpreted as creating deterrence due to lack of cause there. Hence, the greatest imam, the vicegerent of Allah on earth, may his secret be sanctified, stated that there is no threat in reality except the texts stated regarding polytheists.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis debate over how to interpret Quranic verses and hadiths that contain threats (وعيد) of punishment. The issue is whether these threats apply literally to all sinners, including believers, or must be reinterpreted. The author argues that for non-polytheist sinners, we must deviate from the apparent meaning because there is a certain and clear proof that forgiveness is permissible for them. Therefore, the threats are either restricted (e.g., only for those who die without repentance) or are meant as deterrence (إنشاء التخويف) rather than actual reports of punishment. For promises (وعد), no deviation is needed. The response to a counterargument that one statement cannot be both a deterrent and a report is that it can, depending on the audience. Finally, the text notes that verses concerning polytheists remain literal threats because there is no cause for deviation.

Key Terms
وعيد (wa'īd): A threat of punishment for a sin mentioned in the Quran or hadith.
وعد (wa'd): A promise of reward for obedience.
ظاهر (zāhir): The apparent or literal meaning of a text.
تخويف (takhwīf): Creating fear or deterrence, not necessarily a report of actual punishment.
قطعي جلي (qaṭ'ī jaliyy): Certain and clear proof that admits no doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When a certain proof contradicts the apparent meaning of a text, the Hanafi school requires reinterpretation to maintain consistency, as shown with Quranic threats for believers.

The Obligation in Fard Kifayah: On the Part or the Whole?
Analyzing verse 9:122 and rational argument to defend Hanafi fard kifayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on fard kifayah by analyzing verse 9:122 and the rational argument against obligation on an ambiguous part, and distinguish it from the opposing view.

Core Idea
In fard kifayah, obligation falls on the whole and drops when some perform, even if not obligated.

This passage tackles a fundamental usul question: when Allah commands some to act, does the obligation rest on those specific individuals or the entire community? The text begins with a rational argument that punishing an ambiguous group is unjust, then examines verse 9:122 to defend the Hanafi view that the obligation is on all, but drops upon sufficient performance.

Guided Path
1
Follow the rational argument against obligated punishment on an ambiguous part
The passage first argues that if punishment were for an ambiguous part, it would be unjust because each individual has the excuse that the obligation was on someone else.
This uses reasoning (istidlal bi al-ʻaql) to reject obligation on an unspecified group.
2
Identify the opposing proof from verse 9:122
Opponents cite the verse commanding some to go forth, claiming it shows obligation on a specific part.
The verse: 'If only a party from every group among them went forth...'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the verse
The Hanafis argue the verse is incitement (tahrid), not obligation; even if it were, it falls by the act of some.
The form 'lawla' in Arabic indicates encouragement, not direct command.
4
Examine the objection from funeral prayer by a child
Opponents counter that an obligation cannot fall by one not obligated, like a child for funeral prayer. The Hanafi response compares it to a volunteer paying a debt.
The analogy shows that the purpose (act's occurrence) overrides the performer's liability.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi principle: purpose is the act's occurrence
The key principle: if the intended act occurs, the obligation drops, whether performed by obligated or non-obligated persons.
This is based on the illa (effective cause) of fulfilling the objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتوجه الحساب بالذات إليه فتدبر وثانياً : يقول هذا العبد أن تأثيم المبهم باطل، لأن العقاب إما على بعض مبهم من حيث الإبهام أو على بعض معين أو على الكل، وبطلان الأول ضروري، وكذا الثاني إذ لا أولوية للبعض، وكذا الثالث، وهو ظلم، لأن لكل أحد منهم العذر بأنك أوجبت على البعض فعذب سواي ولا يصح أن يقال : إنما يعذب الكل لعدم الأولوية فيه، لأن هذا ليس أولى من عفو الكل بل عفوه أولى، لأن رحمته سبقت غضبه، ولو قيل : إن الوجوب على أفراد البعض والكل من أفراده فآل إلى ما قلنا من الوجوب على الجميع، هذا والعلم الأتم عند علام الغيوب (و) قالوا :(ثالثاً: قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ لِيَتَفَقَّهُوا فِي الدِّينِ وَلِيُنذِرُوا قَوْمَهُمْ إِذَا رَجَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ ﴾ [التوبة: ١٢٢] يعني: لم لا ينفر من كل فرقة طائفة واحد كان أو أكثر مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ليتفقه النافرون وينذروا قومهم بعد الرجوع أو لم لا ينفر طائفة في السرايا ولم ينفر كل أحد ولا يبقى آخرون مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ليتفقه الباقون وينذروا قومهم الذين مع السرايا إذا رجعوا، فقد أمر البعض بالخروج للتفقه أو الجهاد، وكل منهما واجب على الكفاية (قلنا) ليس في الآية الكريمة ما يدل على الوجوب على البعض، بل فيه تحريض لخروج البعض لتحصل لهم فائدة التفقه وعلى التنزل نقول : (مؤوّل بالسقوط بفعل البعض جمعاً بين (الأدلة هذا الدليل والدلائل الدالة على الوجوب على الكل من النصوص وغيرها (ثم) قال في التحرير يشكل) مسألة الوجوب على البعض بدليل السقوط بفعل البعض (بسقوط) صلاة الجنازة بفعل الصبي العاقل كما هو الأصح عند الشافعية لا وجوب عليه ولا يسقط الواجب إلا بأداء من وجب عليه أقول لا إشكال فإن ذلك) السقوط كسقوط الدين بأداء المتبرع مع أنه لا وجوب لأداء الدين عليه، والحاصل أنه ربما يكون المقصود من إيجاب شيء خروج الفعل المقصود منه في الوجود، فإن وجد بنفسه أو بأداء من وجوب عليه يسقط الوجوب، وهذا كما أنه متحقق في حقوق العباد فإن المقصود وصول الدين، مثلاً: فإن وجد الدائن مال المديون وأخذ بقدر دينه من غير إذنه، أو أدى المتبرع الدين سقط الوجوب عن ذمة المديون كذلك في حقوق الله تعالى التي يكون المقصود منها وقوع المصلحة دون إتعاب المكلف بالذات، بل الإتعاب لأجل وقوع الفعل فقط، فلو وقعت المصلحة بنفسها كما إذا أسلم الكفرة أو ماتوا أو اقتتلوا فيما بينهم وقتلوا جميعاً سقط الجهاد عن الذمة فما قيل إن هذا قياس الحقوق الإلهية عن الحقوق العبدية، وهو غير صحيح
The accounting is directed essentially to him, so contemplate. Secondly: This servant says that making the ambiguous sinful is invalid, because punishment is either for some ambiguous part by virtue of ambiguity, or for some specific part, or for the whole. The invalidity of the first is necessary, likewise the second since there is no priority for some, likewise the third, and it is injustice, because each of them has the excuse that you obligated on some and punished other than me. And it cannot be said that the whole is punished due to lack of priority, because this is not more preferable than pardoning all, rather His pardon is more preferable because His mercy precedes His wrath. And if it is said that the obligation is on the individuals of the part and the whole includes its individuals, then it comes back to what we said of the obligation on all. This and the most complete knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And) they said: (Thirdly: Allah said: 'If only a party from every group among them went forth...' [Tawbah 122] meaning: why does not a party from every group, one or more, go forth with the Messenger of Allah to gain understanding and then warn their people upon return, or why does not a party go forth in expeditions without everyone going and others remaining with the Messenger to gain understanding and then warn their people who were with the expeditions when they return. So He commanded some to go out for understanding or jihad, and both are obligatory on sufficiency. (We said) There is nothing in the noble verse indicating obligation on some, rather it is incitement for some to go out to gain the benefit of understanding, and on the assumption we say: it is interpreted as falling by the action of some, combining between this evidence and the evidences indicating obligation on all from texts and others. (Then) He said in al-Tahrir: The issue of obligation on some is problematic by the evidence of falling by the action of some, (by the falling of) the funeral prayer by the action of a sane minor as is the correct view among Shafi'is, there is no obligation on him, and an obligation is not fallen except by the performance of one upon whom it is obligatory. I say there is no problem because that falling is like the falling of a debt by the payment of a volunteer even though there is no obligation to pay the debt on him. The conclusion is that sometimes the purpose of obligating something is the occurrence of the intended act in existence, so if it occurs by itself or by the performance of one upon whom it is obligatory, the obligation falls. This is realized in the rights of servants because the purpose is the arrival of the debt, for example: if the creditor finds the debtor's property and takes his due without permission, or a volunteer pays the debt, the obligation falls from the debtor's liability. Similarly in the rights of Allah where the purpose is the occurrence of the benefit without directly burdening the responsible person, but the burdening is only for the sake of the act, so if the benefit occurs by itself as when the disbelievers embrace Islam or die or fight each other and are killed, jihad falls from liability. So what is said that this is analogy of divine rights to human rights is incorrect.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about whether an obligation is on a part of the community or the whole, specifically regarding fard kifayah (collective obligation). The opponent argues that punishing an ambiguous group is invalid, and uses verse 9:122 to claim that the obligation to go out for jihad or learning is on some, not all. The Hanafi response (the commentator) counters that the verse does not prove obligation on a specific part; rather it encourages some to go, and even if it did, the obligation falls when some perform it, similar to how a debt can be paid by a volunteer. The commentator argues that the purpose of the obligation is the act itself, so if the act occurs by others, the obligation drops. He compares to funeral prayer where a sane child's participation can drop the obligation even though the child is not obligated. The passage maintains that the obligation is on the whole, but it falls by the action of a part.

Key Terms
الوجوب على الكفاية (al-wujūb ʿalā al-kifāyah): An obligation that must be fulfilled by the community collectively; if some perform it, the obligation falls from all.
تأثيم المبهم (taʾthīm al-mubham): Declaring an ambiguous (unspecified) person sinful; a logical problem discussed in the passage.
الوجوب على الكل (al-wujūb ʿalā al-kull): Obligation on the entire community (the Hanafi position).
الوجوب على البعض (al-wujūb ʿalā al-baʿḍ): Obligation on a specific part of the community (the opposing view).
سقوط الوجوب (suqūṭ al-wujūb): The falling away of the obligation once it has been fulfilled by sufficient individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in the passage, why does the obligation of jihad (as a fard kifayah) fall even if performed by a non-obligated person?
A) Because the non-obligated person becomes obligated by performing the act.
B) Because the purpose is the occurrence of the beneficial act, not the identity of the performer.
C) Because the verse explicitly states that any performance suffices.
D) Because the punishment for non-performance is lifted only if all individuals perform.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines fard kifayah as an obligation on the whole community that falls upon sufficient performance, even by non-obligated persons, because the objective is the act's occurrence, not individual duty.

The Five Meanings of Ja'iz in Hanafi Usul
Defines the term ja'iz with five meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five meanings of ja'iz and explain how ja'iz is broader than mubah.

Core Idea
Ja'iz is not just mubah; it includes what is not prevented by Law, not impossible by reason, equal in aspects, or doubtful.

The term 'ja'iz' appears frequently in usul and fiqh texts, but its precise meaning shifts depending on context. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut unpacks five distinct usages, clarifying that ja'iz covers a wider range than the simple 'permissible' (mubah). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly interpreting legal arguments, especially when later scholars discuss abrogation or suspension of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five usages
List the five meanings of ja'iz as given in the passage: (1) what the Lawgiver deems without prevention (includes mubah, wajib, mandub), (2) what the Law has not ruled on (tawaqquf), (3) what is not rationally impossible, (4) what is equal in both aspects (legal or rational), (5) the doubtful.
The passage explicitly enumerates these meanings.
2
Distinguish each meaning
For each meaning, note its scope and relationship to other categories. For example, the first meaning is broader than mubah because it includes wajib and mandub. The fourth meaning (equality) is also broader than mubah because equality can be rational or legal, while mubah implies only legal equality.
The text says: 'وهو أعم من المباح' (it is more general than mubah).
3
Connect to Shafi'i view on abrogation
The passage mentions that the Shafi'is claim that after abrogation of obligation, the ruling remains ja'iz in the first sense (non-prevention). The Hanafis, however, adopt tawaqquf (suspension) until another proof for permissibility arises. This is a key practical difference.
This links to the dispute on the effect of abrogation.
4
Apply the definition to a new example
Consider an act that is neither commanded nor prohibited but the Law is silent. Under which meaning of ja'iz would it fall? The second meaning (tawaqquf) applies because no ruling has been given.
This illustrates the tawaqquf meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الجائز كما يطلق على المباح المباين للواجب والمندوب كذلك (يطلق على ما لا يمتنع شرعاً) هذه العبارة تحتمل محملين : الأول : ما حكم الشارع بعدم امتناعه والحرج فيه فهذا يشمل المباح والواجب والمندوب، وهو الذي يدعي الشافعية بقاءه بعد انتساخ الوجوب . الثاني : أن الشرع لم يحكم فيه بالامتناع، فهذا هو التوقف الذي نقول به بعد انتساخ الوجوب إلى قيام دليل آخر على الجواز، واللاجواز (و) يطلق على ما ليس بممتنع عقلاً) بأحد الوجهين (و) يطلق على ما استوى الأمران فيه شرعاً أو عقلاً) أي قام دليل شرعي أو عقلي على الاستواء، وهو أعم من المباح فإن فيه الاستواء الشرعي فقط، وعلى هذا فالاستواء عدم الحرج في الفعل (و) يطلق على المشكوك فيه (كذلك) عقلاً أو شرعاً كسور الحمار . مسألة (يجوز في الواحد بالجنس اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة بأن يكون نوع منه واجباً ونوع آخر حراماً (كالسجود الله و السجود (للشمس) فإنهما نوعان لمطلق السجود الواحد الجنسي، مع وجوب الأول وحرمة الثاني ومنع بعض المعتزلة هذا الاجتماع (مكابرة) لا يلتفت إليه (وصرفهم) السجود إلى قصد التعظيم بأن السجود ليس حراماً ولا واجباً، إنما الواجب تعظيم الله تعالى والمحرم تعظيم الشمس (لا يجدي) في هذا المقام فإن التعظيم واحد جنسي، وأحد نوعيه هو تعظيم الله تعالى واجب والآخر هو تعظيم الشمس حرام (إنما الكلام في الواحد بالنوع) هل يجتمع فيه الوجوب والحرمة بأن يكون شخص منه واجباً وحراماً، فهذا وما هو المشهور من الكلام في الواحد بالشخص متحدان في المآل لكنه إنما عبر بهذه العبارة لأن التكليف بالنوع والشخص إنما يوجد بعد الإتيان، ولا يتصف بالوجوب والحرمة إلا لأنه فرد من النوع ، وهذا هو مراده مما قال في الحاشية»، وهذا أولى من المشهور، لأنه لا تكليف إلا بالنوع تحقيقاً، لأن التشخيص بعد الوجود، ولأن النوع إنما يتصف بالوجوب والحرمة باعتبارين بخلاف الواحد بالجنس انتهى ولم يرد به أن ما ذكره القوم باطل، بل أن هذا
Know that 'ja'iz' as it is used for the permissible that is distinct from the obligatory and the recommended, likewise (it is used for what is not prevented by the Law) - this expression admits of two interpretations: First: what the Lawgiver has ruled as without prevention and hardship [in it], then this includes the permissible, the obligatory, and the recommended, and this is what the Shafi'is claim remains after abrogation of obligation. Second: that the Law has not ruled regarding its prevention, then this is the suspension (tawaqquf) that we hold after abrogation of obligation until another proof for permissibility arises, and non-permissibility. And (it is used for what is not impossible by reason) in one of two ways, and (it is used for what is equal in both aspects by Law or by reason) i.e., a legal or rational proof has established equality, and it is more general than the permissible (mubah) for it contains only the legal equality, and on this meaning equality is the absence of restriction in the act, and (it is used for the doubtful) likewise by reason or by Law, like [the case of] the wall of the donkey. Issue: (It is permissible in the one by genus for obligation and prohibition to combine such that one type of it is obligatory and another type is forbidden), like prostration to Allah and prostration to the sun, for they are two types of absolute prostration which is one by genus, with obligation of the first and prohibition of the second. And some Mu'tazila prohibited this combination (in obstinacy) not to be regarded, (and their turning) prostration to the intention of veneration, such that prostration is neither forbidden nor obligatory; rather what is obligatory is veneration of Allah and what is forbidden is veneration of the sun (does not avail) in this context, for veneration is one by genus, and one of its types is veneration of Allah which is obligatory and the other is veneration of the sun which is forbidden. (Rather the discussion concerns the one by type): can obligation and prohibition combine in it such that a person of it is both obligatory and forbidden? This and what is famous of the discussion on the one by person are the same in outcome, but he expressed it this way because assignment (taklif) by type and person only occurs after performance, and it is not characterized by obligation or prohibition except because it is an individual of the type, and this is his meaning in what he said in the Hashiyah. And this is more appropriate than the famous [view], because there is no assignment except by type realistically, since individuation is after existence, and because the type is only characterized by obligation and prohibition from two aspects, unlike the one by genus. Ended. He did not intend that what the people mentioned is invalid, but rather that this
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the definition of ja'iz and the combination of wajib and haram in one by genus. The commentator explains the multiple meanings of ja'iz: 1) what the Lawgiver deems not prevented (includes mubah, wajib, mandub), 2) what the Law has not ruled on (tawaqquf), 3) what is not rationally impossible, 4) what is equal in both aspects (legal or rational), and 5) the doubtful. The key point is that ja'iz is broader than mubah. Then the main issue: in one thing by genus (like the act of sujud) it is permissible for obligation and prohibition to combine: sujud to Allah is wajib, sujud to the sun is haram. They are two types of the same genus (sujud). The Mu'tazila's objection that veneration is the real object is refuted because veneration is also one genus. The commentator then transitions to the discussion of 'one by type' (al-wahid bi'l-naw'), which is more specific.

Key Terms
جائز (ja'iz): A term with multiple meanings: (1) what the Lawgiver deems without prevention, (2) what is not prevented by the Law (tawaqquf), (3) what is not rationally impossible, (4) what has equal legal or rational aspects, (5) the doubtful.
التوقف (tawaqquf): Suspension of ruling until evidence emerges; one of the meanings of ja'iz in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed as a meaning of ja'iz?
A) What is not prevented by the Law
B) What is not impossible by reason
C) What is recommended (mandub)
D) What is equal in both aspects

Key Takeaway
Ja'iz is a polysemous term in Hanafi usul, with at least five distinct technical meanings that must be distinguished from mere mubah.

Combining Wajib and Haram in One by Genus
Establishes permissibility of combining wajib and haram in one by genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that wajib and haram can combine in a single genus and distinguish it from the one by type or person.

Core Idea
An act can be wajib in one species and haram in another while sharing the same genus.

Can the same genus of action contain both an obligatory species and a forbidden species? The Hanafi answer is yes, as illustrated by sujud: sujud to Allah is wajib, sujud to the sun is haram, yet both are 'sujud'. This lesson unpacks the logic behind this ruling, refutes the Mu'tazila objection, and clarifies the distinction between 'one by genus', 'one by type', and 'one by person'.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'one by genus' (al-wahid bi'l-jins)
A single genus that includes multiple species or types. The example is 'sujud' as a genus with two species: sujud to Allah and sujud to the sun.
The genus is the common action; the species differ in intention or object.
2
Note the combination of rulings
The passage states that it is permissible (ja'iz) for obligation and prohibition to combine in such a genus: one species is wajib, the other haram. This is not a contradiction because the rulings attach to the species, not the genus as such.
The rulings are on the specific types, not on the abstract genus.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila objection
Some Mu'tazila claimed that what is actually wajib or haram is the veneration (ta'zim), not the prostration itself. The passage refutes this by noting that veneration is also one by genus: veneration of Allah is wajib, veneration of the sun is haram.
The objection fails because it shifts the genus but does not eliminate the problem.
4
Distinguish from 'one by type' (al-wahid bi'l-naw')
The passage then discusses whether obligation and prohibition can combine in a single individual (person) of one type. It says that the famous discussion on 'one by person' effectively merges with 'one by type' because taklif applies to the individual only after performance.
This is a more subtle point: an individual act cannot be both wajib and haram simultaneously, but a type can have individuals with different rulings.
5
Apply the principle
Given a new genus (e.g., 'standing'), we can have one species that is wajib (standing in prayer) and another that is haram (standing in respect for a tyrant). The principle allows this.
The illa is that the rulings attach to the species based on intention and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الواحد بالجنس (al-wahid bi'l-jins): A single genus that includes multiple species or types; in this context, the act of sujud as a genus with two species: sujud to Allah (wajib) and sujud to the sun (haram).
الواحد بالنوع (al-wahid bi'l-naw'): One by type; a single species that can be instantiated in multiple individuals. The passage discusses whether two contradictory rulings can apply to the same type.
الواحد بالشخص (al-wahid bi'l-shakhs): One by person; a single individual act. The passage notes that the discussion on one by type and one by person converge in outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Mu'tazila objection (shifting from sujud to veneration) not resolve the issue?
A) Because veneration is not a relevant concept
B) Because veneration itself is one by genus with wajib and haram types
C) Because the Mu'tazila are always wrong
D) Because the passage does not address that objection

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, obligation and prohibition can coexist in a single genus as long as they attach to different species within that genus.

Is the Recommended Commanded? The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Debates whether mandub is truly commanded or metaphorically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether mandub is commanded, explain the Hanafi proofs, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, amr is a reality only for obligation; mandub is not truly commanded.

This passage addresses a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: is the recommended act (mandub) truly commanded? The Hanafis argue that command (amr) is a reality only for obligation, so the recommended is not commanded except metaphorically. The Shafi'is, led by al-Qadi al-Baqillani, hold that the recommended is truly commanded. The debate hinges on linguistic analysis and the implications of calling something 'commanded'.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi claim: mandub is not commanded in reality; it is only metaphorically called commanded.
2
State the Shafi'i position
Identify the opposing view: the recommended is truly commanded, as per al-Baqillani and the majority of Shafi'is.
The Shafi'is rely on linguistic evidence and the concept of obedience.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic proof
Understand the Hanafi argument: the word 'amr' (command) is a reality for the specific statement 'do', which is a reality only for obligation. Therefore, any usage of 'amr' for recommendation is metaphorical.
The text provides a nuance about the word 'fatiha' being a reality despite containing metaphors.
4
Analyze the Hanafi consequence proof
Consider the reductio: if mandub were commanded, leaving it would be a sin (because violating a command is sinful). But there is consensus that leaving a mandub is not sinful. Hence, mandub is not commanded.
The hadith of siwak is also used to show the Prophet distinguished commanding from recommending.
5
Evaluate the Shafi'i proofs and Hanafi response
Examine the Shafi'i arguments: (1) mandub is obedience, and obedience is doing what is commanded; (2) linguists divide command into obligation and recommendation, implying a shared meaning. The Hanafi response: obedience also applies to recommended acts, and the linguistic division is a broadening of reality, not a proof of reality.
The Hanafis note that if the division were real, then threatened and permitted acts would also be commanded, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (المندوب هل هو مأمور به؟ فعند الحنفية (لا يكون مأموراً به إلا مجازاً، وقيل) في شرح المختصر عن المحققين : نعم إنه مأمور به (حقيقة) وهو قول القاضي الباقلاني وجمهور الشافعية لنا أن الأمر حقيقة في القول المخصوص هو أفعل (وذلك القول حقيقة في الإيجاب فقط) فالأمر حقيقة فيه، قال واقف الأسرار الإلهية قدس سره، إن كون اللفظ حقيقة لفظ لا يلزم أن يكون حقيقة فيما هو حقيقة فيه، بل هو حقيقة مطلقاً، سواء كان هذا اللفظ حقيقة أو مجازاً، ألا ترى الفاتحة حقيقة في القول المخصوص وإن كان فيها ألفاظ مجازية، والحق أن يقال : الواو في قوله : وذلك القول للحال من القول المخصوص والحاصل أن الأمر حقيقة في القول المخصوص حال كونه للوجوب، فالمندوب ليس مأموراً به لعدم الحتم هناك لكن ينبو عن هذا التوجيه بعض عبارات الكتب الأخر، نعم إنه دليل مستقل (وأيضاً: لو كان) المندوب مأموراً به لكان تركه معصية لأنها مخالفة الأمر أو النهي واللازم باطل فإنه لا حرج على تارك المندوب إليه (و) أيضاً لو كان المندوب مأموراً به لما صح) قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم : «لو لم أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل وضوء») رواه النسائي (لأنه) يفهم أنه ما أمرهم وندبهم إليه والقول بالتجوز خلاف الأصل لا يصار إليه، وأمثال هذه العبارات شائعة الشافعية قالوا : أوّلاً إنه أي المندوب (طاعة إجماعاً، والطاعة فعل المأمور به قلنا لا نسلم أنه فعل المأمور به فقط (بل) هو (و) فعل (المندوب إليه) أيضاً (و) قالوا (ثانياً: أرباب اللغة قسموا الأمر (إلى أمر إيجاب وأمر ندب ومورد القسمة مشترك) فالأمر مشترك بين أمر الندب وأمر الإيجاب قلنا : هم قسموا أيضاً إلى أمر تهديد وأمر إباحة إلى غير ذلك فيلزم أن يكون المهدد عليه والمباح مأمورين ، ولم يذهب إليه ذاهب، هذا نقض، ثم أشار إلى الحل بقوله : (فهم توسعوا عن حقيقة الأمر وقسموه أخذاً بالمعنى المجازي فتدبر. وأيضاً ما قسموا مدلول الأمر، إنما قسموا صيغة الأمر إليهما، فلا يلزم أن يكون أمر الندب أمراً حقيقة، فإن قلت : فيلزم أنه صيغة أمر، قلت: لا بأس به، فإنه صيغة أمر في اصطلاحهم، كذا في «الحاشية».
Issue: Is the recommended (mandub) commanded? According to the Hanafis, it is not commanded except metaphorically. And it is said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar from the verifiers: Yes, it is commanded in reality, which is the statement of al-Qadi al-Baqillani and the majority of Shafi'is. Our proof: The imperative (amr) is a reality for the specific statement 'do' and that statement is a reality for obligation only. So the imperative is a reality for it. Waqif al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (may his secret be sanctified) said: The fact that a word is a reality in a particular usage does not require it to be a reality in what is a reality in that usage; rather it is a reality absolutely, whether this word is a reality or a metaphor. Do you not see that al-Fatiha is a reality for the specific statement even though it contains metaphorical words? The truth is to say: The 'waw' in his statement 'that statement' is a circumstantial qualifier for the specific statement. The conclusion is that the imperative is a reality for the specific statement when it is for obligation, so the recommended is not commanded because there is no obligation there. However, some expressions in other books deviate from this interpretation. Yes, it is an independent proof. Also: If the recommended were commanded, then leaving it would be a sin because it would be violating a command or prohibition, and the consequent is false because there is no blame on the one who leaves the recommended. Also, if the recommended were commanded, the saying of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family) 'Were it not for the hardship on my community, I would have commanded them to use the siwak at every ablution' (narrated by al-Nasa'i) would not be valid because it is understood that he did not command them but recommended it. And resorting to metaphor is contrary to the principle and should not be adopted. Such expressions are common. The Shafi'is said: First, the recommended is obedience by consensus, and obedience is doing what is commanded. We say: We do not accept that it is only doing what is commanded, but rather it is also doing the recommended. Second, the linguists divided the command into a command of obligation and a command of recommendation, and the locus of division is shared, so the command is shared between command of recommendation and command of obligation. We say: They also divided it into a command of threat and a command of permission, etc., so it would follow that the threatened and the permitted are commanded, but no one holds that. This is a refutation. Then he pointed to the solution by saying: They broadened the reality of the command and divided it by taking the metaphorical meaning, so reflect. Also, they did not divide the meaning of the command; rather they divided the form of the command into the two, so it does not follow that the command of recommendation is a command in reality. If you say: Then it follows that it is a command form. I say: No problem, for it is a command form in their terminology. Thus in al-Hashiya.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over whether the recommended (mandub) is truly commanded. The Hanafi position is that the command (amr) is a reality only for obligation; the recommended is not commanded except metaphorically. The Shafi'is, led by al-Baqillani, argue that command is shared and the recommended is truly commanded. The Hanafi proofs include: (1) The command has a specific meaning that is genuine only for obligation. (2) If the recommended were commanded, leaving it would be a sin, but there is no sin. (3) The hadith about the siwak shows a distinction between commanding and recommending; the Prophet did not command but recommended. The Shafi'i arguments (division of command into obligation and recommendation, and that the recommended is obedience) are refuted by the Hanafis: the linguistic division is based on broadening the reality, not on actual meaning, and obedience can apply to both commanded and recommended acts. The Hanafis conclude that the recommended is not commanded in reality but only in a metaphorical sense. The text also mentions a nuance about the word 'fatiha' being a reality for a specific statement despite containing metaphors, indicating that calling something a reality does not mean all its parts are realities.

Key Terms
المندوب (al-mandūb): An act that is recommended but not obligatory.
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form or command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis in this passage, what would be the consequence if the recommended were truly commanded?
A) It would become obligatory.
B) Leaving it would be a sin.
C) It would be permissible to leave.
D) It would be equal to the forbidden.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the command (amr) is a reality only for obligation; the recommended is not truly commanded but is called commanded metaphorically.

Supererogatory Acts Become Obligatory by Commencement
Proof that a supererogatory act becomes wajib by commencement, with rebuttals to Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Quranic verse 47:33 and the hadith of Aisha to argue that a supererogatory act becomes obligatory once commenced, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
A supererogatory worship, once started, becomes wajib to complete; spoiling it requires qada.

In Hanafi usul, the principle that a nafl act becomes obligatory upon commencement is a key point of dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. This lesson examines the textual and analogical proofs for this rule, focusing on the Quranic prohibition of invalidating deeds and the hadith evidence. By understanding this debate, students will grasp how Hanafi usul derives obligations from general texts and resolves conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and dispute
Clearly articulate the Hanafi rule that a nafl act becomes wajib by shuru', and identify that al-Shafi'i disagrees.
The dispute applies to all nafl acts, not just fasting or prayer.
2
Analyze the Quranic dalil
Examine verse 47:33, 'Do not invalidate your deeds.' Classify the text (amm) and apply the Hanafi principle that a general command indicates obligation.
The verse is the primary textual proof.
3
Evaluate the hadith evidence
Compare the hadith of Aisha (ordering qada for a broken voluntary fast) with the hadith of Umm Hani (permitting breaking). Understand how Hanafi scholars reconcile them.
The Umm Hani hadith is interpreted as no sin, not no qada.
4
Understand objections and responses
Consider objections that the verse refers to riya' and that spoiling does not invalidate reward. Study the Hanafi responses that the verse is general and that a part has no reward as a part.
These objections arise from a different usul methodology.
5
Apply qiyas to reinforce the rule
Examine analogical arguments from vows and Hajj. The logic: if a vow (obligation by speech) requires fulfillment, then a started naft (obligation by action) should similarly require completion.
Qiyas strengthens the textual proof.

Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage discusses the well-known Hanafi principle that a supererogatory (nafl) worship becomes obligatory once one has commenced it. This is a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi proof is based on the Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33), which implies that once an act of worship is started, it must be completed; otherwise, it is invalidated. The passage also addresses objections: the verse may refer to invalidation by riya' (show) not by spoiling; but the answer is that the verse is general. Another objection is that spoiling does not necessarily invalidate the reward; but the Hanafi response is that the part has no reward as a part. Two difficult issues are raised: (1) The hadith in Sahih Muslim about the Prophet breaking his voluntary fast; this is countered by various interpretations (e.g., it was a concession, or abrogation, or the obligation is like the obligation of prayer on one who deserves it in the end). (2) The issue that a part of a fast is not a fast, so breaking it does not invalidate the action; but this is refuted by the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet ordered qada' for a voluntary fast. The passage also brings qiyas on vows and on Hajj. It concludes with a strong case for the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory worship, which is recommended but not obligatory initially.
الشروع (al-shuru'): The commencement or starting of an act of worship.
لا تبطلوا أعمالكم (la tubtilu a'malakum): Do not invalidate your deeds (Quran 47:33).
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed or spoiled act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Quranic evidence for the Hanafi rule that a Nafl act must be completed once started?
A) 'Perform the prayer from midday.' (17:78)
B) 'Do not invalidate your deeds.' (47:33)
C) 'And whatever good you do, Allah knows it.' (2:197)
D) 'And establish prayer...' (2:43)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a supererogatory act becomes wajib upon commencement; the Quranic prohibition of invalidating deeds and the hadith of Aisha requiring qada establish this, despite Shafi'i objections.
