بين اللفظ ومعناه وإلا فجعل بعضها لمعنى وبعضها الآخر ترجيح من غير مرجح ، وشأن الحكيم يأبى عنه، وهذا ظاهر جداً عند كون الواضع هو الله تعالى (حتى) بین اللغات وبين (الأمزجة التي اكتسب هيولى كل قوم أراد بها ما فيه صلوح كمال لا ما يقول به الفلاسفة (من) عوارضها السماوية والأرضية) أي العارضة من خارج فاختصت لغة كل المناسبة
Then the one who assigns: there is disagreement about him. Al-Ash'ari said: knowledge of assignment is by divine tawqif, so He is the assigner. For His saying, 'And He taught Adam all the names' (Quran 2:31). So teaching the names to Adam is from God, so he (Adam) is not the assigner, nor the angels, because they were incapable of explanation. And the possibility that the Jinn were assigners is more remote. So the assigner is God Almighty. If it is said: what is meant is the named things, and the meaning is that God taught Adam all the named things, rather that is apparent, because perfection is knowledge of realities, not words and their assignments. He said: The named things are not meant, as shown by His saying, 'Inform me of the names of these' (Quran 2:31). For the named things are the things pointed to, and thus there would occur the addition of a thing to itself. He interpreted (the word 'names') as 'names of the realities', so the addition of a thing to itself does not occur. Then what is pointed to are the realities, and the added (word) is 'names'. If you say: This is interpretation, so it should not be resorted to without necessity, and it necessitates another interpretation in His saying, 'Then He presented them to the angels' (Quran 2:31). I say: Interpretation is necessary for him by interpreting His saying 'then He presented them' as a predominant use (taghlib). And the necessity in this interpretation is that there is no perfection worth considering for prophets and saints in knowing words. Also, there is another interpretation of the verse held by the verifiers: That the angels spoke about Adam by shedding blood, and they claimed merit for themselves by glorification. So God wanted to show His favor over them and the merit of His glorification over their glorification. So He taught him the divine names, universal and particular. Every existent being glorifies (God) by another existent being. For each existent being glorifies its Lord by what it knows of His name and attribute, so that Adam may glorify God and supplicate Him by every name, and be perfect in divine knowledge. Then He presented them, i.e., all existent beings, to the angels and said, 'Inform me of the names of these' i.e., the names by which all these glorify, 'if you are truthful' (Quran 2:31) that you are fit for vicegerency and that your knowledge and glorification are better than Adam's glorification. And He expressed the existent beings with the form of rational beings (plural of intelligence) because they are, by virtue of being glorifiers, rational. They said, 'Glory be to You' and acknowledged their inability to grasp the secret of matters, 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us. Indeed You are the Knowing, the Wise' (Quran 2:32). Create whom You will and make him caliph. On this, the verse is not relevant to the topic. Al-Ash'ari said: (It is) by tawqif, because of His saying, 'And among His signs is the creation of the heavens and the earth and the diversity of your tongues and your colors' (Quran 30:22). And what is meant is not the diversity of the tongue organ, for there is no significant diversity in it to be made a sign. Rather what is meant is the diversity of languages, and its being a sign is inconceivable unless the assigner is God, as is not hidden. If you say: It is possible for it to be a sign by virtue of enabling (al-iqdar) upon these different languages, he said: Enabling (al-iqdar) is a departure from the apparent, so it is not considered. There is consideration: it is possible that what is meant by 'tongue' is the organ, and by 'diversity' is the diversity in the ability for different expressions. And the meaning, God knows His intended meaning of the verses, is the diversity of your tongues in expressing what is in the heart, some able to express in a language and others in another. This is not a great deviation from the apparent, and there is no harm in it, as you have said metaphorically in your tongues. And al-Bahshamiyya said: assignment is by convention (istilah) from people, because of His saying, 'And We did not send any messenger except in the language of his people' (Quran 14:4). If the assigner were God, then knowledge of language would be by tawqif from the messengers, so language would not exist before the messenger, while the verse might indicate otherwise. It was answered: We do not accept that if the assigner were God, knowledge of it would be by tawqif from the messengers. How, when He taught it to Adam first before sending? Then He made each people specific with their language, so He sent the messenger of that people in their language. And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini said: by distribution (tawzi'), meaning that what is needed for expressing what is in the heart is by tawqif, and what exceeds that is possible, or by convention, according to different reports. And a group said: (We must) pause, because no proof has been established on either side of negation or affirmation. The truth is what is conveyed: if certain speech is intended, then the truth is to pause; otherwise, the apparent is what al-Ash'ari said, may God sanctify his secret. Then there was disagreement on whether the assignment of a word to its meaning involves an appropriateness between them or not. The truth is the consideration of appropriateness between the word and its meaning, otherwise making some (words) for a meaning and others for another meaning would be a preference without a preponderant factor, and the way of the Wise (God) is averse to this. And this is very apparent when the assigner is God Almighty. Even between languages and the temperaments that the matter of each people has acquired, He intended by them what has the capacity for perfection, not what the philosophers say about their celestial and terrestrial accidents, i.e., those occurring from outside, so each language became specific to the appropriateness.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage addresses the classical dispute on the origin of language (wad' al-lugha). The Ash'ari school holds that language is divinely revealed (tawqif) — God is the assigner. They prove this from Quran 2:31, where God taught Adam all names. The author refutes the objection that 'names' means the named things, by arguing that would lead to adding a thing to itself in 'Inform me of the names of these', so it must be the names themselves. A deeper interpretation is also given: God taught Adam the divine names for glorification, to show Adam's superiority over the angels. Al-Ash'ari also uses Quran 30:22, where the diversity of tongues is a sign of God — if languages came from human convention, they wouldn't be a divine sign. The Bahshamiyya (Mu'tazila) argue that language is by human convention (istilah), citing Quran 14:4, that each messenger speaks his people's language, implying language exists before the messenger. The author responds that God taught language to Adam first. Al-Isfara'ini proposes a middle view: some language is from tawqif, some from convention. Finally, the author states that the correct view is the one attributed to al-Ash'ari, but if certainty is required, one should pause (tawaqquf). The passage also discusses the appropriateness (munasaba) between words and meanings: the truth is that there is a natural connection, otherwise arbitrary assignment would be senseless — especially since God is the assigner.

Key Terms
التوقيف (al-tawqīf): The view that language is established by divine revelation or inspiration, making God the wadi'.
الاصطلاح (al-iṣṭilāḥ): The view that language is established by human convention and agreement.
الواضع (al-wāḍi‘): The entity that establishes the assignment of words to meanings. In tawqif, it is God; in istilah, it is humans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof used by Al-Ash'ari for the divine origin of language?
A) Quran 14:4 about messengers speaking in their people's language
B) Quran 2:31 where God taught Adam all the names
C) The existence of multiple languages without a universal convention
D) The inability of humans to invent language on their own

Key Takeaway
The dispute on wadi' al-lugha has significant implications for usul al-fiqh, as it determines whether linguistic meanings are divinely fixed or subject to human change.

Dispute on the Origin of Language: Tawqif, Istilah, or Tawzi'?
Presents the Ash'ari, Bahshamiyya, and Isfarayini positions on the origin of language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the origin of language (tawqif, istilah, tawzi'), evaluate the Hanafi inclination towards tawqif, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to the use of qiyas in linguistic derivation.

Core Idea
The origin of language is either divine (tawqif) or conventional (istilah); Hanafi usul leans towards tawqif, with tawaqquf as the safest position.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut presents a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: where does language come from? Three positions emerge: tawqif (divine institution), istilah (human convention), and tawzi' (mixed). The Hanafi school often adopts tawaqquf (suspension) but tends towards tawqif. Understanding this dispute is crucial because it affects whether qiyas can be applied to language, a key issue in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List tawqif (al-Ash'ari), istilah (Bahshamiyya), and tawzi' (al-Isfarayini). Also note the tawaqquf (suspension) option mentioned.
2
Examine the Quranic proof for tawqif
Analyze how al-Ash'ari uses the verse 'the difference of your languages' (Quran 30:22) to argue that language is a sign of Allah's creation, implying divine origin.
The commentator refutes the interpretation that the verse refers only to the capacity to speak.
3
Analyze the Bahshamiyya counter-argument
Understand their reliance on the verse 'We sent not a messenger except in the language of his people' (Quran 14:4) and their claim that language must be conventional because it existed before the messenger.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Recognize the practical consequence: if language is tawqif, words are fixed, limiting qiyas; if istilah, qiyas may be permissible in linguistic matters.
This impacts how usul scholars approach istinbat.
5
Determine the Hanafi favored position
Note that tawaqquf is presented as the cautious position, but the apparent view (al-Ash'ari) is tawqif, and the author aligns with it.
The Hanafi school often follows this leaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الأشعري بالتوقيف (لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْ ءَايَيْهِ خَلْقُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [الروم: ۲۲] واختلاف ألسنتكم وليس المراد اختلاف عضو اللسان، فإنه لا اختلاف فيه يعتد به ليجعل آية بل المراد اختلاف اللغات وكونها آية لا يتصور إلا أن يكون الواضع هو الله سبحانه كما لا يخفى فإن قلت يجوز كونها آية باعتبار الإقدار على هذه اللغات المختلفة، قال : والإقدار (رجوع) أي القول بإرادة الإقدار رجوع (عن الظاهر) فلا يلتفت إليه، وفيه نظر، فإنه يجوز أن يكون المراد باللسان العضو وبالاختلاف الاختلاف في القدرة على التعبيرات المختلفة والمعنى والله أعلم بمراده من الآيات اختلاف ألسنتكم في إفادة ما في الضمير يقدر بعضها على التعبير بلغة وآخر على التعبير بأخرى، وليس هذا كثير عدول عن الظاهر ولا بأس به أيضاً، كما أنكم قلتم بالتجوز في ألسنتكم هذا: (وقالت البهشمية) الوضع (بالاصطلاح) من الناس ( لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِن رَّسُولٍ إِلَّا بِلِسَانِ قوْمِهِ [إبراهيم: (٤) ولو كان الواضع هو الله تعالى لكان علمها بالتوقيف من الرسل، فلا تكون اللغة قبل الرسول وقد تشهد الآية خلافه (وأجيب) بأنا لا نسلم أنه لو كان الواضع هو الله تعالى لكان علمها بالتوقيف من الرسل، كيف وأنه تعالى علمها آدم أولاً قبل الإرسال (ثم اختص كل قوم بلغته فأرسل رسول ذلك القوم بلسانهم وقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراييني (بالتوزيع) بأن ما يحتاج إليه للتعبير عما في الضمير بالتوقيف وما زاد عليه محتمل، أو بالاصطلاح على اختلاف النقل عنه (و) قال (جماعة بالتوقيف فإنه لم يقم دليل على شيء من طرفي النفي والإثبات والحق ما أفيد أنه إن أريد جزم القول فالحق التوقف، وإلا فالظاهر ما قال الأشعري قدس سره
And al-Ash'ari said: it is by divine institution (tawqif), because of His statement: 'And among His signs is the creation of the heavens and the earth and the difference of your languages.' And what is meant is not the difference of the tongue organ, for there is no significant difference in it to make it a sign; rather, what is meant is the difference of languages. And it being a sign cannot be imagined unless the Originator is Allah, as is clear. If you say: it can be a sign by virtue of enabling people to speak these different languages. He said: 'enabling' is a departure from the apparent meaning, so it is not heeded. And there is a consideration in this, for it is possible that what is meant by 'tongue' is the organ, and by 'difference' the difference in ability to express variously; the meaning—Allah knows best His intent from the signs—is the difference of your tongues in conveying what is in the inner self, with some able to express in one language and others in another. This is not a great departure from the apparent meaning, nor is it objectionable, just as you have used metaphor for 'your tongues'. This is the statement. (And the Bahshamiyya said) that the assignment of words (wad') is by convention (istilah) from people, because of His statement: 'And We sent not a messenger except in the language of his people.' If the assigner were Allah, then knowledge of language would be by divine institution from messengers, so language would not exist before the messenger, and the verse indicates otherwise. (And it was answered) that we do not concede that if the assigner were Allah, knowledge of it would be only by divine institution from messengers; indeed, He taught them to Adam first before sending him. Then each people were distinguished by their language, so He sent the messenger of that people in their language. (And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said) that it is a distribution (tawzi'): what is needed for expression of inner meanings is by divine institution, and what is additional is possible, or by convention, according to differing reports from him. (And a group said) it is by divine institution, because no proof has been established on either side of negation or affirmation. And the correct view, as conveyed, is that if categorical statement is intended, the correct position is suspension (tawaqquf); otherwise, the apparent position is what al-Ash'ari said—may Allah sanctify his secret.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a classical usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the origin of language (wad' al-lugha). It presents three main positions: (1) al-Ash'ari argues for tawqif (divine institution) using the Quranic verse 'the difference of your languages' as a sign of Allah's creation. He refutes the alternative interpretation that the verse refers to the capacity to speak, as that would be a departure from the apparent meaning. (2) The Bahshamiyya (Mu'tazila followers of Abu Hashim) hold that language is conventional (istilah) by human agreement, citing the verse that messengers are sent in the language of their people. They argue that if language were divinely instituted, it would have to be taught by revelation, making it impossible before revelation. The response is that Allah taught language to Adam first, and then each people had their own language, so the messenger spoke their language without requiring prior revelation of language. (3) Al-Isfarayini proposes a distribution (tawzi'): the essential expressions are divinely taught, while additional words may be conventional. Finally, a group (including many Hanafis) opts for suspension (tawaqquf) due to lack of decisive evidence, but the author suggests that the apparent view is that of al-Ash'ari. The passage emphasizes the role of rational inference and Quranic interpretation in deriving the ruling. All examples are within the Hanafi usul tradition, and the discussion sets the stage for later issues like qiyas in language.

Key Terms
التوقيف (al-tawqif): The view that language is divinely instituted by Allah.
الاصطلاح (al-istilah): The view that language is established by human convention.
التوزيع (al-tawzi'): The view that some words are divinely instituted and others are conventional.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension of judgment due to lack of decisive evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Tawqif
B) Istilah
C) Tawzi'
D) Tawaqquf

Key Takeaway
The origin of language is a legitimate usul dispute; while Hanafis often adopt tawaqquf, the apparent view (al-Ash'ari) favors tawqif (divine institution).

The Occurrence of Mushtarak in the Qur'an and Hadith
Evidence and refutation regarding mushtarak in revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the occurrence of mushtarak in the Qur'an and Hadith, identify the textual proofs, and evaluate the responses to the deniers' objections.

Core Idea
Mushtarak occurs in divine texts, and ambiguity with context or explanation serves rhetorical and legal purposes.

One of the key debates in Hanafi usul al-fiqh is whether mushtarak (homonyms) appear in the Qur'an and Hadith. The dominant Hanafi view affirms that they do, citing examples like the word 'qur'' (menstruation/purity) and 'as'asa' (approach/depart). Critics argue that such words would either make speech redundant or leave it ambiguous. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi evidence and the compelling rebuttals that uphold the occurrence of mushtarak.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Issue
Recognize that the question is whether mushtarak occurs in the Qur'an and Hadith. There are three positions: it does not occur, it does occur, or it occurs only in the Qur'an. The text affirms occurrence in both.
The Hanafi school generally adopts the affirmative.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Proofs (Dalil Naqli)
Examine the specific Qur'anic verses and hadith cited: 'Three quru'' (2:228) and 'the night when it 'as'asa' (81:17) from the Qur'an, and the hadith 'Leave prayer during your aqra''. Understand why these are considered mushtarak.
The word 'qur'' is homonymous for menstruation and purity; 'as'asa' for coming and going.
3
Understand the Opponents' Objection
The deniers argue that if mushtarak is used, it either requires clarifying context (making speech redundant) or lacks it (making it useless), both violating eloquence.
This objection is based on a specific conception of rhetorical perfection.
4
Follow the Hanafi Rebuttal
The Hanafi response refutes the necessity of redundancy: ambiguity followed by explanation is eloquent; there may be no single word conveying the intended meaning; contextual clues can suffice; and unspecified mushtarak can yield other benefits like directing to multiple interpretations or earning reward for readiness.
Each point dismantles the opposition's assumed implications.
5
Recognize the Usul Importance
Understanding this debate affects how a mujtahid deals with ambiguous texts: mushtarak permits multiple valid interpretations when context clarifies, or may be left general for comprehensive application.
The thamarah al-khilaf is in the methodology of interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة البيان (هل وقع المشترك في القرآن اختلف فيه ؟ فقيل : لا وقيل : نعم (وقيل و) هل وقع (في الحديث) اختلف فيه أيضاً والأصح الوقوع في القرآن بل وفي الحديث أيضاً (ولنا) قوله تعالى : ثَلَثَةَ قُرُ ﴾ [البقرة : ۲۲۸] والقرء للحيض والطهر كما مر وقوله تعالى : ﴿وَالَّيلِ إِذَا [التكوير : ۱۷] (وعسعس لأقبل وأدبر) وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : دعي الصلاة أيام أقرائك» رواه الترمذي المنكرون (قالوا : إن وقع المشترك في القرآن (مبيناً طال) الكلام (بلا فائدة) وهو مخل بالبلاغة والملازمة لأن المنفرد عن الاشتراك الدال على ما أريد من المشترك مع مغن عنه أي عن المشترك المبين، وعلى هذا لا يرد ما أورد الفاضل التفتازاني على التقرير المشهور بأن البيان يكفي، فيكون المشترك حشواً أنه يجوز أن يحصل البيان من المجموع واحد حتى يكون الآخر طولاً (وغير المبين غير مفيد للتفهيم فلا يقع البتة (قلنا) نختار الشق الأول ولا نسلم الملازمة، بل (الإبهام) أولاً ثم التفسير ) له من البلاغة) فلا يكون بلا فائدة (و) أيضاً ربما لم يكن هناك منفرد) يفيد معنى هذا المشترك المقرون بالبيان، فلا يكون تطويلاً، بل هو المتعين طريقاً إلى الإفهام (و) أيضاً قد تكون (القرينة) لتعيين المراد (حالية) فلا طول في اللفظ ونختار أيضاً الشق الثاني، ولا نسلم أنه غير مفيد إذ لا يلزم أن تكون الفائدة الإفهام، بل هناك فوائد أخرى تحصل من غير المبين كيف وغير المبين يفيد الذهاب إلى كل مذهب نحو عسعس والاستعداد للامتثال) فينال الثواب (وقد يقصد الإجمال) أي الحكم المجمل (لا إفادة الخصوصيات كأسماء الأجناس فإنه لا يقصد منها إفادة خصوص فرد.
Chapter on Explanation: Did mushtarak occur in the Qur'an? There is disagreement. It is said: No. And it is said: Yes. And it is said: Did it occur in hadith? There is also disagreement. The correct view is that it occurs in the Qur'an and also in hadith. Our proof: Allah's statement: 'Three quru'' [2:228] and qur' is for menstruation and purity as mentioned. And His statement: 'And the night when it 'as'asa' [81:17] - 'as'asa means approaching and departing. And the Prophet's statement: 'Leave prayer during your aqra'' narrated by Tirmidhi. The deniers said: If mushtarak occurs in the Qur'an, it would make speech lengthy without benefit, and that violates eloquence - because there is a word free from homonymy that indicates what is intended from the mushtarak, so it would be redundant. The explanation is sufficient, so the mushtarak would be filler. And unspecified mushtarak is useless for understanding and thus does not occur at all. We said: We choose the first part but do not accept the implication. Rather, ambiguity first then explanation has eloquence, so it is not without benefit. Also, perhaps there is no independent word that gives the meaning of this mushtarak with explanation, so it is not lengthy but the necessary way to convey. Also, the context (qarina) may be situational, so there is no wordiness. And we also choose the second part: we do not accept that it is useless, because benefit is not limited to conveying; there are other benefits from unspecified mushtarak, like directing to each interpretation, as in 'as'asa, and preparation for compliance, so one attains reward. And sometimes the intention is generality, i.e., a general judgment, not conveying particulars, like nouns for species where conveying a particular individual is not intended.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage discusses whether mushtarak (homonyms) occur in the Qur'an and Hadith. The correct position (al-ash) according to the text is that they do occur. Proofs include the word 'qur'' which means both menstruation and purity, and 'as'asa' which means both to come and to go. The deniers object that mushtarak in revealed texts either requires explanatory context (making speech redundant) or lacks it (making it useless). The rebuttal argues that ambiguity followed by explanation is rhetorically effective, that there may be no alternative word, and that context can resolve ambiguity without extra words. Moreover, unspecified mushtarak can have benefits like directing the mind to multiple meanings or preparing for obedience.

Key Terms
قرء (qur'): لفظ مشترك بين الحيض والطهر
عسعس ('as'asa): لفظ مشترك بين الإقبال والإدبار
مشترك (mushtarak): A word that has more than one meaning, i.e., a homonym.
البيان (al-bayan): Explanation or clarification, often following an ambiguous term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is cited as evidence for the occurrence of mushtarak in the Qur'an?
A) The word 'salat' used in different meanings.
B) The word 'qur'' meaning both menstruation and purity.
C) The word 'zakat' used for both purification and charity.
D) The word 'iman' for both belief and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak occurs in the Qur'an and Hadith; ambiguity is resolved by context and explanation, and unexplained mushtarak can serve other beneficial purposes.

Majaz in the Quran and Hadith: Refuting the Zahiris
Proving metaphor in Quran/hadith using verses and refuting Zahiri objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi evidence for majaz in the Quran using Quranic verses and refute the Zahiri claim that majaz is a lie.

Core Idea
Metaphor (majaz) is affirmed in Quran and hadith; the Zahiri denial is refuted by linguistic evidence and the literal/intended distinction.

The Hanafi school, along with the majority of scholars, holds that figurative language (majaz) occurs in the Quran and hadith. The Zahiris deny this, arguing it would be falsehood. This passage presents the Hanafi evidence and answers the Zahiri objections. By analyzing the verses and arguments, you will understand why majaz is a necessary part of Quranic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim and Zahiri position
Note that the Hanafis affirm majaz in Quran and hadith, while the Zahiris deny it entirely.
2
Examine the first Quranic proof (2:15)
Analyze why 'Allah mocks them' cannot be literal mockery and must be a metaphor for requital.
This establishes the principle: when literal meaning is impossible, majaz is recognized.
3
Examine additional proofs and the handling of 42:11
Review verses 19:4, 17:24, 12:36, and the hadith. Understand the debate over 'Nothing is like Him' and the author's argument that majaz includes addition and omission.
The Zahiris attempt to dismiss 42:11 as not being majaz, but the author insists it is within the dispute.
4
Analyze the Zahiri objection that majaz is a lie
The Zahiris argue that if a metaphorical statement can be negated (e.g., 'the head did not kindle'), it would be false. The Hanafi response: negation applies to the literal meaning, not the intended metaphor.
Also, the Quran reports false statements of others without endorsing them.
5
Evaluate the nuanced distinction about majaz in the eternal attribute vs. recited words
Some suggest majaz is only in the recited Quranic text, not in the eternal divine attribute. Consider the implications and the author's position.
The passage ends with a truncated discussion; we take the author's approval of the Hanafi stance.

مسألة اتفاقاً المجاز واقع في القرآن والحديث خلافاً للظاهرية لنا قوله تعالى : اللَّهُ يَسْتَهْزِئُ بهم [البقرة: ١٥] فإن الاستهزاء حقيقة لا يتصور منه تعالى فهو مجاز عن الجزاء المشابه له وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاشْتَعَلَ الرَّأْسُ شَيْبًا ﴾ [مريم: ٤] فإن الاشتعال الحقيقي لا يمكن فهو مجاز عن بياض الشيب وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاخْفِضْ لَهُمَا جَنَاحَ الذُّلِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٢٤] إذ لا جناح للذل حقيقة بل استعارة بالكناية (وغيرها) من الآيات نحو إنّي أَرَني أَعْصِرُ خَمْرًا ﴾ [يوسف: ٣٦] وقوله من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه والاستدلال بقوله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١] ) فإن الكاف زائدة فيه فهو مجاز بالزيادة خروج عن البحث فإن النزاع إنما هو في المعنى المذكور) من المستعمل في غير ما وضع له والمجاز بالزيادة والنقصان ليس منه كما قيل، أقول ليس هذا خروجاً عن المبحث (بل) النزاع فيه مطلقاً سواء كان بالمعنى المذكور أو بالزيادة أو بالنقصان كما يدل عليه دليلهم من لزوم الكذب واستخلاصهم) عن هذا الاستدلال بأنه لا مجاز فيه ولا زيادة بل الكاف على معناه وقرروه بأنه نص في نفي اللازم) وهو مثل المثل (والمقصود نفي الملزوم) وهو المثل فإن المثل ملزوم لمثل المثل، لأنه إذا كان للشيء مثل فهو مثل مثله فإن قيل : نفي مثل المثل نفي له وهو كفر ولا يليق بجنابه أن يكنى بالكفر، قلت : ليس كفراً، لأن مفهومه الصريح هو نفي مثل المثل عن الشيء لا نفي نفسه فتدبر . ووجه دلالة الاستخلاص عليه بأنه لو كان النزاع مخصوصاً لكفى لهم أن يقولوا إنه خارج عن محل النزاع، لكن هذه الدلالة إنما تتم لو لم يكن الاستخلاص تنزلاً (و) يدل عليه (جوابهم عن قوله تعالى : وَسْتَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ ﴾ [يوسف: (۸۲] حكاية عن إخوة يوسف حين جاؤوا من عنده إلى أبيه مع تركهم أخاه العيني من جهة السرقة أنه على سبيل التحدي والمقصود إنك يا يعقوب نبي فاسأل العمران فإنها تجيبك وإن القرية مجتمع الناس فهي حقيقة في الإنسان، فلا استحالة في مأخوذ من قرأت الناقة أي جمعت (ومنه (القرآن لمجموع الآيات، ووجه دلالة هذا الجواب أنه لو لم يكن النزاع عاماً لكفى لهم أن يقولوا يجوز أن يكون هذا من قبيل الحذف خارجاً عما نحن فيه، إلا أنه إنما يتم لو لم يكن تنزلاً وإن كان الاستخلاص المذكور (ضعيفاً) أما الاستخلاص عن الدليل الأول فلأنه تكلف لا يخفى، وأما الجواب الأول عن الدليل الثاني فظاهر أنهم لم يريدوا التحدي كما يدل عليه السياق والعير التي أقبلنا فيها، وأما الثاني فلأن القرية ناقص، وقرأت الناقة والقرآن مهموز اللام فأين الاشتقاق هذا؟ الظاهرية (قالوا : المجاز كذب لأنه يصح نفيه فيصح في اشتعل الرأس شيباً ما اشتعل وإذا كان كذباً (فلا يقع) في القرآن والحديث والجواب أن النفي للحقيقة فهي كذب لا للمجاز المراد، فلا يلزم كذب ما هو المراد (أقول : (وأيضاً لو تم (لا يدل على عدم وقوعه حكاية عن الكفار كعقائدهم الباطلة الواقعة فيه فإنه لا استحالة في نقل الكلام الكاذب ولعل مرادهم أنه لم يقع بتصرف من الشارع) إذ لا مجاز إلا على قانون اللغة لا باختراع منه (فيؤول إلى ما قيل : لا مجاز في القرآن) أي بتصرف منه (بل) المجاز ( في كلام العرب) أي بتصرف منهم، ولعل مراد هذا القائل، إنه لا مجاز في القرآن الذي هو كلام الله تعالى وصفته الغير المخلوقة، وإنما المجاز في كلام العرب، وهو الكلام اللفظي المقروء على الألسنة (وأما قولهم) لو كان المجاز السؤال عنهم
Issue: By unanimous agreement, metaphor occurs in the Quran and hadith, contrary to the Zahiris. Our proof is the verse: 'Allah mocks them' [2:15]; for mockery in its real sense is inconceivable of Him, so it is a metaphor for the similar requital. And His verse: 'And the head has become white with hoariness' [19:4]; for actual kindling is impossible, so it is a metaphor for the whiteness of old age. And His verse: 'And lower to them the wing of humility' [17:24]; for there is no actual wing of humility, but rather a figurative expression by metonymy. And other verses such as 'I see myself pressing wine' [12:36] and the saying: 'Whoever kills a slain man, his spoils are his.' And their inference from the verse: 'Nothing is like Him' [42:11]; for the preposition 'ka' is redundant, so it is a metaphor by addition, which is outside the discussion, for the dispute is about the aforementioned meaning of using a word in other than its original setting. Metaphor by addition and omission is not part of it, as is said. I say: This is not outside the topic, but rather the dispute is about it absolutely, whether it is by the mentioned meaning or by addition or omission, as their proof of the necessity of lying indicates. And their rescue from this inference by saying that there is no metaphor nor addition, but the 'ka' is upon its meaning, and they argued that it is a text that negates the necessary concomitant, which is a thing's equal; and the intended is to negate the thing itself, for a thing's equal is a necessary concomitant of its equal. If it is said: Negation of a thing's equal is a negation of it, and that is disbelief, and it is not appropriate for Him to allude to disbelief, I say: It is not disbelief, because its explicit import is the negation of a thing's equal from the thing, not the negation of itself, so reflect. The aspect of the indication of their rescue is that if the dispute were specific, it would have sufficed for them to say that it is outside the subject of dispute. But this indication only holds if the rescue is not a concession. And it indicates their answer to the verse: 'And ask the village' [12:82] narrated from Joseph's brothers when they came from him to their father, leaving their brother with the Egyptians regarding the theft, that it is in the way of challenge, and the meaning is: O Jacob, you are a prophet, so ask the buildings, for they will answer you. And the village is a gathering of people, so it is literal for humans. There is no impossibility in it being derived from 'qara't al-naqa' meaning she gathered. And from that is 'al-Quran' for the collection of verses. The aspect of the indication of this answer is that if the dispute were not general, it would have sufficed for them to say that this could be of the type of omission, outside what we are in. However, it only holds if it is not a concession. And though the mentioned rescue is weak. As for the rescue from the first proof, it is a clear affectation. And as for the first answer to the second proof, it is apparent that they did not intend challenge, as indicated by the context and 'the caravan with which we came.' And as for the second, the word 'village' is defective, and 'qara't al-naqa' and 'Quran' have a hamzated lam, so where is this derivation? The Zahiris said: Metaphor is a lie because it can be negated; thus in 'the head kindled with hoariness', one can say 'it did not kindle'. And if it is a lie, it does not occur in Quran and hadith. The answer: The negation is of the literal meaning, which is a lie, not of the intended metaphorical meaning, so the intended is not necessarily false. I say: Even if it held, it does not prove its non-occurrence as a narration about disbelievers, like their false beliefs that occur in it, for there is no impossibility in transmitting false speech. Perhaps they meant that it did not occur by the legislator's own invention, for there is no metaphor except according to the laws of language, not by His invention. Thus it reduces to what was said: There is no metaphor in the Quran by His invention, but metaphor is in the speech of the Arabs by their invention. Perhaps this speaker meant that there is no metaphor in the Quran as the speech of Allah and His uncreated attribute, but rather metaphor is in the speech of the Arabs, which is the verbal speech recited on tongues. As for their saying: If metaphor were (the question about them).
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage defends the existence of majaz in the Quran and hadith against the Zahiri school, who deny it. It provides several Quranic verses as evidence: 'Allah mocks them' (2:15) — mockery is metaphor for requital; 'the head kindled with hoariness' (19:4) — impossible literally; 'lower the wing of humility' (17:24) — metonymy; 'pressing wine' (12:36); and the hadith about spoils. The text also addresses the Zahiris' objection using 'Nothing is like Him' (42:11), interpreting the 'ka' as redundant, but the author argues it is not outside the dispute. Further, it refutes the claim that majaz is a lie by distinguishing between literal falsity and intended meaning. Finally, it considers a nuanced view that majaz in the Quran may be only in the recited words, not in the eternal divine attribute. The Hanafi position is that majaz is present in both the Quran and hadith, backed by linguistic evidence.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majāz): Using a word in other than its original meaning due to a relation.
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The original, literal meaning of a word.
الاستعارة (al-istiʿārah): A type of majaz based on similarity (metaphor proper).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Zahiri argument that majaz is a lie invalid?
A) Because the Quran never uses figurative language.
B) Because negation applies to literal meaning, not intended metaphor.
C) Because the Zahiris accept majaz in other contexts.
D) Because the Quran explicitly says it uses metaphors.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms majaz in the Quran and hadith, logically defending it against the Zahiri objection that metaphorical language would constitute falsehood.

Majaz in the Quran: The Hanafi Defense Against the Zahiriyya's Accusation of Kadhb
Expounding the debate on majaz in the Quran and responding to the accusation of kadhb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to IDENTIFY the Zahiriyya's argument that majaz is kadhb, EXPLAIN the Hanafi response based on the distinction between literal and intended meaning, and EVALUATE the thamarah al-khilaf regarding the interpretation of Quranic metaphors.

Core Idea
Majaz is not a lie because negation applies to the literal meaning, not the intended metaphorical meaning.

The Zahiriyya (literalists) argue that metaphor (majaz) is a form of falsehood because its literal meaning can be truthfully negated. This would, if true, preclude the existence of majaz in the Qur'an. The Hanafi school responds by drawing a critical distinction: the negated literal meaning is false, but the intended metaphorical meaning is true. This lesson unpacks the dispute through key Qur'anic examples and the Hanafi rebuttal, revealing how usul al-fiqh deals with figurative language in divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zahiriyya objection
Recognize their claim that majaz is kadhb because a metaphorical statement (e.g., 'the head ignited') can be truthfully negated, implying falsehood.
The literal meaning is indeed false, but the question is whether that makes the overall statement a lie.
2
Trace the Hanafi response
Understand how Hanafis distinguish between literal meaning (which can be false) and intended metaphorical meaning (which is true). Examine the examples from the Quran in the source.
The key is that truth conditions apply to the intended meaning, not the literal sense.
3
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider how this dispute affects tafsir: Hanafi usul permits metaphorical interpretation, while Zahiriyya restricts to literal meanings, impacting rulings derived from such verses.
This difference appears in the interpretation of divine attributes and other figurative expressions.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
فإن الاستهزاء حقيقة لا يتصور منه تعالى فهو مجاز عن الجزاء المشابه له وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاشْتَعَلَ الرَّأْسُ شَيْبًا ﴾ [مريم: ٤] فإن الاشتعال الحقيقي لا يمكن فهو مجاز عن بياض الشيب وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاخْفِضْ لَهُمَا جَنَاحَ الذُّلِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٢٤] إذ لا جناح للذل حقيقة بل هو استعارة بالكناية (وغيرها) من الآيات نحو إنّي أَرَني أَعْصِرُ خَمْرًا ﴾ [يوسف: ٣٦] وقوله من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه والاستدلال بقوله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١] ) فإن الكاف زائدة فيه فهو مجاز بالزيادة خروج عن البحث فإن النزاع إنما هو في المعنى المذكور) من المستعمل في غير ما وضع له والمجاز بالزيادة والنقصان ليس منه كما قيل، أقول ليس هذا خروجاً عن المبحث (بل) النزاع فيه مطلقاً سواء كان بالمعنى المذكور أو بالزيادة أو بالنقصان كما يدل عليه دليلهم من لزوم الكذب واستخلاصهم) عن هذا الاستدلال بأنه لا مجاز فيه ولا زيادة بل الكاف على معناه وقرروه بأنه نص في نفي اللازم) وهو مثل المثل (والمقصود نفي الملزوم) وهو المثل فإن المثل ملزوم لمثل المثل، لأنه إذا كان للشيء مثل فهو مثل مثله فإن قيل : نفي مثل المثل نفي له وهو كفر ولا يليق بجنابه أن يكنى بالكفر، قلت : ليس كفراً، لأن مفهومه الصريح هو نفي مثل المثل عن الشيء لا نفي نفسه فتدبر . ووجه دلالة الاستخلاص عليه بأنه لو كان النزاع مخصوصاً لكفى لهم أن يقولوا إنه خارج عن محل النزاع، لكن هذه الدلالة إنما تتم لو لم يكن الاستخلاص تنزلاً (و) يدل عليه (جوابهم عن قوله تعالى : ﴿وَاسْتَأْتِ الْقَرْيَةَ﴾ [يوسف: ٨٢] حكاية عن إخوة يوسف حين جاؤوا من عنده إلى أبيه مع تركهم أخاه [العيني؟] من جهة السرقة أنه على سبيل التحدي والمقصود إنك يا يعقوب نبي فاسأل العمران فإنها تجيبك وإن القرية مجتمع الناس فهي حقيقة في الإنسان، فلا استحالة في مأخوذ من قرأت الناقة أي جمعت (ومنه (القرآن لمجموع الآيات، ووجه دلالة هذا الجواب أنه لو لم يكن النزاع عاماً لكفى لهم أن يقولوا يجوز أن يكون هذا من قبيل الحذف خارجاً عما نحن فيه، إلا أنه إنما يتم لو لم يكن تنزلاً وإن كان الاستخلاص المذكور (ضعيفاً) أما الاستخلاص عن الدليل الأول فلأنه تكلف لا يخفى، وأما الجواب الأول عن الدليل الثاني فظاهر أنهم لم يريدوا التحدي كما يدل عليه السياق والعير التي أقبلنا فيها، وأما الثاني فلأن القرية ناقص، وقرأت الناقة والقرآن مهموز اللام فأين الاشتقاق هذا؟ الظاهرية (قالوا : المجاز كذب لأنه يصح نفيه فيصح في اشتعل الرأس شيباً ما اشتعل وإذا كان كذباً (فلا يقع) في القرآن والحديث والجواب أن النفي للحقيقة فهي كذب لا للمجاز المراد، فلا يلزم كذب ما هو المراد (أقول : (وأيضاً لو تم (لا يدل على عدم وقوعه حكاية عن الكفار كعقائدهم الباطلة الواقعة فيه فإنه لا استحالة في نقل الكلام الكاذب ولعل مرادهم أنه لم يقع بتصرف من الشارع) إذ لا مجاز إلا على قانون اللغة لا باختراع منه (فيؤول إلى ما قيل : لا مجاز في القرآن) أي بتصرف منه (بل) المجاز ( في كلام العرب) أي بتصرف منهم، ولعل مراد هذا القائل، إنه لا مجاز في القرآن الذي هو كلام الله تعالى وصفته الغير المخلوقة، وإنما المجاز في كلام العرب، وهو الكلام اللفظي المقروء على الألسنة (وأما قولهم) لو كان المجاز في القرآن (يلزم أن يكون الباري متجوزاً) ولا يصح إطلاق المتجوز عليه سبحانه (فجوابه أن فيه إيهاماً بالمنقصة فإنه للانتقال من مكان إلى آخر، فلذا لا يطلق عليه لا لأنه لم يورد المجازي في كلامه أو لا توقيف من الشارع، وأسماء الله تعالى توقيفية فلا يطلق المتجوز عليه لهذا لا لعدم إيراده المجاز .
The third article on linguistic principles. Then mockery (istihza) is a reality not conceivable from Him Exalted, so it is a metaphor (majaz) for the similar recompense. And His statement: 'And the head has become white with gray hair' [Maryam:4], for real kindling is impossible, so it is a metaphor for whiteness of gray hair. And His statement: 'And lower to them the wing of humility' [Al-Isra:24], for there is no wing for humility in reality, but it is a metonymical metaphor (istihara bil-kinaya). (And others) of the verses like 'Indeed, I see myself pressing wine' [Yusuf:36] and his saying: 'Whoever kills a slain person, then his spoil is his.' And the inference by His statement: 'There is nothing like Him' [Al-Shura:11] — for the kaaf (letter) is extra in it, so it is a metaphor by addition (majaz bil-ziyada), which is outside the discussion, for the dispute is only regarding the mentioned meaning: using a word in other than its original placement, and metaphor by addition and deletion is not from it as was said. I say: This is not outside the discussion, rather the dispute is absolute, whether it be by the mentioned meaning or by addition or deletion, as indicated by their proof of the necessity of falsehood (kadhb) and their extraction from this inference that there is no metaphor nor addition, but the kaaf is on its meaning, and they established it as a clear text (nass) in negating the necessary (lazim) which is the like of the like (mithl al-mithl), and the intent is the negation of the subject (malzum) which is the like (mithl), for the like necessitates a like of the like. So if it is said: Negation of the like of the like is negation of it, and that is disbelief, and it is not befitting His presence to allude to disbelief. I say: It is not disbelief, because its explicit meaning is the negation of the like of the like from the thing, not negation of itself, so contemplate. And the indication of their extraction is that if the dispute were specific, it would suffice for them to say that it is outside the subject of dispute, but this indication is only complete if the extraction is not a concession. And it is indicated by their answer to His statement: 'And ask the village' [Yusuf:82] as a narration about the brothers of Yusuf when they came from him to their father, leaving their brother [al-'aini?] regarding the theft, that it is by way of a challenge, and the intent is that O Jacob, you are a prophet, so ask the buildings ('umran) for they will answer you, and the village is a gathering of people, so it is real (haqiqa) in humans, so there is no impossibility. It is derived from 'qara't al-naqa' meaning she gathered, and from it is 'Quran' for the collection of verses. And the indication of this answer is that if the dispute were general, it would suffice for them to say that it is permissible for this to be of the deletion type outside our subject, except that it is only complete if not a concession, even if the mentioned extraction is weak. As for the extraction from the first proof, it is an obvious affectation. As for the first answer to the second proof, it is apparent that they did not intend a challenge as indicated by the context and the caravan in which we came. As for the second, because the village (qarya) is deficient (naqis), and 'qara't al-naqa' and 'Quran' have a hamzated lam, so where is this derivation? The Zahiriyya said: Metaphor is a lie (kadhb) because its negation is valid, so it is valid in 'the head has become white with gray hair' that it did not ignite. And if it is a lie, then it does not occur in the Quran and Hadith. The answer: Negation applies to the literal meaning, which is a lie, not to the intended metaphor, so the intended is not necessarily a lie. I say: Also, if it were complete, it does not indicate its non-occurrence in the narration about the disbelievers like their false beliefs that occur in it, for there is no impossibility in transmitting false speech. Perhaps their intent is that it did not occur by the legislator's initiative, for there is no metaphor except according to the laws of language, not by his invention. So it comes down to what was said: There is no metaphor in the Quran—by his initiative—but metaphor is in Arab speech—by their initiative. Perhaps the intent of this speaker is that there is no metaphor in the Quran which is the speech of Allah Almighty and His uncreated attribute; rather, metaphor is in Arab speech, which is the verbal speech recited on tongues. And as for their saying: If there were metaphor in the Quran, then the Creator would be called a user of metaphor (mutajawiz), and it is not valid to apply that term to Him. The answer: That implies a deficiency, for it is to move from one place to another, so it is not applied to Him, not because He did not use metaphor in His speech or because of lack of divine instruction (tawqif). The names of Allah are tawqifiyya, so the term mutajawiz is not applied to Him for this reason, not because of absence of metaphor.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the third article on linguistic principles, specifically the nature of majaz (metaphor) in the Qur'an. The author begins by giving examples of metaphorical expressions in the Qur'an, such as 'and the head has become white with gray hair' (Maryam:4) and 'lower to them the wing of humility' (al-Isra:24), showing that the literal meaning is impossible and therefore a metaphorical interpretation is necessary. He then addresses the claim that the verse 'There is nothing like Him' (al-Shura:11) involves an extra letter (the kaaf), making it a metaphor by addition, but he argues that the dispute includes all forms of majaz, including addition and deletion. The main objection comes from the Zahiriyya (literalists), who argue that majaz is kadhb (a lie) because if someone says 'the head ignited', the literal meaning can be negated truthfully, implying that the metaphorical statement is false and thus cannot occur in the Qur'an. The author responds that the negation applies to the literal meaning, not the intended metaphorical meaning. He further analyzes the verse 'and ask the village' (Yusuf:82), where the word 'village' is used to mean 'people', showing that this is a metaphorical usage based on derivation from 'qara't al-naqa' (the she-camel gathered). The discussion then turns to the claim that if majaz were in the Qur'an, Allah would be described as 'mutajawiz' (one who uses metaphor), which is inappropriate. The answer is that this term implies deficiency (moving from one place to another) and is not among the revealed names of Allah, so its prohibition is due to tawqif (divine instruction), not because of the absence of majaz. Throughout, the author presents the Hanafi position that majaz is valid in the Qur'an and is not kadhb.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): A word used in other than its original literal meaning due to a relationship between meanings.
كذب (kadhib): A statement that does not correspond with reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the Zahiriyya claim that majaz is kadhb?
A) Majaz is not in the Quran anyway.
B) The negation applies to the literal meaning, not the intended metaphorical meaning.
C) The Quran cannot contain false statements.
D) The Zahiriyya are not qualified.

Key Takeaway
Majaz is valid in the Quran and is not false; the Zahiriyya's objection confuses literal falsity with metaphorical truth.

The Debate on Mu'rab (Arabicized Words) in the Quran
Usul dispute on mu'rab in the Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mu'rab in the Quran, identify the key evidence, and explain the responses to the deniers' two arguments.

Core Idea
The Quran contains Arabicized words that were foreign in origin but became Arabic through usage, preserving its Arabic composition.

This passage tackles a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: Does the Quran contain words of non-Arabic origin (mu'rab)? The Hanafi school, following Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah, affirms this, while the majority denies it. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our ability to interpret Quranic verses that involve foreign-origin terms and clarifies how the Arabic nature of the Quran is understood in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main arguments of the deniers
Read the passage and extract the two Quranic verses the deniers use: (1) Yusuf:2 claiming the Quran is wholly Arabic, and (2) Fussilat:44 which they interpret as negating a mixture of languages.
Focus on the logical structure: if part is foreign, the whole is not Arabic (argument 1); mixture is negated (argument 2).
2
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal to argument 1
The passage responds by (a) asserting that arabicization transforms foreign words into Arabic, (b) reinterpreting the pronoun 'it' in Yusuf:2 as referring to the surah, not the entire Quran, and (c) invoking the principle that the majority (of words) gives the whole its ruling.
Note the shift from individual words to composition (nazm) as the criterion for Arabicness.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal to argument 2
The passage argues that Fussilat:44 does not negate division; rather it asks whether it is non-Arabic speech to an Arab. Arabicization removes the foreignness, so there is no actual mixture.
Understand the linguistic nuance: the verse is about understandability, not purity.
4
Evaluate the evidence for mu'rab
Identify the specific words cited (mishkah, sijjil, qistas) and note the textual critique that mishkah may not be Indian. Also note that proper nouns like Ibrahim are excluded from the debate.
This step involves sifting valid evidence from weak claims.
5
Derive the usul principle at work
Generalize: The Hanafi approach prioritizes the overall composition and the effect of arabicization over the origin of individual words. This reflects the principle that language is defined by usage and structure (nazm).
This connects the specific dispute to broader usul methodology.

الأظهر أن في القرآن معرباً)، وهو لفظ استعمله العرب على وضعه العجمي في محاوراتهم (كما روي عن عبد الله بن عباس وعكرمة ونفاه الأكثر، لنا المشكاة هندية، وسجيل فارسية) أصله سنك كل وقسطاس (رومية وقد وقعت في القرآن قال الله تعالى: ﴿مَثَلُ نوره كمشكاة﴾ [النور: ٣٥] وقال: ﴿وَزِنُوا بِالْقِسْطَاسِ الْمُسْتَقِيمِ﴾ [الإسراء: ٣٥] وقال: ﴿ترميهم بحجارة من سجيل﴾ [الفيل: ٤] ثم كون المشكاة هندية غير ظاهر، فإن كالصابئون فإنها لغة فارسية وعربية أيضاً (بعيد) فإنه نادر لا يقاس عليه غيره، ثم إنه لا اتفاق في الصابئون فإن الذي في العربية بالصاد وفي الفارسية بالسين، ونص أهل الفرس على أنه لا صاد في لغتنا والاستدلال بنحو إبراهيم فإنه لفظ أعجمي، وقد وقع في القرآن (لا لأن العلم لا نزاع فيه أي في وقوعه في القرآن، فنحو إبراهيم خارج عن مسألتنا على أنه ليس بمعرب فإنه اسم الجنس الذي وضعه غير العرب ثم استعمله على ذلك الوضع بالتغيير أولاً، فالعلم خارج عنه فلا حاجة إلى تخصيص زائد، ثم المنكرون للوقوع (قالوا) أولاً (لو وقع المعرب في القرآن لزم حينئذ أن لا يكون عربياً لانتفاء) عربية (الكل بانتفاء) عربية (الجزء) والتالي باطل كيف وقد قال الله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَهُ قُرْءَانَا عَرَبِيًّا﴾ [يوسف: ٢] قلنا لا نسلم الملازمة وإنما يلزم) عدم كونه عربياً (لو لم يكن معرباً) وإذا كان معرباً صار عربياً بالتعريب على أن ضمير إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَهُ ﴾ للسورة) على تأويل الكلام وحينئذ فبطلان اللازم ممنوع والآية إنما تدل على أن السورة التي هي فيها عربية، فإن قلت: فكيف يصح حمل القرآن على السورة؟ قال : والقرآن كالماء) يصدق على القليل والكثير (مع أن للأكثر حكم الكل وإذا كان الأكثر عربياً كان الكل عربياً، فيجوز أن يكون إطلاق العربي على القرآن باعتبار أكثر الأجزاء فتدبر ولا يبعد أن يقال : المراد إنا أنزلناه قرآنا عربي النظم لا المفردات، فإن المعتبر في كون اللغة فارسية أو عربية هو النظم (و) قالوا (ثانياً) لو كان في القرآن معرب لزم تنويعه إلى الأعجمي والعربي، وهو باطل إذ (قوله: ﴿ أَعْجَمِيٌّ وَعَرَبيٌّ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٤] ينفي التنوع (قلنا لا نسلم أنه ينفي التنوع بل المعنى: أكلام أعجمي ومخاطب عربي لا يفهم) فعن التنوع ونفيه ساكت (أقول) الملازمة ممنوعة وإنما يلزم التنوع لولا التعريب) إذ بالتعريب صار الكل عربياً على أن وقوع لفظ فقط لا يستلزم تنوع الكلام فافهم.
Issue: Two languages (ajami and arabic). The most apparent is that there are arabicized words (mu'rab) in the Quran, i.e., a word that the Arabs use according to its non-Arabic original in their conversations, as narrated from Abdullah ibn Abbas and Ikrimah, and the majority deny it. Our evidence: 'al-mishkah' is Indian, 'sijjil' is Persian (its origin is 'sang kal'), and 'qistas' is Roman. They have occurred in the Quran. Allah said: 'The likeness of His light is as a niche' (al-Nur:35), and 'Weigh with the straight balance' (al-Isra:35), and 'Throwing them with stones of baked clay' (al-Fil:4). Then, that 'al-mishkah' is Indian is not apparent, for the Brahmins who are knowledgeable in Indian languages do not know it. Yes, 'al-miskah' with damma on mim and important sin, meaning 'smile', is Indian, but not in the Quran in that meaning, as in the gloss. If you say: It is possible that there is agreement (between Arabic and foreign language), like 'al-sabi'un' which is both Persian and Arabic. (Reply: That is far-fetched) because it is rare and not analogous to others. Moreover, there is no agreement in 'al-sabi'un', for in Arabic it is with 'sad' and in Persian with 'sin', and Persian scholars state that there is no 'sad' in their language. And the inference by something like 'Ibrahim' which is a non-Arabic word and has occurred in the Quran (is invalid) because proper nouns (a'lam) are not disputed regarding their occurrence in the Quran, so 'Ibrahim' is outside our issue; moreover, it is not a mu'rab because it is a proper noun placed by non-Arabs then used by Arabs with or without change. So proper nouns are excluded, no need for further specification. Then the deniers of occurrence said: First, if mu'rab occurred in the Quran, then it would not be Arabic because non-existence of the whole follows from non-existence of a part. The consequent is false, for Allah said: 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran' (Yusuf:2). We say: We do not accept the entailment; rather, it would not be Arabic only if it were not arabicized (mu'rab). But if it is arabicized, it becomes Arabic by arabicization. Moreover, the pronoun 'innahu' (it) refers to the surah (chapter) on the interpretation of the speech. So the falsity of the consequent is not conceded; the verse only indicates that the surah in which it occurs is Arabic. If you say: How is it valid to apply 'Quran' to the surah? He said: 'Quran' is like water, applying to both a little and a lot. Moreover, the majority has the ruling of the whole: if most is Arabic, the whole is Arabic. So it is permissible to call the Quran Arabic in consideration of most of its parts. Contemplate, and it is not far-fetched to say that the meaning is: We sent it down as a Quran of Arabic composition (nathm), not of individual words. For the consideration of whether a language is Persian or Arabic is based on composition. And they said: Second, if there were mu'rab in the Quran, it would necessitate its division into non-Arabic and Arabic, which is invalid because His statement: 'Non-Arabic and Arabic' (Fussilat:44) negates division. We say: We do not accept that it negates division; rather, the meaning is: Is it a non-Arabic speech addressed to an Arab who does not understand? So it is silent about division and its negation. I say: The entailment is not conceded; rather, division would occur without arabicization, for by arabicization it all becomes Arabic, especially since the occurrence of only a word does not necessitate division of the speech, so understand.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of whether there are Arabicized words (mu'rab) in the Qur'an. The author states that the most apparent opinion, attributed to Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah, is that such words exist, while the majority of scholars deny this. Evidence is presented from specific words found in the Qur'an: 'mishkah' (niche) claimed to be Indian, 'sijjil' (baked clay) from Persian 'sang kal' (stone and clay), and 'qistas' (balance) from Roman. The author then critiques the claim about 'mishkah', noting that Brahmins are unfamiliar with it, though another word 'miskah' (with different letters) meaning 'smile' is Indian but not in the Qur'an. The argument that there could be accidental agreement between languages is rejected using the example of 'al-sabi'un' (the Sabians) which has different letters in Arabic (sad) and Persian (sin). The use of proper nouns like 'Ibrahim' is excluded from the debate. The deniers present two main arguments: (1) If there were foreign words, the Qur'an would not be entirely Arabic, contradicting 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an' (Yusuf:2). The Hanafi response is that arabicization transforms foreign words into Arabic, and the verse may refer to the surah or to the composition (nazm) rather than individual words. (2) The presence of foreign words would make the Qur'an a mixture, conflicting with the verse 'Non-Arabic and Arabic' (Fussilat:44) which suggests purity. The Hanafi response is that this verse asks whether it is a non-Arabic speech to an Arab, not about division; arabicization removes the division. The author concludes that the occurrence of a few foreign words does not affect the overall Arabic composition.

Key Terms
معرب (muʿrab): A word originally from a non-Arabic language that has been used by Arabs and considered part of Arabic after modification.
عجمي (ʿajamī): Non-Arabic; a foreign language, especially Persian in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) إبراهيم
B) مشكاة
C) الصابئون
D) الماء

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms the presence of arabicized words in the Quran, relying on the process of arabicization and the principle that language is defined by composition, not isolated origins.

Arabicized Words in the Quran: A Hanafi Usul Analysis
Dispute over existence of non-Arabic words in Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on arabicized words in the Quran and evaluate the opposing school's objections using Quranic evidence and Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
Arabicized words in the Quran become Arabic through usage; the Quran's overall Arabicness is preserved by its majority composition.

This passage delves into a foundational usul debate: does the Quran contain any non-Arabic words? While some scholars insist on purely Arabic vocabulary, the Hanafi school acknowledges the presence of arabicized terms (mu'rab). The discussion examines key Quranic examples and responds to objections, showing how arabicization does not undermine the Quran's linguistic identity. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to interpret Quranic language and engage with scholarly differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being disputed? Determine whether the Quran contains words originally non-Arabic that have been adopted into Arabic usage.
The terms 'mu'rab' and 'a'jami' are central.
2
Examine the Hanafi evidence
What are the specific words (mishkat, sijjil, qistas) cited as examples, and which Quranic verses contain them? How does the author justify their inclusion?
Note the author's qualification about mishkat being Indian.
3
Analyze the opposing school's first objection
What is the opponent's argument based on 'إنا أنزلناه قرآنا عربيا' (Surah Yusuf 12:2)? How does the denier claim that arabicized words would negate the Quran's Arabicness?
The objection is that the whole is negated by the negation of a part.
4
Trace the Hanafi response to the first objection
How does the author refute the entailment? He offers multiple replies: arabicization makes the word Arabic, the verse refers to the surah, and the majority constitutes the whole. Also consider the interpretation that the verse refers to composition (nazm) not vocabulary.
Notice the use of 'ta'wil' and analogy to water.
5
Analyze the second objection and its response
The opponent cites 'أعجمي وعربي' (Surah Fussilat 41:44) to argue against division into Arabic and non-Arabic. How does the Hanafi response clarify that the verse is a rhetorical question, not a negation of diversity?
The author states 'لا نسلم أنه ينفي التنوع'.

الاتفاق بين اللغتين والاتفاق كالصابئون فإنها لغة فارسية وعربية أيضاً. قلنا: بعيد فإنه نادر لا يقاس عليه غيره، ثم إنه لا اتفاق في الصابئون فإن الذي في العربية بالصاد وفي الفارسية بالسين، ونص أهل الفرس على أنه لا صاد في لغتنا. والاستدلال بنحو إبراهيم فإنه لفظ أعجمي وقد وقع في القرآن لا لأن العلم لا نزاع فيه أي في وقوعه في القرآن، فنحو إبراهيم خارج عن مسألتنا على أنه ليس بمعرب فإنه اسم الجنس الذي وضعه غير العرب ثم استعمله على ذلك الوضع بالتغيير أولاً، فالعلم خارج عنه فلا حاجة إلى تخصيص زائد. ثم المنكرون للوقوع قالوا أولاً: لو وقع المعرب في القرآن لزم حينئذ أن لا يكون عربياً لانتفاء الكل بالتعريب. على أن ضمير ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَاهُ﴾ للسورة على تأويل الكلام. وحينئذ فبطلان اللازم ممنوع. والآية إنما تدل على أن السورة التي هي فيها عربية. فإن قلت: فكيف يصح حمل القرآن على السورة؟ قال: والقرآن كالماء يصدق على القليل والكثير مع أن للأكثر حكم الكل. وإذا كان الأكثر عربياً كان الكل عربياً، فيجوز أن يكون إطلاق العربي على القرآن باعتبار أكثر الأجزاء. فتدبر. ولا يبعد أن يقال: المراد إنا أنزلناه قرآنا عربي النظم لا المفردات، فإن المعتبر في كون اللغة فارسية أو عربية هو النظم. وقالوا ثانياً: لو كان في القرآن معرب لزم تنويعه إلى الأعجمي والعربي وهو باطل إذ قوله: ﴿أَعْجَمِيٌّ وَعَرَبِيٌّ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٤] ينفي التنوع. قلنا: لا نسلم أنه ينفي التنوع بل المعنى: أكلام أعجمي ومخاطب عربي لا يفهم؟ فعن التنوع ونفيه ساكت. أقول: الملازمة ممنوعة، وإنما يلزم التنوع لولا التعريب، إذ بالتعريب صار الكل عربياً. على أن وقوع لفظ فقط لا يستلزم تنوع الكلام فافهم.
Issue: The two languages [Arabic and non-Arabic]. The apparent view is that there are arabicized words in the Quran, i.e., a word used by Arabs according to its non-Arabic original usage in their speech, as narrated from Abdullah ibn Abbas and Ikrimah, but the majority deny it. Our evidence: al-mishkat is Indian, sijjil is Persian, originally 'sang gil', and qistas is Roman, and these occur in the Quran. Allah said: 'The likeness of His light is as a niche' [24:35], and 'Weigh with the straight balance' [17:35], and 'Pelting them with stones of baked clay' [105:4]. But that mishkat is Indian is not apparent, for the Brahmins knowledgeable in Indian languages do not know it; rather, 'miskat' with damma on mim and sin means 'smile' in Indian, but not in this Quranic meaning, as in the hashia. If you say: it is possible that there is coincidence between the two languages, like 'al-sabi'un' which is both Persian and Arabic. We say: unlikely, for it is rare and cannot be analogized; moreover, there is no coincidence in 'al-sabi'un' because in Arabic it is with sad and in Persian with sin, and Persian scholars state that there is no sad in their language. And the argument using examples like Ibrahim (which is a non-Arabic name that occurs in the Quran) is invalid because proper names are not subject to dispute regarding their occurrence; Ibrahim is outside our issue and is not arabicized, for it is a proper noun coined by non-Arabs and used as such with or without modification. So proper nouns are excluded, and no extra specification is needed. Then the deniers said: First: if arabicized words occurred in the Quran, it would not be entirely Arabic, because the whole is negated by the negation of a part. The consequent is false, for Allah said: 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran.' We say: we do not grant the entailment; it would only entail non-Arabicness if it were not arabicized. When it is arabicized, it becomes Arabic by arabicization. Moreover, the pronoun 'We have sent it down' refers to the surah, interpreting the speech. Thus the negation of the consequent is denied. The verse only indicates that the surah in which it occurs is Arabic. If you say: how can 'Quran' be interpreted as 'surah'? He said: 'Quran' is like water, applied to both little and much, and the majority has the ruling of the whole. Since the majority is Arabic, the whole is Arabic, so it is permissible to apply 'Arabic' to the Quran in consideration of its majority parts. So reflect. It is not far-fetched to say: the meaning is 'We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran in its composition, not its vocabulary,' for consideration in whether a language is Persian or Arabic is the composition. And they said: Second: if there were arabicized words in the Quran, it would necessitate division into non-Arabic and Arabic, which is invalid, for His statement: 'Non-Arabic and Arabic' [41:44] negates division. We say: we do not grant that it negates division; rather, it means: 'Is it a non-Arabic speech addressed to an Arab who does not understand?' So it is silent about division and its negation. I say: the entailment is denied; division would only be necessary without arabicization, for with arabicization the whole becomes Arabic. Moreover, the occurrence of a single word does not necessitate division of the speech. Understand.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the dispute over whether the Quran contains arabicized words (mu'rab). The author presents the view of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah that such words exist, against the majority denial. He gives examples: mishkat (Indian), sijjil (Persian), and qistas (Roman), each cited in Quranic verses. However, he notes that mishkat as Indian is questionable. The deniers raise two objections. First, if arabicized words were present, the Quran would not be entirely Arabic, contradicting the verse 'We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran.' The author responds that arabicization makes them Arabic; the verse refers to the surah, and since the majority of the Quran is Arabic, the whole is considered Arabic. Alternatively, the verse may refer to composition (nazm) rather than vocabulary. Second, the verse 'Non-Arabic and Arabic' would prohibit division. The author clarifies that the verse questions whether a non-Arabic speech can be addressed to an Arab and does not forbid linguistic diversity. Thus, the Hanafi position upholds the possibility of arabicized words without compromising the Quran's Arabicness.

Key Terms
معرب (muʿrab): A word originally non-Arabic that has been used by Arabs and adapted into Arabic, occurring in the Quran.
مشكاة (mishkāh): A niche or lamp niche; according to the passage, some claim it is of Indian origin.
سجيل (sijjīl): Stones of baked clay; said to be Persian (sang gil).
قسطاس (qisṭās): Balance or scale; claimed to be Roman (Greek) origin.
توقيف (tawqīf): The principle that names of Allah are established by divine revelation and cannot be derived by human reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The verse refers to the surah and the majority parts, not every individual word.
B) The verse means the Quran is entirely Arabic and prohibits any foreign origin.
C) The verse is abrogated by later revelations.
D) The verse applies only to the Meccan surahs.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms arabicized words in the Quran, arguing that arabicization makes them Arabic and the Quran's overall Arabicness is by its majority composition.

Refutation of the Claim That Legal Realties Make the Quran Non-Arabic
Defends that legal realties do not contradict Quran's Arabic nature via mu'rab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul argument against the claim that haqiqah shar'iyyah would make the Quran non-Arabic by analyzing the Quranic verse (12:2) and the concept of mu'rab.

Core Idea
If legal realities were new coinages, the Quran would contain non-Arabic—but the Quran is Arabic, so the objection is invalid because Arabic includes mu'rab words.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: does the existence of legal terminology (haqiqah shar'iyyah) contradict the Quran's Arabic nature? Some scholars argue that if terms like ṣalāh were newly coined by the Lawgiver, they would be non-Arabic, conflicting with the verse 'Inna anzalnāhu Qur'ānan 'arabiyyan.' The Hanafi response, as presented in this passage, refutes that inference by invoking the concept of mu'rab (non-Arabic words naturalized into Arabic).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Claim
The opponent asserts that if haqīqah shar'iyyah existed, the Quran would contain non-Arabic coinages, thus contradicting its description as Arabic. This claim is used to deny the existence of legal realties.
The opponent relies on a literal reading of 'arabiyyan' as excluding any non-Arabic element.
2
Locate the Hanafi Response
The author responds by citing the Quranic verse and referring to the issue of mu'rab (non-Arabic words incorporated into Arabic). The answer was already given in that discussion.
The response implies that the presence of a few non-Arabic words does not negate the overall Arabic nature of the Quran.
3
Analyze the Dalil
The Quranic verse (12:2) is the primary proof. Determine how the opponent and the author each use it. The opponent uses it to deny legal realties; the author uses it to show that the Quran is Arabic despite having mu'rab words.
The verse is decisive in transmission (thubūt qaṭ‘ī) but open to interpretation in meaning (dalālah ẓannī).
4
Understand the Concept of Mu'rab
Mu'rab refers to non-Arabic words that have been naturalized into Arabic and are considered part of the language. The Quran contains such words (e.g., 'Ibrahīm', 'Jibrīl'), yet it remains Arabic.
This concept is key to the Hanafi rebuttal: even if legal terms were borrowed, they become Arabic through usage.
5
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence is whether legal rulings derived from the Quran and Sunnah are based on new linguistic coinages or on existing metaphorical usages. The Hanafi view affects how one derives rulings from terms like ṣalāh, zakāh, etc.
Hanafis treat these as existing words given new meanings by custom (urf), not as inventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية كذلك لصرح به أحد ولو في وقت كما صرحوا ببعض أوضاع اللغة، ثم إن دعوى النقل دعوى على الله تعالى، فلا بد لإثباتها من قاطع وليس ههنا أمارة ظنية فضلاً عن القاطع، فلا يليق بحال مسلم أن يجترىء على الله بما لا يعلم (وأما قولهم في نفي النقل الشرعي : لو كان حقيقة شرعية لكانت غير عربية لعدم وضع العرب ولكان القرآن غير عربي) لاشتماله عليه وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَهُ قُرْآَنًا عَرَبِيًّا﴾ [يوسف: 2] (فقد مر الجواب عنه في مسألة المعرب . تتمة: المعتزلة سموا قسماً من الحقيقة الشرعية: حقيقة دينية، وهو ما دل على أصول الدين كالإيمان والمؤمن دون الصلاة والمصلي ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح، قال في «الحاشية» لا مشاحة معهم في مجرد التسمية، لكن ادعوا أنها موضوعات مبتدأة بلا مناسبة مصحة للتجوز والنقل واستدلوا عليه بأن الإيمان يعتبر فيه الأعمال ومحله الكلام.
The third article: on linguistic principles. Likewise, no one has explicitly stated it, even if at times they have stated some linguistic coinages. Then, claiming transmission is a claim against Allah, so its proof requires decisive evidence, and here there is not even a conjectural indication, let alone decisive proof. So it is not fitting for any Muslim to dare against Allah what he does not know. (As for their statement in denying legal transmission: if it were a legal reality, it would be non-Arabic due to the lack of Arab coinage, and the Quran would be non-Arabic) because it contains it, and Allah said: 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran.' (The answer to it has already passed in the issue of mu'rab). Postscript: The Mu'tazila called a type of legal reality 'religious reality', which is what indicates the principles of religion like iman and mu'min, not salat and musalli, and there is no quibbling over terminology. The Hashiya says: there is no quibbling with them over mere naming, but they claim that they are newly invented subjects without a suitable relation for metaphorical usage and transmission, and they argued that iman includes actions and its locus is speech.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding the existence of haqiqah shar'iyyah (legal realties). The author refutes the claim that legal terms like salat and zakat are haqiqah shar'iyyah newly coined by the Lawgiver. The opponent argues that if such realties existed, they would be non-Arabic coinages, contradicting the Quran's description as Arabic. The author responds by citing the verse and referring to a previous discussion on mu'rab (non-Arabic words in the Quran). Then a postscript differentiates the Mu'tazila position: they accept 'religious reality' for fundamental beliefs but not for practical legal terms, and they argue that iman (faith) includes actions, thus its locus is speech (the heart's affirmation expressed verbally). The author dismisses the semantic quibble.

Key Terms
حقيقة شرعية (haqiqah shar'iyyah): A term used in shariah that is a new coinage by the Lawgiver (like salat for prayer).
نقل شرعي (naql shar'i): The assumed shift of a word from its linguistic meaning to a legal meaning by the Lawgiver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) There is no need for new meanings

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school refutes the claim that legal realties contradict the Quran's Arabic nature by invoking the concept of mu'rab, demonstrating that the presence of a few non-Arabic elements does not undermine the Quran's essential Arabic identity.

Conjunction of Commands and Zakat on Minors: A Hanafi Usul Critique
Refuting the use of conjunction to prove zakat on minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi response to the claim that conjunction of commands implies identical rulings, by distinguishing between bodily and financial obligations.

Core Idea
Conjunction does not force identical rulings when underlying reasons differ: bodily hardship vs. financial delegation.

In this passage, the author confronts an argument that since prayer and zakat are conjoined in the Quran, and prayer is exempted for minors, zakat must also be exempted. The Hanafi school agrees on the outcome but rejects the reasoning, offering a nuanced distinction based on the nature of the obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim and its proof
Some scholars argue that because 'establish prayer' and 'give zakat' are conjoined, and prayer is specific to adults, zakat must also be specific to adults (i.e., not obligatory on minors). Their proof is the Quranic verse.
2
Understand the Hanafi agreement on outcome
The Hanafi school agrees that minors are not obliged to pay zakat, but they reject the reasoning via conjunction.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique: distinction in reason (ʿilla)
Prayer is bodily and imposes hardship on a minor, so specification (takhsis) for adults is justified by necessity. Zakat is financial and can be discharged by a guardian, so no hardship; thus the conjunction does not necessitate the same specification.
This shows that the ruling on the second term depends on its own reason, not merely on the conjunction.
4
Address the objection about multiplicity of addressees
The opponent might say this would make the conjoined command address different people, which is invalid. The author responds that this is allowed for independent sentences (jumal mustaqilla), unlike cases where the second clause has a grammatical position (like a noun in apposition).
The linguistic principle: conjunction of independent sentences does not require unity of addressee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة (الثالثة) نقل ( عن البعض أن عطفها مستقلة على جملة أخرى كذلك يقتضي الاشتراك في الحكم، فلا زكاة في مال الصبي، لقوله تعالى : (وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَوَةَ وَءَاتُوا الزَّكَوةَ ) [البقرة : ٤٣ - ٣ - ١١٠] [النساء : ۷۷] [النور: ٥٦] [المزمل: (۲۰] والأول مخصوص بالبالغ، فكذا الثاني (قلنا): نحن نساعدكم على أن لا زكاة على الصبي، لكن الطريق الذي ذكرتم فاسد، فإن تخصيص الأول للضرورة لا يوجب تخصيص الثاني كما قال : (خص الأول بالعقل لأنها بدنية) والصبي ضعيف البدن، ففي الإيجاب عليه حرج عظيم بخلاف الزكاة فإنها مالية تتأدى بالنائب) فلا حرج في إيجابها عليه فلا يلزم فيه التخصيص فتدبر فإن قلت: وحينئذ يلزم تعدد المخاطب في المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، وقد أبيتم من قبل، قلت: لا بأس في الجمل المستقلة، وإنما الكراهية عنه فيما له محل من الإعراب كلا تقبلوا وأولئك فافهم.
The third benefit: It is transmitted from some that the conjunction of independent sentences to another sentence likewise necessitates sharing in the ruling, so there is no zakat on the property of a minor, because of His saying: 'Establish prayer and give zakat' [Al-Baqarah: 43, etc.]. The first (prayer) is specific to the adult, so likewise the second (zakat). We say: We agree with you that there is no zakat on the minor, but the method you mentioned is corrupt. For specifying the first out of necessity does not necessitate specifying the second, as he said: The first is specified by reason because it is bodily, and the minor is weak of body, so imposing it would be a great hardship. Contrastingly, zakat is financial and can be performed by a deputy, so there is no hardship in imposing it upon him. Thus, specification is not necessitated. So ponder. If you say: Then it necessitates multiplicity of addressees in the conjoined and the conjoined-to, and you have previously forbidden that. I say: There is no harm in independent sentences; the dislike is only regarding those that have a grammatical position of declension, like 'do not accept' and 'those are', so understand.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific argument by some scholars who claim that because prayer and zakat are conjoined in the Quran, and prayer is exempted for minors due to bodily inability, zakat should also be exempted. The author agrees that minors do not pay zakat, but rejects the reasoning via conjunction. The reason for exempting prayer is bodily hardship (the minor is weak), while zakat is financial and can be paid by a guardian, so no hardship. Thus, the conjunction does not force the same ruling. Additionally, the objection that this would cause a difference in addressees (the conjoined commands address different people) is dismissed because here the sentences are independent (not forming a single verbal clause).

Key Terms
جمل مستقلة (jumal mustaqilla): Independent sentences that stand alone; their conjunction does not create a single syntactic unit, so the rule of preferring nearer conjunction may not apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because of the conjunction with prayer in the Quran.
B) Because it is financial and can be performed by a guardian, unlike bodily acts.
C) Because minors are not addressed by any financial obligations.
D) Because zakat is like prayer in requiring physical ability.

Key Takeaway
Conjunction of commands does not force identical rulings when the underlying reasons (ʿilal) differ; each command must be examined independently.
