The Legal Effect of Fa' in Istinbat: Two Case Studies
Deriving legal rulings from the use of fa' in a sale acceptance and a tailor's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the Arabic letter fa' functions as an indicator of sequence and condition in Hanafi usul, and apply this to derive rulings in sale acceptance and permission scenarios.

Core Idea
The letter fa' links condition to result; its presence transforms meaning from absolute to qualified.

This passage presents two classic Hanafi usul examples that illustrate the powerful effect of the particle fa' in legal texts. By understanding how fa' operates as an indicator of ta'qib (sequence) and taqyid (conditionality), we can derive correct rulings in contracts and permissions. The first example deals with acceptance in a sale; the second with a tailor's liability. Both hinge on whether the fa' is present or absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fa' and its occurrence
In each case, locate the letter fa' and the words before and after it. Determine if fa' is used for ta'qib (sequence) or for linking a condition to a result.
In the first case: 'fa-huwa hurr' following an offer. In the second: 'fa-qta'hu' following a condition.
2
Classify the utterance
Is the speaker making an acceptance, a rejection, a permission, or a statement? The classification depends on the implied meaning.
In the sale, 'fa-huwa hurr' could be acceptance or rejection.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
Recall that fa' in Arabic indicates sequence and often conditionality. If fa' follows an offer, it affirms the offer and adds a consequence. If fa' follows a condition, it makes the permission contingent.
The principle: al-fa'u lil-ta'qib wa al-taqyid.
4
Derive the ruling
Based on the presence of fa', determine the legal consequence: validity of sale, liability of tailor, etc.
In the first: sale valid, slave free. In the second: tailor liable if condition not met.
5
Contrast with the negation case
Consider what happens if fa' is not present or if a different wording is used (e.g., 'huwa hurr' without fa' or 'iqta'hu' without fa'). This highlights the decisive role of fa'.
The second part of the passage notes that if the owner simply said 'iqta'hu' (cut it), the tailor is not liable because permission is absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع يتضمن (القبول أي قبول البيع (قوله : فهو حر في جواب بعتكه بألف) لأن الفاء للتعقيب، فيقرر ما سبق ويجعل عقبه ما بعده، فكأنه قال قبلت البيع فهو حر، فيجب الألف ويعتق (لا) هو حر) أي لا يتضمن القبول هو حر (بل هو رد للإيجاب) لأنه إخبار عن الحرية المنافية للبيع ولا تقرير فيه للعقد، فكأنه قال : كيف تبيع وهو حر لا يقبل البيع وضمن الخياط ثوباً قال له مالكه أيكفيني قميصاً ؟ قال : ( هذا الخياط (نعم : (قال) المالك (فاقطعه، فلم يكفه) بعد القطع، وإنما ضمن لأن إذن المالك إنما كان بالقطع مقيد بالكفاية لأن كلمة الفاء للتعقيب، فكأنه قال : إذا كان يكفيني قميصاً ،فاقطعه فلم يتناول إجازته لهذا النحو من القطع فقطع من دون إذن المالك فيضمن (لا) في اقطعه أي لا يضمن الخياط في قوله اقطعه لأنه إجازة مطلقة .
A branch includes (acceptance, i.e., acceptance of sale) (the statement: 'he is free' in response to 'I sold him to you for one thousand') because fa' is for sequence, so it affirms what preceded and makes what follows its consequence, as if he said: 'I accepted the sale, so he is free,' so the thousand is obligatory and he becomes free. (No, he is free) i.e., it does not include acceptance; rather it is a rejection of the offer because it is an announcement of freedom that contradicts the sale and contains no affirmation of the contract, as if he said: 'How can you sell while he is free? He does not accept sale.' And a tailor guaranteed a garment; its owner said to him: 'Will it suffice for a shirt?' He (the tailor) said: 'Yes.' The owner said: 'Then cut it,' and it did not suffice after cutting. He is liable because the owner's permission was only for cutting conditioned upon sufficiency, because the word fa' is for sequence, as if he said: 'If it suffices me for a shirt, then cut it.' So his permission did not cover this type of cutting; thus he cut without the owner's permission and is liable. (No) in 'cut it' i.e., the tailor is not liable in his saying 'cut it' because it is an absolute permission.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is an application (far') of the rule of fa' to two scenarios. First, in a sale contract: if the seller says 'I sold this slave to you for a thousand' and the buyer responds 'fa-huwa hurr' (he is free), there are two interpretations. The correct one is that this is a valid acceptance because fa' indicates sequence and affirmation: the buyer affirms the offer and then declares freedom. The price is owed and the slave becomes free. The alternative view is that it is a rejection because it only announces freedom. Second, a tailor is given a cloth; the owner asks if it can make a shirt; the tailor says yes; the owner says 'fa-qta'hu' (then cut it). If the cloth turns out insufficient, the tailor is liable because the permission was conditioned on sufficiency, indicated by the fa' linking the condition to the permission. If the owner simply said 'iqta'hu' (cut it) without condition, the tailor is not liable because it is absolute permission.

Key Terms
القبول (al-qabul): Acceptance in a contract, specifically the response that matches the offer.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer in a contract.
الفاء (al-fa'): The Arabic letter fa' used as a particle for sequence (ta'qib) and conditionality (taqyid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a tailor is asked 'Will this cloth suffice for a shirt?' and he says 'Yes', and the owner says 'Then cut it' (fa-qta'hu), and it does not suffice, why is the tailor liable?
A) Because his yes was false
B) Because fa' indicates the permission was conditional on sufficiency
C) Because he cut without asking again
D) Because he cut the wrong size

Key Takeaway
The presence of fa' in a legal utterance can transform its meaning from a statement to an acceptance or a conditional permission, making it a critical tool in istinbat.

The Particle 'أو' as Ghayah (End) in Qur'anic Context
Textual analysis of 'أو' in Qur'anic exegesis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'أو' can indicate ghayah (end) or istithna (exception) to interpret a Qur'anic verse and justify the preference for 'ghayah' based on context and hadith.

Core Idea
'أو' can mean 'until' — context and revelation determine the legal meaning.

In this passage, the author delves into the linguistic flexibility of the particle 'أو', arguing that it can serve not just as a disjunctive ('or') but also as a marker of an end point (ghayah) or an exception (istithna). He directly applies this to verse 3:128 of the Quran, where the phrase 'أو يتوب عليهم' is interpreted by the Hanafi school as 'until He turns to them', supported by the historical context of the Prophet's supplication and its subsequent answer. This lesson will walk you through the analytical steps to derive such a meaning and understand the dispute with other tafsir traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic particle
Locate 'أو' in the verse and note its common function. Recognize that Hanafi usul acknowledges multiple possible meanings beyond simple disjunction.
The particle is in Q.3:128: 'أو يتوب عليهم'.
2
Examine the context of revelation (sabab al-nuzul)
Study the hadith about the Prophet's prayer against specific individuals on the day of Uhud. The fact that later they repented indicates that the verse was not giving a choice but setting a condition that led to their repentance.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, etc.
3
Apply the Hanafi tarjih methodology
Weigh the two possible meanings: if 'أو' is simple disjunction, the verse means 'or He turns to them' (which seems almost like a threat); if it means 'until', it becomes 'not for you the decision until He turns to them', aligning with the prophetic event.
Preference is given to the meaning consistent with the Sunnah and the actual outcome.
4
Evaluate the alternative reading
Consider the view of al-Kashshaf and others who treat 'أو' as a conjunction with an interrupting parenthetical. The Hanafi author argues this is forced and unnecessary.
This step highlights the importance of contextual coherence over grammatical complexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية وأخذ يجوز للإمام أن ينفي أي يحبس ولم تعهد هذه المقابلة في الشرع (فقلنا بتوزيع الأجزية على الجنايات) كما بينا لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَجَزَاءُ سَيِّئَةٍ سَيِّئَةٌ مِثْلُهَا ﴾ [الشورى: ٤٠]) وبمثله روى أبو يوسف الخبر أيضاً وشهدت به الآثار أيضاً، والإمام إنما خير في القتل والأخذ وغير حكم الكريمة بهذه الآية وبقصة العرنيين فإنهم قطعوا وقتلوا إلا أن المثلة المروية فيها نسخت واستعير) أو (للغاية والاستثناء في مثل لألزمنك أو تعطيني حقي أي إلى أن تعطيني أو إلا أن تعطيني حقي (وقيل) في أصول الإمام فخر الإسلام (منه) قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا النَّصْرُ إِلَّا مِنْ عِندِ الله العزيز الْحَكِيمِ لِيَقْطَعَ طَرَفَا مِّنَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَوْ يَكْبِتُهُمْ فَيَنقَلِبُوا خَابِينَ لَيْسَ لَكَ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ شَيْءٍ أَوْ يَتُوبَ عَلَيْهِمْ أَوْ يُعَذِّبَهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ ظَلِمُونَ [آل عمران : ١٢٦ - ١٢٧ - ١٢٨] أي حتى يتوب عليهم، ۱۲۷ قال الشيخ ابن الهمام: تقليد الصاحب الكشاف وغيره أنه عطف على يكبتهم فيما قبل، وقوله جل وعلا: لَيْسَ لَكَ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ شَيْءٍ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۲۸] اعتراض بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، وأذعنوا به زعماً منهم أنه أسلم من التكلف وقال المصنف والأول أقرب بحسب المعنى، : ویرى هذا العبد أنه لا يصح ما في الكشاف فإنه آية أخرى نزلت متفرقة عن الأولى وسيقت لغرض آخر فلا ارتباط . روى البخاري في التاريخ والترمذي وأحمد والنسائي والبيهقي في الدلائل عن ابن عمر قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم يوم أحد : اللهم العن أبا سفيان اللهم العن الحارث بن هشام، اللهم العن سهيل بن عمرو، اللهم العن صفوان بن أمية، فنزلت هذه الآية الكريمة ولَيْسَ لَكَ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ شَيْءٍ أَوْ يَتُوبَ عَلَيْهِمْ أَوْ يُعَذِّبَهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ ظَلِمُونَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ۱۲۸] فتيب عليهم كلهم. وروى البخاري والترمذي ومسلم وابن أبي شيبة والبيهقي في الدلائل عن أنس أن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم كسرت رباعيته يوم وشج في وجهه حتى سال الدم على وجهه فقال : كيف يفلح قوم فعلوا هذا بنبيهم وهو يدعوهم إلى ربهم فأنزل الله : لَيْسَ لَكَ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ شَيْءٍ أَوْ يَتُوبَ عَلَيْهِمْ أَوْ يُعَذِّبَهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ ظَلِمُونَ [آل عمران: ۱۲۸] وبعضهم جعلوه معطوفاً على شيء اسم ليس أي ليس لك من الأمر شيء أو توبتهم أو تعذيبهم من قبيل عطف الخاص على العام، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف.
The third article on linguistic principles. And (the statement) 'the Imam may banish or imprison' and this comparison is not known in the Sharia. (Then we said: distributing retributions according to crimes) as we explained by His saying: 'And the recompense of an evil is an evil like it' (42:40). And similarly Abu Yusuf narrated the report and the traditions also testified to it. And the Imam only had a choice in killing and taking and the ruling of the generous verse is not overridden by this verse and by the story of the Urayniyyun, for they cut and killed, except that the reported mutilation was abrogated. And 'aw' is used for ghayah (end) and istithna (exception) as in 'I will surely accompany you or you give me my right' i.e., until you give me my right or except that you give me my right. And it was said in the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam: from it is His saying: 'And victory is only from Allah, the Mighty, the Wise, that He might cut off a part of those who disbelieved or suppress them so that they turn back disappointed. Not for you is the decision whatsoever, or that He turns in mercy to them or punishes them, for indeed they are wrongdoers' (3:126-128) i.e., until He turns to them. 127. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: The author of al-Kashshaf and others followed that it is an 'atf on 'yakbitahum' in what precedes, and His saying 'Not for you is the decision' is an interpolation between the conjoined and the conjoined to, and they submitted to it claiming that it is safer from difficulty. And the author said the first is closer in meaning. And this servant sees that what is in al-Kashshaf is not correct, for it is another verse revealed separately from the first and was brought for another purpose, so there is no connection. Al-Bukhari narrated in al-Tarikh and al-Tirmidhi, Ahmad, al-Nasa'i, and al-Bayhaqi in al-Dala'il from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah said on the day of Uhud: 'O Allah curse Abu Sufyan, O Allah curse al-Harith ibn Hisham, O Allah curse Suhayl ibn Amr, O Allah curse Safwan ibn Umayyah.' Then this noble verse was revealed: 'Not for you is the decision whatsoever, or that He turns in mercy to them or punishes them, for indeed they are wrongdoers.' And He turned in mercy to all of them. And al-Bukhari, al-Tirmidhi, Muslim, Ibn Abi Shayba, and al-Bayhaqi narrated from Anas that the Prophet's molar was broken and his face was wounded until blood flowed on his face, and he said: 'How can a people who do this to their Prophet succeed while he calls them to their Lord?' Then Allah revealed: 'Not for you is the decision whatsoever, or that He turns in mercy to them or punishes them.' And some made it conjoined to 'shay' the noun of 'laysa', i.e., 'Not for you is the decision or their repentance or their punishment' as a type of conjoining the specific to the general, and the difficulty in it is not hidden.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the particle 'أو' in a Qur'anic verse, specifically within the context of usul al-fiqh. The author begins by noting that the ruling that an Imam may banish or imprison criminals is not directly attested in the Shari'a, but is derived from the principle of distributing retributions according to crimes, citing verse 42:40. He then notes that Imam Abu Yusuf narrated a tradition supporting this view, and that the Imam (Abu Hanifa) had a choice between killing and taking (booty) in certain situations, referencing the story of the Urayniyyun. The main focus is on the usage of 'أو' to denote either ghayah (end/goal) or istithna (exception), as in the example 'لألزمنك أو تعطيني حقي' meaning 'I will accompany you until you give me my right' or 'except if you give me my right'. The discussion then shifts to the interpretation of verse 3:126-128, particularly the phrase 'أو يتوب عليهم'. Some scholars, like the author of al-Kashshaf, consider 'أو' here as a conjunctive (عطف) meaning 'or', with the intervening verse as an objection (اعتراض). The author of the Matn (likely Ibn al-Humam) favors the view that 'أو' means 'until' (حتى), making the meaning 'until He turns to them'. He supports this by citing traditions showing the context of the Prophet's supplication against polytheists and the subsequent revelation indicating that some of them repented. He disagrees with the interpretation that 'أو' conjoins 'thing' (shay') in the predicate, as that would be forced. This is a classic Hanafi usul approach: analyzing the particle to derive the correct legal implication, preferring the meaning that aligns with the context and avoiding arbitrary grammatical structures.

Key Terms
أو (aw): Particle used for ghayah (end/goal) or istithna (exception), not limited to disjunction.
غاية (ghayah): The end point or ultimate goal of an action or ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation presented in the lesson, what does 'أو' mean in the verse 'ليس لك من الأمر شيء أو يتوب عليهم'?
A) It means 'or', giving two options: repentance or punishment.
B) It means 'until', making the meaning 'until He turns to them'.
C) It means 'except', excluding repentance.
D) It means 'and', conjoining repentance to punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul method uses contextual evidence, including sabab al-nuzul and the Sunnah, to determine that the particle 'أو' in Q.3:128 indicates a temporal end (ghayah) rather than simple disjunction.

The Particle 'إلى' and Inclusion of the Endpoint (Ghayah): Hanafi Tafsil
Usul dispute on inclusion of ghayah after 'ila'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi tafsil regarding the inclusion of the ghayah after 'ila', distinguish between ghayat al-isqat and ghayat al-madd, and apply the principle to examples like khiyar and rental.

Core Idea
The endpoint is included if the preceding clause would naturally encompass it; otherwise it is not.

The particle 'ila' raises a key usul question: does the limit (ghayah) after it enter under the rule of what precedes it? The Hanafi school adopts a detailed view (tafsil) that depends on whether the preceding expression, without 'ila', would have covered the limit. This lesson unpacks the tafsil, its basis in sunna, and its application in contracts like khiyar and rental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general views on ghayah inclusion
Some hold that the endpoint is always included (as with 'hatta'), others that it is never included. The Hanafi tafsil occupies a middle ground.
The most famous view for 'ila' is non-inclusion, but the tafsil refines this.
2
Understand the two types of ghayah
Ghayat al-isqat: if the preceding expression would have encompassed the limit without 'ila', then the limit is included (e.g., washing to the elbows). Ghayat al-madd: if not, the limit is excluded (e.g., fasting until night).
The distinction is based on the logical scope of the preceding clause.
3
Examine the proof from sunna
The Prophet washed his forearms when teaching wudu, demonstrating that the endpoint (elbows) is included. This is not a default of 'ila' but by explicit sunna.
This sunna is the evidence for inclusion in that specific case.
4
Apply the tafsil to practical cases
In rental until Ramadan, the endpoint is ghayat al-madd (since rental can be for any term), so Ramadan does not enter. In khiyar, the endpoint is also ghayat al-madd (perpetual khiyar invalid), so it enters to make the contract valid.
The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the default is non-inclusion, but exceptions exist based on evidence.
5
Consider the objection and response
Qadi Abu Zaid objected that the tafsil presumes a pre-existing general ruling, but the response clarifies that it is about logical implication, not a prior ruling.
The tafsil is about the natural scope of the expression, not a ruling that is then dropped.

من الدخول وعدمه لكن الأشهر في حتى مذهب الدخول وفي إلى مذهب عدمه، والتفصيل بتناول الصدر لولا الغاية لها كالمرافق فإنه لولاه لتناول وجوب الغسل للمرافق بل لما بعدها، ولذا فهم بعض الصحابة في التيمم اليد كله إلى الإبط، كما حكي في الكشف ناقلاً عن المبسوط فيدخل الغاية حينئذ في الحكم لأنه كان داخلاً فلا يخرج ويسمى هذا غاية الإسقاط و التفصيل بعدمه أي عدم تناول الصدر لولا الغاية كالليل فإنه لولاه لما دخل المبسوط : قال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : لا تدخل الغاية في مدة الخيار لأنها جعلت غاية والأصل إن الغاية لا تدخل في الصدر إلا بدليل ، ولهذا سميت غاية، لأن الحكم ينتهي إليها، دل عليه الصوم إلى الليل والأكل إلى الفجر، ولهذا لو آجر إلى رمضان أو باع بأجل إلى رمضان أو حلف لا أكلمه إلى رمضان لم يدخل رمضان تحت الجمل لأنه غاية ولا يلزم علينا المرافق فإنها دخلت تحت الجملة، لأن ذلك ثبت بالسنة، فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه وسلم حين علم الوضوء الذي لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به غسل المرافق، هكذا حكى الحاكي الوضوء انتهى ؛ وهذا يدل دلالة واضحة أنهما اختارا عدم الدخول، وأما الأمثلة التي أورداها لعدم الدخول، ففيها الغاية غاية المد إلا مسألة الحلف فإن فيها تفصيلاً، فإن الليل غير داخل في حكم إتمام الصوم ذكر أو لم يذكر، وكذا الفجر في قوله تعالى: (وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا) [البقرة: ١٨٧] فإن صيغة الأمر لا توجب التكرار فإنه لو لم يذكر الغاية لا يتناول الأمر إياها، وكذا في الإجازة فإنها تمليك المنفعة وهي تصدق بتمليك المنفعة ساعة، وكذا الأجل في ثمن المبيع، لأنه تأخير عن وقت وجوبه وهو يصدق بالتأخير ساعة، فهذه الغايات لمد الأحكام المغياة بها، فلا يلزم من عدم الدخول فيه عدمه في غايات الإسقاطات، ولا يلزم منه أن يدخل في الخيار، فإن الغاية فيه غاية المد عنده لأن الخيار المؤبد غير صحيح، فلا عندهما وإلى ثلاثة أيام عنده وأما مسألة الحلف فالغاية تدخل فيه في رواية الحسن فلا تصلح حجة هذا واعترض القاضي الإمام أبو زيد على التفصيل بأن الكلام إذا اقترن في آخره غاية أو شرط يتوقف عليه ويستفاد من الاعتماد على رواية الأصل دون رواية الحسن انتهى منقولاً منه في الكشف، والجواب عنه أنه ليس حاصل التفصيل أن هناك حكماً عاماً أو خاصاً مفاداً من أول الكلام ثم الغاية أسقطه أو مده حتى يرد عليه ذلك بل المقصود أن الغاية لو كانت بحيث لو لم يذكر ويلفظ بما قبله أفاد شمول الحكم الغاية وما بعدها وسميت هذه الغاية غاية إسقاط لا أن هناك إسقاط حكم موجود، وإن كانت بحيث لو لم يذكر لم يشمل حكم ما قبله لها لم يدخل وتسمى غاية مد
Issue: Concerning 'ila' for ending the ruling of what precedes it to what follows it, which is the endpoint (ghayah). Regarding the inclusion of what follows it into what precedes it, there are schools (madhahib) like 'hatta', i.e., as in 'hatta' there are schools of inclusion and non-inclusion. Inclusion applies if what follows is of the same genus as what precedes; otherwise, non-inclusion; and there is also non-indication of either inclusion or non-inclusion. However, the more famous view for 'hatta' is inclusion, and for 'ila' is non-inclusion. The detailed view is based on whether the beginning (sadr) would encompass [the goal] without the endpoint. For example, the forearms: without the endpoint (ila), the obligation of washing would encompass the forearms and beyond. Hence some Companions understood in tayammum: 'the whole hand up to the armpit,' as mentioned in al-Kashf quoting al-Mabsut. In that case, the endpoint enters the ruling because it would have been included; it does not exit. This is called 'ghayat al-isqat' (endpoint of dropping). The other case is non-inclusion, i.e., the beginning would not encompass without the endpoint, like the night: without it, it would not enter fasting because fasting is abstention in the daytime. So it does not enter the ruling because it was outside; it remains so. This is called 'ghayat al-madd' (endpoint of extension). This detail is supported by agreement of most jurists and linguists. The author of al-Kashf, quoting al-Mabsut, said: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: The endpoint does not enter the period of option (khiyar) because it was made an endpoint, and the principle is that the endpoint does not enter the beginning unless there is evidence. For this reason it is called 'ghayah' because the ruling terminates at it. Evidence: fasting until night, eating until dawn. Hence if one rents until Ramadan or sells with a term until Ramadan or swears not to speak to him until Ramadan, Ramadan does not enter under the sentence because it is an endpoint. And we are not bound by the forearms because they entered under the sentence due to the sunna: when the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions and wives) taught the ablution without which Allah does not accept prayer, he washed the forearms. Thus the narrator narrated the ablution. This clearly indicates that they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) chose non-inclusion. As for the examples they gave for non-inclusion, in them the endpoint is an endpoint of extension. Except the case of oath: there is a detail. For the night does not enter the ruling of completing the fast whether mentioned or not. Similarly, dawn in His saying: (and eat and drink) [2:187] because the imperative form does not require repetition; if the endpoint were not mentioned, the command would not encompass it. Similarly in rental (ijarah): it is a transfer of usufruct, which is realized by transferring usufruct for an hour. Similarly the term in the price of a sold item: it is a delay from the time of obligation, realized by delay for an hour. These endpoints are for extending the rulings they delimit. Therefore, non-inclusion in these does not necessitate non-inclusion in endpoints of dropping. Nor does it necessitate inclusion in khiyar, because the endpoint there is an endpoint of extension according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), since perpetual khiyar is invalid. Hence the absolute is not restricted by the endpoint except for an unspecified hour. So mentioning the endpoint is to extend the khiyar to that period. Hence the contract becomes void with khiyar unanimously because it causes uncertainty leading to dispute. Therefore, extension to the endpoint is necessary absolutely according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and up to three days according to him (Abu Hanifa). As for the case of oath, the endpoint enters according to the narration of al-Hasan, so it is not valid as evidence. Qadi Imam Abu Zaid objected to the detail: if speech is accompanied at its end by an endpoint or condition, the ruling depends on it and the whole yields the delimited or conditioned ruling. So there is no general ruling in the beginning that the endpoint then drops, nor a non-general ruling that the endpoint extends. Thus the aforementioned detail does not stand. The truth is what they said. How so? And the case of oath is binding according to Imam Abu Hanifa's way, since reliance is on the original narration, not al-Hasan's narration. End quote from al-Kashf. The response to this is: the point of the detail is not that there is a general or specific ruling conveyed from the start of speech that the endpoint then drops or extends such that this objection applies. Rather, the intent is: if the endpoint is such that, if it were not mentioned and only what precedes it were uttered, the ruling would be understood to encompass the endpoint and beyond, then it is called 'ghayat al-isqat'—not that there is a pre-existing ruling that is dropped. And if it is such that, if not mentioned, the ruling would not encompass it, then it does not enter and is called 'ghayat al-madd' (endpoint of extension).
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This is a major usul discussion on the particle 'ila' and whether the limit (ghayah) is included in the ruling of what precedes it. The text first compares 'hatta' (until) where inclusion is more famous, versus 'ila' where non-inclusion is famous. However, a detailed view (tafsil) is presented: if the preceding clause would naturally encompass the limit without 'ila' (like 'wash your hands to the elbows' where without 'ila' the obligation would extend beyond), then the limit is included—this is 'ghayat al-isqat' (endpoint of dropping). If not (like 'fast until night' where fasting is only daytime), then the limit is not included—'ghayat al-madd' (endpoint of extension). This detail is supported by the fatwa of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who consistently rule that the endpoint does not enter unless there is a specific evidence (like the sunna for washing elbows in wudu). They give examples: renting until Ramadan, selling with term until Ramadan, oath not to speak until Ramadan—Ramadan does not enter. However, the case of khiyar (option) is special: the endpoint here is for extension because perpetual khiyar is invalid, so the endpoint must be included to make the khiyar valid (up to three days according to Abu Hanifa, and up to the specified time according to the two). The text then records an objection by Qadi Imam Abu Zaid al-Dabusi, who argues that the tafsil is flawed because the ruling is derived from the whole expression, not from a general then dropped. The author responds that the tafsil is not about a pre-existing ruling but about whether the endpoint would logically be included if 'ila' were absent. The case of oath is discussed: al-Hasan's narration says the endpoint enters, but the relied-upon view is per Abu Hanifa that it does not (original narration). This is a characteristic example of Hanafi istihsan and precision in differentiating between types of ghayah.

Key Terms
غاية (ghayah): The endpoint or limit mentioned after a particle like 'ila' up to which a ruling applies.
غاية الإسقاط (ghayat al-isqat): An endpoint that, had it not been mentioned, the ruling would have included it; so when mentioned, it is included as if dropping an extension.
غاية المد (ghayat al-madd): An endpoint that extends the ruling up to it; without it, the ruling would not have covered it, so it is not included.
خيار (khiyar): Option in a contract; the endpoint in khiyar is a ghayat al-madd because perpetual khiyar is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi tafsil, which of the following is an example of ghayat al-isqat?
A) Fasting until night
B) Washing hands to the elbows in wudu
C) Eating until dawn during Ramadan
D) Renting a house until Ramadan

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi tafsil on 'ila' provides a nuanced rule: the endpoint is included only when the preceding expression would naturally cover it, otherwise not.

Inclusion of the Elbow in Wudu: Analysis of 'إلى'
Expansion probe on the inclusion of the endpoint in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principles to determine whether the preposition 'إلى' includes the endpoint in the wudu verse and evaluate the opposing arguments.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school argues that 'إلى' in the wudu verse includes the elbow, based on logical necessity and prophetic practice.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut examines the linguistic ambiguity of the preposition 'إلى' in the wudu verse. The commentator presents multiple arguments, ultimately favoring inclusion based on the necessity of washing the overlapping part and the Prophet's practice. We will analyze the reasoning and see how Hanafi usul handles such textual ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic issue
The verse commands washing hands 'to the elbows' – does 'to' include or exclude the elbows? This is the core dispute.
2
Examine the opponent's argument
The opponent claims 'إلى' excludes the endpoint, leaving the elbow unwashed by default (original absence).
3
Analyze the Hanafi first response
Washing to the elbow necessitates washing the adjacent part due to overlapping bones – logical necessity.
4
Consider the precaution argument
Some argue for including out of precaution, but this is refuted because precaution applies only when obligation is the default, which is disputed here.
5
Evaluate prophetic practice
The constant practice of the Prophet washing his elbows creates a semblance of obligation, and the hadith of Imam Muhammad confirms it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غسل اليد إلى المرفق فلا يلزم غسل جزء الذراع وجزء العضد المتلقيين فساقط، لأنه لا يخفى عليك أن غسل اليد إلى المرفق لا يتم إلا بغسل الجزء منه الملاصق للمرفق، وهناك العظمان متشابكان قطعاً، فلا يتم غسل اليد إلى المرافق إلا بغسله فافهم، ومنها: ما قيل الغاية قد تدخل وقد لا تدخل، فوقع الشك في الدخول وعدمه، فصار الآية مجملة، وفعله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام مبيناً، وهذا إنما يتم لو ثبت اشتراك إلى الدخول وعدمه، وهو ممنوع عند الخصم، بل يقول : إلى لا يدل على شيء، فبقي المرفق على العدم الأصلي، اللهم إلا أن يستعان بكونه مقدمة الواجب. ومنها أنها تارة تدخل وتارة لا فتدخل احتياطاً، وفيه أن الاحتياط إنما يوجب لو كان الأصل الوجوب، كصوم ثلاثين من الشهر المبارك، لا كصوم الشك، وأصالة الوجوب ههنا ممنوعة، بل هو أول المسألة، ثم إن حديث الاحتياط لا يوجب كون الحكم مستفاداً من الشرع والكلام فيه فافهم، ومن هنا اندفع أيضاً ما في «التحرير» الأقرب أن يقال : إن المواظبة على غسل المرافق قد ثبت فأورث شبهة الإيجاب، فأوجبنا احتياطاً، وأيضاً منقوض بأكثر السنن، ثم ثبوت المواظبة أيضاً مشكل، فإنه لم ينقل إلا بكلمة إلى، وهي توجب عدم الدخول عنده، وما روى الإمام محمد أنه دار الماء على المرفق فغسله يوجب المواظبة فافهم هذا كله ما عندي وهذا المبحث على هذا التفصيل لعله من خصائص هذا الكتاب، ولعل الله يحدث بعد ذلك أمراً.
The washing of the hand to the elbow does not require washing the part of the forearm and the part of the upper arm that meet, so it is dropped, because it is not hidden from you that washing the hand to the elbow is only completed by washing the part adjacent to the elbow, and there the two bones are intertwined without doubt, so washing the hand to the elbows is not completed except by washing it, so understand. And among the [arguments]: it is said that the endpoint may be included and may not be included, so doubt occurred regarding its inclusion and non-inclusion, so the verse became ambiguous, and his action, peace be upon him, and his companions, clarifying. This is only complete if it is established that 'إلى' is shared between inclusion and non-inclusion, and this is denied by the opponent, but rather he says: 'إلى' does not indicate anything, so the elbow remains on the original absence. Unless one seeks help by it being a prerequisite of the obligatory. And among them: sometimes it includes and sometimes not, so it includes out of precaution, and in it is that precaution only necessitates if the original is obligation, like fasting thirty days of the blessed month, not like fasting the doubtful day, and the original obligation here is denied, but rather it is the beginning of the issue. Then the hadith of precaution does not necessitate that the ruling is derived from the Law, and the discussion is about that, so understand. And from here is also refuted what is in al-Tahrir: the nearest is to say that the adherence to washing the elbows has been confirmed, so it created a semblance of obligation, so we made it obligatory out of precaution. Also it is refuted by most supererogatory acts. Then the confirmation of adherence is also problematic, for it is not reported except with the word 'إلى', and it necessitates non-inclusion according to him, and what Imam Muhammad narrated that water flowed over the elbow and he washed it necessitates adherence, so understand all this from me, and this discussion in this detail is perhaps from the specific characteristics of this book, and perhaps God will bring about after that a matter.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the ruling of washing the elbow in wudu, arising from the verse 'wash your hands to the elbows' (Quran 5:6). The commentator analyzes whether the preposition 'إلى' includes the endpoint (the elbow) or not. He presents multiple arguments from the Hanafi perspective. First, he notes that washing to the elbow necessarily requires washing the part adjacent to the elbow due to the overlap of bones. Second, he discusses the ambiguity of 'إلى' and claims that it does not indicate inclusion, so the elbow remains unwashed originally. Some argue for precaution, but the commentator refutes that by noting that precaution only applies when obligation is the default, which is not the case here. He also mentions that the constant practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) of washing the elbow creates a semblance of obligation, but that is not strong enough. The passage ends by stating that this detailed discussion is unique to this book.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): A preposition meaning 'to' or 'until', the endpoint is disputed

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Consensus of scholars excludes it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses prophetic practice as a bayan to clarify the ambiguous preposition 'إلى', thereby including the elbow in the obligation of wudu.

Do General Commands Include Slaves? The Default Presumption
Dispute whether general lines of address include slaves as a legal presumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on whether general commands include slaves and apply the principle of linguistic generality versus legal custom to derive the default presumption for a given command.

Core Idea
Linguistic generality includes slaves; exclusion requires proof, not presumption.

When Allah or His Messenger issues a general command, such as "Establish prayer" or "Do not steal," does that command automatically include slaves? The answer is not obvious, because slaves have limited legal capacity. In this passage, the Hanafi usuli analyzes three views on the default presumption: inclusion, non-inclusion, and a middle distinction between the rights of Allah and rights of people. Understanding this dispute helps us derive rulings from general texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Locate the three views: the majority (al-akthar) holds that general commands include slaves by default. A second view (qīla) holds the opposite: non-inclusion is the default. Abu Bakr al-Jassas distinguishes between rights of Allah (inclusion) and rights of people (non-inclusion).
These positions exist within the Hanafi school itself, though the majority view is relied upon.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
The majority argues that the linguistic form (ṣīghah) of a command indicates generality and inclusion. Therefore, unless there is a specific proof (dalīl) excluding slaves, they are included. Custom (urf) can override linguistic implication, but that requires evidence.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that amm (general) is authoritative until specified (takhsīs).
3
Evaluate the custom (urf) argument
Opponents say that the Shari'ah customarily excludes slaves, citing examples like Jihad and Hajj. The majority responds that those exclusions are based on specific proofs, not a general custom; hence no abrogation is needed, and no custom is established.
The text includes a refutation (radd) of the deniers' argument that exclusion would require abrogation if the general form originally included them.
4
Apply the inductive survey
The author suggests an inductive method (istiqrā'): most commands regarding rights of Allah (e.g., prayer, fasting, prohibitions) include slaves, except where there is clear harm to the master. In contrast, most commands regarding rights of people (e.g., contracts, transactions) exclude slaves. This supports Al-Jassas's distinction.
This inductive approach shows that custom can be inferred from the preponderance of rulings, even if not explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
الخطاب الذي يعم العبيد لغة هل يتناولهم شرعاً أو لا، قال الأكثر : نعم يتناولهم فيعم الحكم لهم (وقيل : لا يتناولهم فلا يعمهم الحكم (وقال الشيخ أبو بكر الجصاص الرازي الحنفي رحمه الله تعالى : يتناولهم في حقوق الله تعالى فقط لا في حقوق العباد، تحرير محل النزاع أنه لا شك أن من الخطابات ما يتناول الكل من الأحرار والعبيد بالاتفاق، ومنها ما يختص الأحرار فقط بالإجماع، وإنما النزاع في أن الظاهر شرعاً ما هو، فعند الأكثر الظاهر التناول كما كان لغة فيحتاج في عدمه إلى دليل آخر، وقيل: الظاهر عدم التناول، فيحتاج في شمول الحكم والتناول إلى دليل زائد وعند الشيخ أبي بكر التفصيل بحقوق الله تعالى وحقوق العباد (لنا) الصيغة كانت للعموم والتناول وما عرف عرف طار على اللغة يخرجها عن مقتضاها لغة وإن دل دليل على الخروج أي خروج العبيد عن بعض الخطابات كالجهاد والحج إلى غير ذلك ولا يلزم منه العرف هذا وفيه إشارة إلى رد استدلال النافي بأن حكم الجهاد ونحوه لا يتناول العبيد، فلو لم يكن خارجاً عن الخطاب لزم النسخ، وجه الرد أنها خارجة فلا نسخ، لكن لدليل فلا يلزم العرف فافهم، ولك أن تقول : استقرى الأحكام الشرعية فوجد أكثرها المتعلق بحقوق الله تعالى شاملة لهم إلا ما فيه ضرر بين للمولى كالجمعة والحج والجهاد قبل النفير العام، وأما سائر النواهي كالزنا والشتم والكفر والقتل والغصب فشاملة قطعاً، فلا يمكن فيها ادعاء العرف من أحد، وأما حقوق العباد فأكثرها مختصة بالأحرار، وقلما يدخل فيها العبيد فلا يبعد أن يدعي فيها العرف ويقال : إن عرف الشارع حاكم بعدم دخولهم فيها إلا بالدليل فإن الأكثر عدم الدخول والظن تابع للأغلب فكلما ورد الخطاب الشرعي المتعلق بحقوق العباد يتسارع الذهن إلى اختصاصه بالأحرار، وهذا معنى العرف فقوله : وما عرف عرف طار مطلقاً ممنوع ، النافون للدخول (قالوا : منافع العبد مملوك
Issue: The line of address that generally includes slaves linguistically — does it include them legally or not? The majority said: Yes, it includes them and the ruling applies to them. It was said: It does not include them, so the ruling does not apply to them. And Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi al-Hanafi said: It includes them in the rights of Allah only, not in the rights of people. Clarification of the point of dispute: There is no doubt that some lines of address include all, free and slave, by agreement, and some are specific to free only by consensus. The dispute is only about what is the legal presumption. According to the majority, the presumption is inclusion as it was linguistically, so non-inclusion requires another proof. It was said: The presumption is non-inclusion, so inclusion of the ruling and address requires an additional proof. And according to Abu Bakr, there is a distinction between rights of Allah and rights of people. Our proof: The formula was for generality and inclusion, and what custom has become custom overrides, removing it from its linguistic implication. If there is a proof for exclusion — i.e., exclusion of slaves from some lines of address like jihad and Hajj, etc. — that does not necessitate a general custom. This contains an indication to refute the denier's argument that the ruling of Jihad and similar does not include slaves; if they were not excluded from the address, abrogation would be necessitated. The response is that they are excluded, so no abrogation, but by a proof, so no custom is necessitated. So understand. And you may say: He investigated the legal rulings and found that most related to the rights of Allah include them except where there is clear harm to the master, like Jumu'ah, Hajj, and Jihad before general mobilization. As for other prohibitions like adultery, insult, disbelief, murder, and usurpation, they are definitely inclusive; no one can claim custom there. As for rights of people, most are specific to free men, and slaves rarely enter them. So it is not far-fetched to claim custom there and say: The custom of the Lawgiver judges their non-entry except by proof, for the majority is non-entry, and probability follows the majority. So whenever a legal address related to the rights of people comes, the mind hastens to its specificity to free men. This is the meaning of 'custom.' So his statement: 'What custom has become custom overrides' is absolutely rejected. The deniers of entry said: The benefits of the slave are owned.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute (ikhtilāf) regarding whether general commands (like 'Establish prayer' or 'Do not steal') include slaves by default in Islamic law. Three positions are stated: (1) The majority (al-akthar) hold that the default is inclusion, as per the linguistic generality of the command, so excluding slaves requires a specific proof (dalīl). (2) Some (qīla) hold that the default is non-inclusion, so extending a command to slaves requires an extra proof. (3) Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi al-Hanafi distinguishes between the rights of Allah (ḥuqūq Allāh) and rights of people (ḥuqūq al-‘ibād). For the rights of Allah, the default is inclusion; for the rights of people, slaves are generally not included. The text then provides evidence for the majority (lana). It argues that the linguistic form (ṣīgha) indicates generality and inclusion. Custom (‘urf) may override the linguistic implication, but only if there is evidence for exclusion. For example, commands for Jihad and Hajj are excluded for slaves by specific proofs, but that does not establish a general custom of non-inclusion. The author further suggests an inductive approach: most commands regarding rights of Allah cover slaves, except when there is clear harm to the master (like Friday prayer, Hajj, Jihad before general call). Prohibitions like zina, insult, murder, etc., definitely include slaves. For rights of people, most are exclusive to free men, so it is plausible to claim a custom that they are not included without evidence. The passage ends abruptly with the deniers' argument that the benefits of the slave are owned by the master, implying that the slave's lack of full capacity prevents inclusion.

Key Terms
عبيد (‘abīd): Plural of 'abd, meaning slaves. In fiqh, a slave is a human being owned by another with limited legal capacity.
خطاب (khiṭāb): A line of address from the Lawgiver, such as a command or prohibition, directed to the legally responsible (mukallafūn).
عرف (‘urf): Custom or prevailing practice. In usul, it can specify general terms if it is a continuous custom established by the Lawgiver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi's view presented in this passage, do general lines of address include slaves?
A) Yes, always, without exception.
B) No, never, unless there is specific proof.
C) Only in rights of Allah, not in rights of people.
D) Only in rights of people, not in rights of Allah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default is that general commands include slaves linguistically, and exclusion requires a specific proof; custom must be established pervasively, not inferred from occasional exceptions.

The Condition of Connection for Exception (Ittisal al-Istithna)
Ruling on the necessity of exception being connected to speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that an exception must be connected to the beginning of speech and identify customary interruptions that break it.

Core Idea
An exception must be immediately connected; turning to another speech invalidates it.

The validity of an exception (istithna) depends on it being immediately connected to the statement it qualifies. This lesson clarifies what counts as connection and what breaks it according to Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Recognize that 'istithna' is a linguistic construction that excludes an item from a general statement using particles like 'illa'.
2
State the condition
The exception must be connected (ittisal) to the beginning of the speech. Connection is assessed customarily (urfan).
3
Distinguish interruptions
Minor interruptions like coughing are excused, but turning to another speech is considered abandonment and invalidates the exception.
The break must involve starting a new topic or speech act.
4
Apply the rule
If a person says 'I have ten' and then after a long pause adds 'except three', the exception is invalid because it is not connected.
Hanafi view: any significant delay, especially intentional, breaks connection.

The third article: Linguistic Principles. Issue: (The condition of exception: connection, i.e., its connection to the beginning of the speech. And (even if) the connection is (customarily) considered connected. So (exception) is not harmed by interruption due to a cough, for example, or other excuses. But interruption by engaging in another speech harms, as it is considered abandonment and turning away customarily. And it is narrated from Ibn Abbas regarding its contrary, narrations: in one narration, delay is valid up to a month; in another, up to a year; in another, up to the whole life, as in al-Hashiyah. And due to its being extremely far-fetched, or the innocence of Ibn Abbas from uttering such a far-fetched statement, let alone adopting it as a madhhab, (the author) interprets what is narrated from him as what Imam Ahmad said: delay is valid by intention, by analogy to other (specificrs). This analogy is only valid for one who permits delay of the specifier, and it is established by analogy to other connected things, and this is even more egregious. If you say: then the condition should also be delayed by intention, I say: it is not refuted by the condition as in al-Minhaj, because they say the condition is like the exception (so no agreement). So there is no obligation. And it is said that separation is valid in exception (specifically in the Quran, not elsewhere, due to what is narrated in His statement: 'Not equal are the believers who sit [at home], other than those with disability, and those who strive in the cause of Allah' [An-Nisa 4:95] and 'other than those with disability' was not revealed initially, then it was revealed after some time, due to the complaint of Abdullah ibn Umm Maktum and others, may Allah be pleased with them. And it can be rebutted that the meaning of 'those who sit' from the believers is those who sit from those upon whom jihad is obligatory, and that was known from the necessity of religion, for what is understood from sitting is refraining from performing an obligation, and it is not said customarily of a bankrupt that he sat for Hajj and Zakat. So His statement 'other than those with disability' is not a specifier nor an exception, but rather a clarifying statement (bayan taqrir) that occurs as an emphasized circumstantial clause, and its separation is permissible by agreement. So it is not from what we are discussing at all. Reflect. The author said: It appears to be like the statement of al-Abbas: 'except the idhkhir' when the Messenger of Allah, may Allah honor him and grant him peace, prohibited cutting the trees and plants of Mecca. For his statement is connected to something omitted. And it is not hidden from you that then the meaning would be: 'Not equal are the believers who sit absolutely, except those with disability', making it an exclusion from a ruling that was general, which would only be by abrogation, and that is not valid, for it is an informative sentence. Also, the ruling of jihad was not general for those with disability; unless it is said that the first ruling was specific, then this ruling with the exception was revealed as a confirmation of it. Understand. And it is said that delay is valid as long as the session continues; this is the statement of Taj al-Awliya' al-Hasan al-Basri, may Allah sanctify his secret, and Tawus, as in al-Tahrir. Our proofs: First: the consensus of the grammarians on the obligation of connection between the exception and the beginning; for this reason, if someone says 'I have ten' then after a month adds 'except three', it is considered nonsense customarily by consensus, so it cannot be linked to what preceded. Second: if connection were not obligatory, one could not be certain of truth or falsehood in any statement due to the possibility of an exception. For if the general was true in reality, the possibility of an exception remains for the falsehood, otherwise the possibility of truth remains for it. And (one could not be certain of the validity of) contracts like sale and others, and divorces like divorce and others, due to the possibility of a modifying exception. It is narrated that Imam Abu Hanifa repelled the reproach of al-Mansur al-Dawaniqi, the second Abbasid caliph, regarding contradicting his grandfather Ibn Abbas in this issue, for he permits delaying the exception while the Imam forbids it, (by citing) the non-binding nature of the oath of allegiance (bay'a) of the people to him accepting his leadership. And this story indicates that the madhhab...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) on the condition of exception (istithna) in speech: it must be immediately connected (mutassil) to the beginning of the statement. The author explains that connection is required even customarily (urfan), so a brief interruption like a cough does not break it, but turning to another speech does. The Hanafi position is that any delay invalidates the exception. Then the passage presents opposing views: (1) Narrations from Ibn Abbas allowing delay up to a month, a year, or a lifetime—rejected as extremely unlikely and attributed to a misunderstanding. (2) Some allow delay specifically in the Quran, citing the verse on al-Qa'idun; the author rebuts this by showing it is not an exception but a qualifying clause (bayan taqrir). (3) Hasan al-Basri and Tawus allow delay within the same session. The Hanafi proofs are: (a) consensus of grammarians (ijma' al-udaba') that e.g., saying 'I have ten' then after a month adding 'except three' is nonsense; (b) without immediate connection, certainty in statements and contracts would be impossible because a future exception could always change the meaning. The story about Imam Abu Hanifa defending his position against Caliph al-Mansur illustrates the practical application: if exception could be delayed, the oath of allegiance (bay'a) would be non-binding because the ruler could later add an exception. The passage conclusively establishes the Hanafi rule as the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): A linguistic construction that excludes an item from a general statement using an exceptive particle (e.g., 'illa').
الاتصال (al-ittisal): The condition that the exception must be connected to the first part of speech without a significant break.
الانقطاع (al-ingita'): Interruption of connection; a break that nullifies the exception if it involves turning to another speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what constitutes a break that invalidates an exception?
A) A cough or sneeze
B) Turning to another speech
C) Any pause longer than a breath
D) Only intentional silence

Key Takeaway
The exception must be immediately connected to the statement; only a break involving new speech invalidates it.

intermediate
10 min
The Dispute over Delayed Exception and Hanafi Proofs
Dispute over delayed exception and Hanafi proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the opposing views on delayed exception and construct the Hanafi proofs (ijma and necessity) that require immediate connection.

Core Idea
Hanafi proofs: consensus of grammarians and the need for certainty in statements necessitate immediate connection.

While the Hanafi school requires immediate connection, some scholars like Ibn Abbas reportedly allowed delay. This lesson examines these opposing views, the rebuttals, and the two strong proofs that establish the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the opposing views
Narrations from Ibn Abbas allow delay of exception up to a month, a year, or even a lifetime. Other scholars like Hasan al-Basri and Tawus allow delay within the same session.
These views are considered weak in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal of narrations
The narrations are reinterpreted as referring to intention (niyya) by analogy to other specifiers, but this analogy only works for those who permit delayed specification.
The author dismisses the narrations as extremely far-fetched.
3
Analyze the Quranic verse objection
Some argue that verse 4:95 allows delayed exception because 'other than those with disability' was revealed later. The Hanafi rebuttal: 'other than' is not an exception but a clarifying clause (bayan taqrir) that can be separated by agreement.
The verse is interpreted as a qualifying attribute, not an exclusion.
4
Examine the two Hanafi proofs
Proof 1: Consensus of grammarians (ijma' al-udaba') that a delayed exception is nonsense (laghw). Proof 2: Without immediate connection, no statement can be verified as true or false, and contracts become uncertain.
The second proof is based on practical necessity (darura).
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
If delayed exception were allowed, a ruler could later modify an oath of allegiance (bay'a), undermining its binding force.
Imam Abu Hanifa used this argument against Caliph al-Mansur.

Hadith Evidence for Istithnā' al-Akthar
Hanafi proof for istithnā' al-akthar from hadith with refutation of alternative interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi proof from the hadith for istithnā' al-akthar and evaluate alternative interpretations that fail to counter it.

Core Idea
The hadith proves the excepted can be the majority because 'whom Allah feeds are more.'

In the previous lesson, we examined the Quranic proof for excepting the majority. Now we turn to a second proof from the Sunnah: the hadith 'All of you are hungry except whom I have fed' recorded in Sahih Muslim. This hadith, when properly understood, demonstrates that the excepted group can outnumber the general group, supporting the Hanafi position against those who restrict istithnā' to less than half.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary textual evidence
Locate the hadith quoted in the passage: 'All of you are hungry except whom I have fed.' Recognize that this is a general statement (ʿāmm) with an exception (istithnā').
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim, but its chain is not mentioned here; the focus is on its meaning.
2
Recognize the Hanafi interpretation
The Hanafi scholar argues that the excepted (those fed) are more numerous than the hungry, citing the phrase 'whom Allah feeds are more' (or an implied understanding). This is the key basis for permissibility of istithnā' al-akthar.
The passage references a phrase not explicitly in the hadith text; it may be a contextual inference.
3
Evaluate alternative interpretations
The passage lists several alternative ways to read the hadith: literal feeding, spiritual feeding, perpetual hunger, etc. For each, assess whether it would undermine the Hanafi proof or actually support it.
These alternatives are presented as potential objections; the Hanafi response shows they either fail or backfire.
4
Determine which interpretation withstands scrutiny
Conclude that the Hanafi reading—where the excepted are the majority—is the only one that consistently aligns with the apparent meaning and other proofs, thus affirming istithnā' al-akthar.
This step reinforces the usul principle that contextual clues (qarā'in) can establish the predominance of the excepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) لنا (ثانياً) قوله تعالى بلسان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كلكم جائع إلا من أطعمته كما في صحيح مسلم) وفيه تعريض على من جعله مثلاً مشهوراً (ومن يطعمه الله أكثر) فالمستثنى أكثر، وعلى ما قررنا يرد ما قيل إن الخطاب للحاضرين والمعنى : كلكم جائع إلا من أطعمه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم، ومن أطعمه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يجوز أن يكون أقل مع أنه إن أريد إطعامه الظاهري، فكون الذين لم يطعمهم جائعين غير ظاهر وإن أريد الإطعام بمدده الباطني بناء على أن كل ما يصل من الفيوضات الدنيوية والدينية فهو من مدده الباطني، فغير المطعم قليل بل لا يكاد يوجد فافهم، وينبغي أن يعلم أن الحديث يحتمل معاني منها كلكم جائع دائماً إلا أطعمته ليس بجائع دائماً، بل مطعم في وقت من الأوقات، وعلى هذا فالظاهر أنه دليل لما اختاره مشايخنا، فإنه لم يبق أحد موصوف بدوام الجوع إلا في الإمكان والفرض، ومنها أن كلكم جائع في وقت إلا من أطعمته، فإنه ليس بجائع في وقت أصلاً، وعلى هذا لا يتم الدليل، فإن المطعم دائماً أقل القليل بل لا يكاد يوجد. لا من ، ومنها : أن كلكم جائع في نفسه لكن من أطعمته اندفع جوعه وعلى هذا الاستثناء منقطع فليس مما نحن فيه . ومنها : أن كلكم جائع للعلوم والمعارف إلا من أطعمته طعاماً روحانياً من المعارف والعلوم، وعلى هذا أيضاً لا يتم الاستدلال، فإن العلماء الكاملين وهم الأولياء الكرام أقل من العامة . ومنها أن الاستثناء مفرغ من عموم الأحوال أي: كلكم جائع في كل حال إلا حال إطعامه، وهذا بعيد من اللفظ قريب المعنى، لكن لا يتم حينئذ أيضاً، فإن أحوال الإطعام أقل من سائر الأحوال
And we have a second proof: the saying of Allah by the tongue of His Messenger, peace be upon him: 'All of you are hungry except whom I have fed' as in Sahih Muslim. In it is a reference to those who made it a famous proverb. 'And whom Allah feeds are more' so the excepted are more. According to what we have established, the objection that the address is to those present and the meaning is 'All of you are hungry except whom the Messenger fed' is refuted, and those whom the Messenger fed may be fewer, although if literal feeding is intended, then it is not apparent that those not fed are hungry; and if spiritual feeding is intended based on the idea that all worldly and religious graces come from his spiritual support, then those not fed are few, almost nonexistent, so understand. And it should be known that the hadith may bear meanings: one, 'All of you are perpetually hungry except whom I fed is not perpetually hungry but fed at a time,' and on this it is evidence for what our scholars chose, for no one remains described as perpetually hungry except in possibility and supposition. Another: 'All of you are hungry at a time except whom I fed, he is not hungry at any time,' and on this the proof is not complete, for the perpetually fed are very few, almost nonexistent. Another: 'All of you are hungry in themselves but whom I fed, his hunger is repelled,' and on this the exception is disconnected, so it is not our subject. Another: 'All of you are hungry for knowledge and sciences except whom I fed spiritual food of knowledge and sciences,' and on this also the deduction is not complete, for the complete scholars who are the noble saints are fewer than the common people. Another: the exception is empty of the generality of states, i.e., 'All of you are hungry in every state except the state of feeding him,' and this is far from the wording but close in meaning, but even then it is not complete, for the states of feeding are fewer than other states.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the second Hanafi proof for the permissibility of excepting the majority (istithna' al-akthar) from the hadith. The author quotes the hadith 'All of you are hungry except whom I have fed' from Sahih Muslim. He argues that the excepted group (those fed) are more than the hungry, as indicated by the phrase 'whom Allah feeds are more.' He then refutes alternative interpretations: if the address is to those present, or if literal feeding is meant, or if spiritual feeding is meant, or if the hadith bears other meanings, he shows that these interpretations either do not support the opponent or actually support the Hanafi position. The commentary then lists several possible meanings of the hadith, each time concluding that the Hanafi interpretation (that the excepted are more) is the most plausible or that the alternative fails to provide a counter-proof. The overall point is to defend the validity of excepting the majority as a linguistic and legal construction.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): A linguistic construction that excludes something from a general statement.
استثناء الأكثر (istithnā' al-akthar): Excepting the majority, i.e., the excepted group is larger than the general group.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): Specification; limiting a general (ʿāmm) statement by an exception or other evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence used in this passage to support the permissibility of excepting the majority?
A) Consensus of jurists
B) Analogy with conditional statements
C) A hadith from Sahih Muslim
D) A verse from the Quran

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'All of you are hungry except whom I have fed' provides a valid proof that the excepted can be the majority, supporting the Hanafi position on istithnā' al-akthar.

15 min
Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Qadhf Verse Interpretation
Usuli debate on the scope of an exception clause in a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi linguistic arguments for why the exception in the qadhf verse does not apply to the rejection of testimony, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by applying Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
The exception clause attaches only to the last declarative sentence, not to the imperatives—grammar determines the scope of hadd.

This passage delves into one of the most intricate usul disputes between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the interpretation of Surah An-Nur, verse 4. At stake is whether the divine punishment for false accusation (qadhf) includes both flogging and the eternal rejection of testimony, or whether the latter is a separate ruling that can be lifted by repentance. The Hanafi position, as defended by Imam Fakhr al-Islam, relies on a rigorous grammatical analysis of conjunction (atf), exception (istithna), and address (khitab). By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate and apply these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Locate the exception clause ('illa alladhina tabu') in the verse and note which phrases it might logically attach to according to the apparent conjunction.
The verse: '...fajliduhum... wa la taqbalu... illa alladhina tabu... wa ula'ika hum al-fasiqun'.
2
Understand the Hanafi claim
The Hanafis argue that the exception attaches only to the last declarative sentence ('wa ula'ika hum al-fasiqun'), not to the preceding imperatives.
This is based on grammatical disconnection.
3
Analyze the three Hanafi proofs
Work through each proof: (a) the exception should be conjoined to a similar sentence type (declarative to declarative), (b) the imperatives are insha'iyya while the last is khabariyya, so conjunction is not preferred, (c) the addressee of the imperatives is plural (judges) while the last uses a singular 'ka' (the Prophet), preventing direct conjunction.
These proofs are cumulative.
4
Evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument and Hanafi response
The Shafi'is rely on the apparent conjunction (waw) connecting all parts. The Hanafis respond that indicators override the conjunction and that the Shafi'i view would require accepting testimony after pardon, which they do not hold.
This step applies the usul principle of tarjih (preference) when textual indicators conflict.

أيضاً، فتأمل (قيل الممتنع إنما هو عطف الخبرية على الإنشائية فيما لا محل لها من الإعراب، وههنا لها أي للإنشائية (محل) من الإعراب لأنها خبر من المبتدأ، فلا يمتنع عطف هذه الاسمية عليها وهذا إنما يرد لو
