ثُمَّ قَسَتْ قُلُوبُكُم مِّنْ بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ ﴾ [البقرة : 74] وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَقُلْنَا اضْرِبُوهُ بِبَعْضِهَا كَذَلِكَ يُحْيِ اللَّهُ الْمَوْتَى ﴾ [البقرة: 73] وغير ذلك ساقط، فإن الكلام في الاستعمال الحقيقي ولا شك أن الكاف موضوع لإفراد المخاطب بإطباق أهل النحو ، كيف ولولاه لم يكن للتثنية والجمع فائدة، وفيما استشهد به يجوز أن يكون قوله من بعد ذلك خطاباً لغير بني إسرائيل على طريقة الالتفات إشعاراً بأنهم غير قابلين للخطاب، وينبغي أن يخاطب غيرهم بإعلام حالهم، وقوله: ﴿ كَذَلِكَ يُحْيِ اللَّهُ الْمَوْتَى [البقرة: 73] غير داخل تحت المقول والمعنى : فقلنا اضربوه ببعض منها فضربوا كذلك فحيي، الله الموتى يا من صلح للاعتبار والتذكر وعلى هذا فقس، وبعد التنزل لا يضر استعماله في الجمع مجازاً، كما يستعمل ضمير المتكلم مع الغير في الواحد، وههنا لو عطف الاسمية على الطلبية يلزم اختلاف المخاطبين لو أبقى الكاف على الحقيقة، والحمل على المجاز خلاف الأصل، فلا يعطف عليه، ولو تنزل عن هذا أيضاً فلا شك في صلوحه مرجحاً فتدبر أقول لو منع ذلك أي اختلاف الخطاب العطف على جزء الجملة) وهو قوله تعالى : وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا ﴾ [النور: 4] الجزء للاسمية وقع خبراً فيها لمنعه على كلها) لكون المخاطب فيها أيضاً جمعاً (والتالي باطل اتفاقاً فإنه لا بد من العطف على واحد منهما، وفيه نوع خفاء، لأن احتمال جعل الواو للاعتراض قائم على ما جوز بعض النحاة، ثم إن الجملة الطلبية لا يصلح وقوعها خبراً إلا بتأويل القول على ما هو المشهور، فالتقدير : وَالَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ ﴾ [النور: 4] إلى الآخر مقول فيهم فاجلدوا ولا تقبلوا، وحينئذ يجوز أن يكون في الجملة الكبرى الخطاب له عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام وفي متعلق الخبر الخطاب للأئمة، والمعنى والله أعلم : يا أيها النبي الذين يرمون المحصنات قيل فيهم كذا وكذا، وحينئذ لا مانع من عطف الجملة الإسمية على الكبرى لاتحاد الخطاب وأيضاً لا تقبلوا في محل الخبر فلو عطف عليه كان خبراً فيلزم تعدد الخطاب في جملة واحدة بخلاف الجملة الكبرى فإنها لا محل لها من الإعراب فلا يلزم من العطف عليها إلا الاختلاف في جملتين وهذا ليس بتلك المثابة فاحفظه ولا تغلط (إلا يحيي
They are witnesses forever. [An-Nur: 4] If you say: It should have been, according to their view, the dropping of flogging for him based on the return of the exception that follows the sentences to all, he said: Were it not for the prevention of the evidence from its connection to His statement: 'Flog them eighty lashes' [An-Nur: 4] because it (i.e., flogging) is a right of the human being, and it does not drop by repentance, the exception would also connect to it and flogging would drop. I say: What was mentioned as a distinction only holds if the non-acceptance of testimony were not part of the hadd, which is denied. Rather, in our view, the hadd is flogging together with non-acceptance of testimony, and this is suitable for legislating as a hadd because its legislation is for deterrence, and it is also a deterrent, even more severe than beating for people of honor. Then the crime originated from the tongue, so deterrence upon it is suitable, making what originated from his tongue like what originated from the animal. This is like the hadd of theft, for it originated from the hand, so hadd was legislated for it and amputation was commanded. All this is what Imam Fakhr al-Islam indicated by his saying: And based on this we said regarding His statement: 'Flog them eighty lashes and do not accept their testimony ever' [An-Nur: 4] that His word 'Flog them' is the recompense, and His word 'And do not accept' although it is complete, but in that it is suitable as a recompense and a hadd, it depends on the condition, because the recompense must have a condition, so it was made attached to the first. Do you not see that disparaging testimony is painful like beating? Do you not see that it was delegated to the imams? As for His word 'And those are the wicked,' it is not suitable as a recompense, because the recompense is what is established initially by the authority of the imam in the present situation. So its completeness was considered by its form, and it was with respect to the recompense in the status of the subject. And he also said: And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, separated His word 'And do not accept' while proof of connection existed, and connected His word 'And those are the wicked' to what preceded it while proof of separation existed. We said by the form of speech that slander is a cause and inability to produce evidence is a condition in the form of delay, and rejection is a hadd sharing with flogging because it is conjoined with 'waw', and inability is conjoined with 'thumma'. End. If you reflect on this speech, you will find what the author mentioned in a more complete manner and you will be certain of the collapse of what was said that it is not suitable for seriousness, because establishing the hadd is an act obligatory upon the imam. How can it be that refraining from acceptance is an act of his and painful, as this scholar verified. So understand. And it is possible to present the speech of Imam al-Shafi'i from the beginning that flogging also drops by repentance, but repentance in human rights is completed by the pardon of the right-holder, and in his view it drops by the pardon of the slandered. But according to this, testimony should not be accepted except after pardon, which is against his madhhab, so ponder. And for the Hanafis: First, what preceded regarding the connection of the exception to the last (sentence). Second, that what precedes it (i.e., before the verse 'And those are the wicked') is an imperative sentence, and this statement is a nominal declarative sentence, so it is not conjoined to the first. This aspect was indicated by Imam Fakhr al-Islam by his saying: And he connected His word 'And those are the wicked' to what preceded it while proof of separation existed. So ponder, and the waw is interpreted as for interpolation as chosen by some commentators of the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam. As for the narration from him, if it is made for conjunction, then it is conjoined to His word 'And those who slander' [An-Nur:4] because it is the predicate interpreted as a saying, a declarative sentence. And according to this, the exception does not connect to the imperative sentence as well. And this is based on 'those' being a subject. But if it is an object of an implicit verb, then it is necessarily said that the waw is for interpolation entirely, so understand. If you say: Why is it not excepted from 'those'? I say: Then dropping of flogging would also result, so reflect. It was said: The impossibility is only conjoining a declarative sentence to an imperative sentence in what has no grammatical position, whereas here the imperative sentence has a grammatical position because it is a predicate of the subject, so conjoining this nominal sentence to it is not impossible. This only applies if 'those' is made a subject. But if it is made an object of an implicit verb and the imperative is explanatory, then it has no grammatical position, so conjunction is impossible. I say: We have no issue with impossibility; the issue is preference when there is doubt in conjoining to an imperative or a declarative. There is no doubt that similarity is more effective, so conjoining a sentence to its similar is better than conjoining it to its dissimilar, so what was mentioned suffices for preference. And they have a third (argument): the first sentence was addressed to the judges by the evidence of the plural addressee and that establishing hadd is the duty of the imam, whereas this verse is addressed to the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his companions) by the evidence of the 'ka' and its singularity. When the addressee differs, it is not conjoined to it, so the exception does not refer to both. This aspect was indicated by Imam Fakhr al-Islam by his saying: Do you not see that it was delegated to the imams? For delegation, as it can be an indication of it being part of the hadd, so too it can be a preponderating factor for preventing conjunction. And what is in al-Talwih that there is no prevention in addressing the group with the singular 'ka' if it is a letter of address, as in His statement 'Then your hearts became hard after that' [Al-Baqarah:74] and His statement 'So We said: Strike him with part of it. Thus Allah gives life to the dead' [Al-Baqarah:73] and others, is invalid. For the discussion is about real usage, and there is no doubt that 'ka' is used for singular addressee by consensus of grammarians. How could it not be so, for otherwise there would be no benefit in dual and plural? In the examples cited, it is possible that His statement 'after that' is addressed to other than the Children of Israel by way of iltifat (shift) to indicate that they are not worthy of address, and others should be addressed by informing them of their state. And His statement 'Thus Allah gives life to the dead' is not included in what was said; the meaning is 'So We said: Strike him with part of it. So they struck him. Thus Allah gives life to the dead, O you who are fit for reflection and remembrance.' And upon this, analogize. And even if we concede, its use for the plural metaphorically does not harm, as the first-person pronoun is used with another in the singular. Here, if the nominal sentence is conjoined to the imperative, it would require differing addressees if the 'ka' is retained in its real meaning, and taking it metaphorically is against the principle, so it is not conjoined to it. And even if we concede this as well, there is no doubt that it is sufficient as a preponderating factor, so ponder. I say: If that (i.e., difference in addressee) prevents conjunction to part of the sentence (i.e., His statement 'And do not accept' is part of the nominal sentence and is a predicate in it), it prevents it on the whole, because the addressee in it is also plural. And the following is invalid by consensus, for it is necessary to conjoin to one of them. And there is some subtlety, because the possibility of making the waw for interpolation exists according to some grammarians. Then the imperative sentence is not suitable to be a predicate except by interpretation as 'say' according to the well-known view. So the estimation is: 'And those who slander chaste women...' up to the rest, it is said regarding them: 'Flog and do not accept.' Then it is possible that in the larger sentence, the address is to him (peace and blessings be upon him) and his companions, and in the object of the predicate, the address is to the imams. And the meaning, Allah knows best: 'O Prophet, those who slander chaste women are said regarding them such and such.' Thus, there is no prevention from conjoining the nominal sentence to the larger one due to unity of address. Also, 'do not accept' is in the position of the predicate, so if it is conjoined to it, it would be a predicate, resulting in a single sentence having multiple addresses, unlike the larger sentence which has no grammatical position, so conjoining to it only results in difference across two sentences, which is not of that magnitude. So preserve this and do not err. (Except that He gives life).
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usuli dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the interpretation of Surah An-Nur, verse 4. The core issue is whether the phrase 'and do not accept their testimony ever' is part of the divinely prescribed hadd (fixed punishment) for false accusation of adultery (qadhf), or whether it is an additional ruling that can be lifted by repentance. The Hanafis hold that the entire verse—flogging plus rejection of testimony—constitutes the hadd, and thus neither part drops by repentance. The Shafi'is, according to the text, argue that flogging drops by repentance (since it is a human right), and the rejection of testimony is separate; but the Hanafis rejoin that even in the Shafi'i view, the hadd should not drop because the right belongs to the slandered person and only his pardon can remove it. The passage then presents three Hanafi linguistic proofs. First, the exception clause ('except those who repent...') relates only to the last sentence ('those are the wicked'), not to the preceding imperatives, so the hadd remains. Second, the first part ('Flog them...') is an imperative (request) sentence, while the last is a declarative (informative) sentence; it is grammatically more appropriate to conjoin a declarative to a declarative, so the exception applies only to the declarative. Third, the addressees differ: the first imperative is in plural ('flog them' and 'do not accept') addressed to the judges, while the declarative sentence uses 'those' and a singular 'ka', which the Hanafis interpret as addressed to the Prophet (peace be upon him) alone; thus the exception does not apply to the earlier part. The author then engages with objections from al-Talwih, arguing that the singular 'ka' is for real singular address, and any metaphorical use for plural is secondary. He further discusses grammatical possibilities regarding whether 'alladhina yarmuna' is a subject or an object, and how conjunction would work. The commentary concludes that the Hanafi position is preponderant. This passage exemplifies how Hanafi usul relies on a meticulous analysis of Arabic grammar (the 'urf al-lugha) and the precise form of revelation to derive rulings, while also considering the maqasid (deterrence) and prior rulings (like theft).

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): The exception clause introduced by 'illa' (except) in the verse, which the Hanafis argue applies only to the last part of the verse.
العطف (al-'atf): Conjunction with waw; the debate centers on whether 'wa la taqbalu' is conjoined to 'fajliduhum' or to a later part.
الجملة الإنشائية (al-jumlat al-insha'iyya): An imperative/request sentence (like 'Flog them' and 'do not accept').
الجملة الخبرية (al-jumlat al-khabariyya): A declarative sentence (like 'those are the wicked').
الكاف (al-kaf): The letter 'kaf' indicating singular masculine addressee in 'ula'ika hum al-fasiqun'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this passage, why does the exception in verse 4 of Surah An-Nur not apply to the command 'do not accept their testimony'?
A) Because the exception is attached to a declarative sentence while the command is imperative, and it is linguistically preferred to conjoin similar sentence types.
B) Because the command is addressed to the Prophet (peace be upon him) while the exception is addressed to the judges.
C) Because the exception only applies to flogging, not to rejection of testimony.
D) Because the command 'do not accept' is not part of the original verse but an addition by later jurists.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school demonstrates that the scope of an exception in a Quranic verse is determined by grammatical analysis of sentence type and addressee, not by mere conjunction.

Exception of the Repentant: Disconnected or Connected?
Hanafi analysis of the qadhf verse exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of istithna' to distinguish between muttasil and munqati' exceptions in Qur'anic verses and evaluate the argument for munqati' in the qadhf verse.

Core Idea
The repentant were sinners before repentance, so the exception must be disconnected (munqati') to preserve textual coherence.

The Qur'anic verse on qadhf (Surah An-Nur 24:4-5) declares slanderers as sinners (fasiqun) except those who repent. A central usul question arises: does the exception remove the repentant entirely from the category of sinners (muttasil), or does it simply exclude them from the ruling of testimony rejection while acknowledging their past state (munqati')? The Hanafi school, as explained by the author of al-Hidayah, argues forcefully for the latter, using a linguistic and rational analysis that avoids contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical structure
Locate the general term ('fasiqun') and the exception phrase ('illa alladhaba tabu') in the verse. Note that the exception follows a general statement.
The verse: 'And those who accuse chaste women... they are the sinners, except those who repent...'
2
Distinguish muttasil vs munqati'
Muttasil exception removes the excepted from the general category entirely (they were never part of it). Munqati' exception removes them only from the ruling, not the category (they were part of it but are now excluded).
Hanafi usul accepts both as valid forms of takhsis.
3
Test the muttasil reading
If muttasil, the exception would either (a) remove the repentant from the attribute of sinfulness (impossible because the essence is not included in the attribute) or (b) remove them from the essence of the slanderers, implying they were never sinners.
Option (b) contradicts the fact that they repented – repentance implies prior sin.
4
Confirm the munqati' reading
Thus the only coherent reading is munqati': the exception targets the ruling (rejection of testimony), not the attribute of sinfulness. The repentant were sinners but after repentance they are no longer under that specific ruling.
This preserves the integrity of the text and avoids forced interpretations.
5
Recognize the Hanafi tarjih
The author of al-Hidayah adopts this position, and it becomes the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school. The alternative interpretations require takalluf (forced interpretation) that experts reject.
This showcases Hanafi method: prioritizing linguistic and rational coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) لهم (رابعاً أنه أي استثناء التائبين (منقطع فلا يكون متصلاً مخرجاً لهم عن الفاسقين ولا عن المحكوم عليه بعدم قبول الشهادة، وهذا الوجه مما اختاره صاحب الهداية رحمه الله تعالى (وذلك لأن في الجملة الفاسقون، فلو كان استثناء التائبين متصلاً المشار إليهم بأولئك (وصفة) الذات المشار إليها بأولئك وهم الرامون أو عن صفة الفسق واستثناء الذات الصفة من لا يجوز لأن الذات غير داخلة فيها فبطل الثاني ولو كان الاستثناء (من الذات أفاد عدم ثبوت الحكم للمستثنى وصار الحاصل : وأولئك هم الفاسقون الخارجون عن طاعة الله تعالى، إلا الرامين الذين تابوا فإنهم ليسوا فاسقين بل مطيعين (وهو) خلاف الواقع إذ التفسيق الكل) من التائب وغيره ولو لم يكونوا فساقاً فمن أي شيء تابوا؟ لكن التائبين يصيرون بعد التوبة صالحين والباقون الخالدون فيه لا أن التائبين غير متصفين به أصلاً (وبالجملة الاتصال من أولئك أو من عموم الأحوال لا يستقيم إلا بتكلف غير مرضي عند الحذاق (لفظاً) كما إذا قيل الاستثناء من الأحوال والمعنى أولئك هم الفاسقون في كل وقت إلا وقت التوبة عنه ويأباه لفظ المستثنى إلا بتقدير مستغنى عنه أو معنى كما إذا استثنى عن أولئك بجعل فسقهم كلا فسق
And they have a fourth [argument]: that the exception of the repentant is disconnected (munqati'), so it is not connected (muttasil) to remove them from the classification of sinners nor from the ruling that their testimony is not accepted. This is the view chosen by the author of al-Hidayah. That is because in the sentence there is 'the sinners' (al-fasiqun). If the exception of the repentant were connected, referring to those indicated by 'those' (ula'ik) (who are the slanderers), or [referring] to the attribute of sinfulness, and excepting the essence (dhat) from the attribute is not permissible because the essence is not included in the attribute, so the second [alternative] becomes invalid. And if the exception were from the essence, it would imply that the ruling does not apply to the excepted, and the result would be: 'Those are the sinners who rebel against Allah, except the slanderers who repent, for they are not sinners but obedient.' This is contrary to reality, because the designation of sinfulness [applies] to all, including the penitent and others. If they were not sinners, from what did they repent? Rather, after repentance they become righteous, while the rest remain in sin—not that the penitent were never characterized by sin. In summary, considering it connected either to 'those' or to a general state cannot be established without a forced interpretation that is not acceptable to the experts. Verbally, as if one were to say the exception is from states, meaning 'they are sinners at all times except at the time of repentance,' but the wording of the excepted phrase forbids that without a superfluous assumption. Or in meaning, as if one were to except from 'those' by making their sinfulness complete sinfulness, but this is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Qur'anic verse on qadhf (slander) regarding the exception of the repentant. The Hanafi position, as chosen by the author of al-Hidayah, is that the exception is munqati' (disconnected). The reason is that if the exception were muttasil (connected), it would either be from the attribute of sinfulness or from the very essence of the slanderers. The first is invalid because the essence is not within the attribute. The second would imply that the repentant were never sinners, which contradicts the fact that they sinned and then repented. Thus, the only coherent reading is munqati', meaning the exception removes them from the ruling of rejected testimony after repentance, but they were indeed sinners before. This avoids contradiction and is the position adopted by the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): A linguistic operation that excludes something from a general ruling.
متصلة (muttasil): An exception that is directly connected to the general term and removes the excepted from the ruling entirely.
منقطع (munqati'): An exception that is not directly connected; the excepted was never included in the general term but is excluded from the ruling for a different reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, what is the ruling on the exception of the repentant slanderers in the verse on qadhf?
A) The exception is muttasil, so the repentant were never sinners.
B) The exception is munqati', meaning the repentant were sinners before repentance but are excluded from the ruling of testimony rejection after repentance.
C) The exception is invalid, so the repentant remain under the same ruling as non-repentant slanderers.
D) The exception applies only to the attribute of sinfulness, not to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's acceptance of munqati' exception allows for a coherent reading of the verse: the repentant were sinners but after repentance they are no longer under the same ruling.

Shart's Legal Properties and Its Placement in Speech
Comparing shart to istithna and debating its grammatical placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that shart is like istithna in legal effect, identify the exception regarding following sentences, and apply the rule of taqdim taqdiran to analyze conditional clauses in legal texts.

Core Idea
A condition (shart) is legally like an exception (istithna) but applies to the whole sentence in a deferred manner, and it is considered fronted in estimation.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains two interconnected rules: first, the analogy between shart and istithna, and second, the grammatical and legal discussion on the rightful position of the condition in speech. Understanding these principles is crucial for interpreting conditional clauses in legal texts, as the placement of the condition affects the scope and binding force of the ruling. The Hanafi school defends the view that the condition is always considered fronted in estimation, even if it appears later in the actual sentence, and this has significant consequences for legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core analogy
The text states 'al-shart ka al-istithna' fi al-ahkam' (the condition is like the exception in rulings). Identify what this analogy means: both shart and istithna modify the ruling they are attached to. Note the exception: shart applies to all preceding sentences collectively in a deferred manner, while istithna does not.
The analogy is limited to 'exceptive rulings' (al-ahkam al-istithnā'iyyah).
2
Understand the placement rule
The condition has the right to be at the beginning of speech (al-ṣadārah) and is considered fronted in estimation (muqaddam taqdīran). This means even if the condition appears later in the sentence, it is treated as if it were at the beginning for legal purposes.
This is similar to interrogative particles (istifhām) and optative particles (tamannī).
3
Analyze the Basran grammatical objection
Basran grammarians argue that in sentences like 'akramuka in dakhaltu' (I honor you if you enter), the part before 'in' is an independent predicate (khabar) and the result (jazā') is omitted. The Hanafi response is that the preceding part is not an independent assertion; it indicates a restricted honor, so it would not be false if the condition is not met.
The lack of jussive mood on the verb before 'in' is because fronting invalidates the operation of the conditional particle.
4
Examine the Kufan grammatical support
The Hanafi position aligns with the Kufan school, which permits fronting the subject and argues that fronting can indicate secondary meanings (like emphasis) without changing the primary attribution. Intuitive perception (wijdān) may miss these nuances.
The commentator cites that the Kufans allow fronting the subject, while Basrans treat it as a subject-initial sentence.
5
Evaluate the practical implication
The practical effect is that in legal texts, a condition always restricts the preceding statement, even if it appears later. This affects rulings like oaths, contracts, and ritual obligations where conditions are attached.
For example, in a conditional oath, the condition is considered operative from the moment the oath is uttered, not when the condition is mentioned.

Source Text

Issue: Because they said: The condition is like the exception in legal rulings except in its following sentences, for it is not like the exception, but rather for all it is advanced in estimation, since its right is to be at the beginning of speech like the interrogative and optative. And it has preceded that the condition also necessitates a ruling contrary to what it excludes, like the exception, and perhaps the similarity is in exceptive rulings. Then the statement that the condition is delayed, since it contradicts the statement of the Basran grammarians, he intended to invalidate it and said: As for the Basran view in examples like 'I honor you if you enter' – what precedes is the predicate, i.e., an independent declarative sentence, and it is not a result; and if he had said 'what precedes is a sentence' it would be more comprehensive, and the result is omitted because what precedes indicates it, and therefore it is not in the jussive mood even though it is a present tense verb, while a present tense verb is put in jussive as condition and result. In it there is that this statement does not indicate except an honor restricted and suspended on entry, so what precedes is not an absolute report of honor, and therefore it is not considered a lie on the assumption of not honoring due to lack of entry; if it were an absolute ruling, it would be a lie. And the restriction, i.e., restricting honor and suspending it on entry twice, is not understood by necessary intuitive perception; so what precedes is not a report of a restricted honor interpreted as the estimated result, like the sentence occurring after the implied object on the condition of interpretation, e.g., 'Zayd, I struck him.' This, and as for their saying: What precedes is not put in jussive if it is a present tense verb, we say: Perhaps it is because fronting invalidates the operation of the conditional particle, so reflect. It is said in the glosses of Mirzajan on the commentary of al-Mukhtasar: Its analogy is what they (the Basrans) said: In 'Zayd stood' there is a pronoun as the subject, and what precedes is a subject, and intuitive perception denies it, for what is understood from it in fronting and postponing, i.e., fronting the explicit noun and postponing it, is one, namely the attribution of standing to Zayd. Therefore a pure Arab who had not heard grammar rules did not differentiate between them, i.e., between fronting and postponing, in meaning. So the truth is with the Kufan scholars who permitted fronting the subject, according to what the author of al-Muhakamat narrated. And I heard from the master of divine secrets, Abi (may Allah sanctify his secret), repeatedly that this narration does not correspond to grammar books, for the scholars of Kufa and Basra all agree that the subject does not precede at all, and in the case of fronting, the explicit noun is a subject by agreement. Therefore they agreed on the agreement of the verb with it in fronting in singular, dual, and plural because it carries a pronoun, not in the case of postponement; rather they required the verb to be always singular. So reflect. I say: The scholars of eloquence agreed on the difference between the two forms of fronting and postponing according to secondary meanings, which are the qualities and advantages additional to the original intended meaning understood from speech, such as being a refutation of denial, etc. Here, from it in fronting, a confirmed ruling is understood, not in postponing. So the denial, i.e., the denial of intuitive perception of the difference, is perhaps due to the lack of a natural faculty for understanding subtle points of speech. And as for the lack of difference of the pure Arab: if he is an uneducated non-eloquent person, it is not considered if his lack of difference is granted; how could it be, when he does not differentiate between 'what I said' and 'what I said' (with 'an'), for the former indicates negation of speech from the speaker while affirming it for someone else, unlike the latter which indicates negation from him while remaining silent about others, and so on from such speech? So what is not considered is the lack of difference between 'I' and 'with'.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage discusses two related rules: (1) Shart (condition) is like istithna (exception) in all legal rulings, except that shart applies to the entire sentence in a deferred manner, whereas istithna does not. The condition's rightful place is at the beginning of speech, like interrogative and optative particles. (2) The Basran grammarians argued that in a sentence like 'I honor you if you enter,' the part before 'if' is a predicate, and the result is omitted. The commentator refutes this, arguing that the protasis ('if you enter') modifies the meaning, so the preceding part is not an independent assertion. He then engages in a broader grammatical dispute about fronting (taqdīm) and postponing (ta’khīr), siding with the Kufan view that fronting does not change the primary meaning but can add secondary nuances. The discussion emphasizes that legal interpretation of conditional statements must consider the governing purpose and the rules of Arabic usage. The commentator refutes Mirzajan's analogy and upholds the Hanafi usul position on the placement and effect of shart.

Key Terms
الشرط كالاستثناء (al-shart ka al-istithnā'): A principle stating that a condition (shart) and an exception (istithna') share the same legal effect: both modify the ruling of what they are attached to, except that shart applies to all preceding sentences collectively in a deferred manner, while istithna' does not.
الصدارة (al-ṣadārah): The rightful position at the beginning of speech, which the condition is entitled to in estimation, similar to interrogative and optative particles.
التقديم التقديري (al-taqdīm al-taqdīrī): A concept meaning that something is considered fronted in estimation even if it appears later in the actual utterance. Applied to shart, it means the condition is treated as if it were at the beginning of the sentence for purpose of legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, how is a condition regarded in terms of its position in speech?
A) It is always placed at the beginning of the sentence
B) It is considered fronted in estimation even if it appears later
C) It follows the result in order
D) It has no fixed position and depends on the speaker's intention

Key Takeaway
The condition in Hanafi usul is legally like an exception, applies to the whole sentence, and is always considered fronted in estimation, which affects how conditional clauses are interpreted in legal texts.

The First Proof: Command Form Indicates Obligation from Quranic Narrative
First proof for amr = wujub from Quranic narrative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the syllogism using Quran 20:93 and 72:23 to prove that the command form indicates obligation, and evaluate the objections regarding specificity of the verses.

Core Idea
Leaving a commanded act is disobedience, and every disobedience is threatened—thus the command must be for obligation.

In this lesson, we examine the first of several proofs used by Hanafi usul scholars to establish that the imperative form (sigha if'al) indicates obligation (wujub). The proof is drawn from two clear Quranic verses: the story of Musa rebuking Harun and the general threat against disobedience. By analyzing the logical structure of this argument, you will understand how Quranic narratives serve as evidence for usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Find the verse where leaving a command is called 'disobedience' (asi). Quran 20:93: 'Did you disobey my command?'
The label 'asi' implies sin.
2
Identify the major premise
Find the general threat against disobedience. Quran 72:23: 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell.'
This is universal.
3
Combine to conclude wujub
If leaving a commanded act is disobedience, and disobedience is threatened, then the act must be obligatory (since only obligations carry threat for leaving).
This is a syllogism: sughra + kubra = result.
4
Address the objection of specificity
Some argue that 'amri' in the Musa verse is specific (ahdi) and that the verse is not general. Consider the response: one can replace the minor premise with Quran 66:6 about angels.
This shows the proof is robust.
5
Address the objection about the major premise
Some limit 'whoever disobeys Allah' to disbelievers. Note that the Hanafi respond by appealing to consensus (ijma') that any disobedient is threatened.
Ijmā' strengthens the major premise.

أن العصيان بمخالفة الأمر لا بمخالفة الصيغة، ويدفع بضم الإجماع على أن الصيغة أمر فتذكر (أقول : بهذا الدليل تمسك الشافعية في كتبهم على أن صيغة الأمر للوجوب (وقد غفلوا أنه ينفي ما ادعوه من كون المندوب مأموراً (به فإنه لو كان مأموراً به كان تاركاً عاصياً بالدليل المذكور بعينه وكل عاص متوعد كما ذكر، فتارك المندوب متوعد هذا خلف (وهل هذا إلا تناقض إلا أن يراد تارك المأمور به بصيغة افعل مجردة عن القرائن عاص، والحاصل التقييد في الصغرى وفيه ما فيه فإن دليل إثبات الصغرى عام فلا يصح التقييد، وفيه أنه لا تنافي بين كون المندوب مأموراً به وبين كون الصيغة للوجوب، فإن معنى الأول أن المندوب تعلق به صيغة الأمر، ومعنى الثاني هذه الصيغة للوجوب، ولا شبهة في عدم المنافاة بينهما . وحاصل الاستدلال أن تارك ما وقع عليه صيغة أفعل حقيقة عاص وكل عاص متوعد، وهذا غير واف، فإنه ليس الغرض أن بين مفهومي الدعويين تنافياً، بل إن هذا الاستدلال ينفي كون المندوب مأموراً به لأن الصغرى كلية هي أن كل تارك مأمور به عاص، وإرادة ما وقع عليه الصيغة حقيقة لا يزيد على ما ذكره المصنف والدليل العام ينفيه وإذا كان هذا الدليل ينفيه فادعاؤه مع اعتراف مقدماته في قوة التناقض فافهم واستقم
He argued first by [saying] that the one who leaves the commanded thing is rebellious (asi), based on the saying of Allah, may He be exalted, narrated from Musa: 'Did you disobey my command?' [Ta-Ha 20:93], addressing his brother Harun, meaning: Did you replace me among my people? He said that when he wished to go to the Mount to receive the Torah, as Allah, may He be glorified, narrated in His Book. And every rebellious one is threatened with punishment by His saying, 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell' [al-Jinn 72:23]. So the one who leaves the commanded thing is threatened, so the command is for obligation. (In al-Tahrir, the addition of 'amri' is a specified one, and we do not accept its being devoid of a countervailing indication. That is, the seating of the Prophet as caliph was for executing Allah's rulings, so it was obligatory for the caliph to accept it. So what was said that the command here is devoid of a countervailing indication is invalid. This is easy because of His saying concerning the angels: 'They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them' [al-Tahrim 66:6]. So we can change the argument and prove the minor premise by this verse. (And it may be denied that the proof of the major premise) necessitates it, namely that every rebellious one is threatened, based on that what is meant by 'whoever disobeys Allah' is the disbelievers by the evidence of perpetuity and eternity, and carrying it to long duration is extremely far-fetched; the arguer cannot claim it. The better way in proving the major premise is to turn to agreement, for there is consensus (ijma') that the rebellious one is threatened. This: It is refuted by what passed that the utmost that follows is that rebellion is by opposing the command, not by opposing the form. It is repelled by adding consensus that the form is a command, so remember. (I say: This argument is held by the Shafi'is in their books that the command form is for obligation, and they have neglected that it negates what they claim of the recommended being commanded. For if it were commanded, the one leaving it would be rebellious by the same argument, and every rebellious one is threatened as mentioned, so the one leaving the recommended would be threatened. This is a contradiction, unless what is meant is that the one leaving the commanded thing by the form if'al devoid of countervailing indications is rebellious. The result is restriction in the minor premise, and there is something in it, for the proof of the minor premise is general, so restriction is not valid. And there is in it that there is no contradiction between the recommended being commanded and the form being for obligation, for the meaning of the first is that the recommended is connected to the command form, and the meaning of the second is that this form is for obligation, and there is no doubt about the absence of contradiction between them. The gist of the argument is that the one who leaves what is truly covered by the form if'al is rebellious, and every rebellious one is threatened, and this is not sufficient, for the goal is not that between the concepts of the two claims there is contradiction, but rather that this argument negates the recommended being commanded because the minor premise is universal: everyone who leaves what is commanded is rebellious, and intending what is truly covered by the form does not increase over what the author mentioned, and the general proof negates it. And if this proof negates it, then claiming it with admission of its premises is in the strength of contradiction. So understand and be upright.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents and analyzes the first proof that the command form (sigha if'al) indicates obligation. The proof is based on two Quranic verses: (1) Musa (AS) addressing Harun (AS): 'Did you disobey my command?' (Quran 20:93), where leaving the command is labeled 'asi' (disobedient), and (2) the general threat against disobedience: 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell' (Quran 72:23). The syllogism is: leaving a commanded act is disobedience (minor premise); every disobedience is threatened (major premise); therefore, leaving a commanded act entails threat; thus, the command must be for obligation (since only obligatory acts carry threat for leaving). The author then addresses objections: Some (like in al-Tahrir) argue that the command in the Musa verse is specific (ahdi) and not general, and that it is not devoid of an indication (since Harun was appointed for a specific task). But the author counters that this does not harm the general proof; one can instead use the verse about angels 'They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them' (Quran 66:6) to establish that leaving the commanded act is disobedience. Another objection denies the major premise that every disobedient person is threatened, arguing that 'whoever disobeys Allah' in Quran 72:23 refers specifically to disbelievers (due to the word 'everlasting'). The author rejects this as far-fetched and suggests the better course is to rely on consensus (ijma') that the disobedient person is threatened. Then a further objection is raised: the argument only proves that opposing the command (amr) is disobedience, but not necessarily that opposing the command form (sigha) is. The response is that consensus establishes that the form is a command. The author then critiques the Shafi'i position: they use this same argument to prove the form is for obligation, but they also claim that the recommended (mandub) is 'commanded' (ma'mur bih). The Hanafi argues that this is inconsistent: if the recommended is truly commanded, then leaving it would be disobedience, which is threatened, but leaving a recommended act is not threatened. So the Shafi'i claim is contradictory unless they restrict the argument to commands without countervailing indications. But the Hanafi responds that the minor premise ('one who leaves a commanded act is disobedient') is universal, and restricting it is invalid because the proof from the Quran is general. The passage concludes by explaining that there is no real contradiction: the Shafi'i can hold that the form is for obligation while also saying the mandub is ma'mur bih (by the form), but this does not entail that leaving the mandub is disobedience because the form, when used for recommendation, is not a true command for obligation. However, the Hanafi argues that the Shafi'i proof for the form being for obligation actually negates the mandub being commanded, because if the form is for obligation, then any act covered by it without a countervailing indication is obligatory, and the mandub would fall under it only if the form is used in its literal sense, which is counter to the Shafi'i position that the mandub is also commanded by the same form. So the Hanafi punctures the Shafi'i argument as self-defeating. The careful reader must understand the nuance.

Key Terms
العاصي (al-asi): The one who disobeys a command; here specifically, the one who leaves the commanded act is called asi, implying sin.
الوعيد (al-wa'id): Threat of punishment in the afterlife; the text argues that every 'asi' is threatened, but some interpret 'asi' here as disbeliever.
الصغرى والكبرى (al-sughra wa al-kubra): The minor and major premises of a syllogism. Sughra: leaving a commanded act is disobedience. Kubra: every disobedience is threatened.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first proof for amr indicating wujub, what is the role of Quran 66:6 (about angels not disobeying Allah's command)?
A) It replaces the minor premise from the Musa verse to show generality.
B) It proves the major premise that all disobedience is threatened.
C) It shows that the command form can be used for non-obligatory acts.
D) It is used to establish that the command in the Musa verse was specific.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi proof for amr = wujub relies on the syllogism that leaving a commanded act is disobedience, and disobedience is threatened, thus the command must be for obligation.

The Shafi'i Contradiction: Why the Mandub Cannot be 'Commanded' According to the Hanafi Proof
Critique of Shafi'i position on mandub using the same proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi criticism that the Shafi'i position on the mandub (recommended) being 'commanded' contradicts the very proof both schools use to establish that the command form indicates obligation.

Core Idea
If the recommended were truly commanded, leaving it would be disobedience—which is false—so the command form cannot cover the recommended.

This lesson delves into a crucial intra-usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Both schools accept the proof from the story of Musa that the command form indicates obligation. However, the Hanafi argue that the Shafi'i claim that the mandub (recommended act) is also 'commanded' (ma'mur bih) is logically inconsistent with that very proof. By analyzing this contradiction, you will deepen your understanding of how usul principles must be internally coherent.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i claim
The Shafi'is hold that the mandub (recommended) is ma'mur bih (commanded) by the same imperative form, meaning it falls under the scope of amr.
This is a key difference with the Hanafi.
2
Recall the Hanafi proof for wujub
The proof: leaving a commanded act is disobedience (asi), and disobedience is threatened (wa'id). Therefore, the command form indicates obligation.
This is accepted by both schools.
3
Apply the proof to the mandub
If the mandub is truly commanded (ma'mur bih), then leaving it would be disobedience, and thus threatened with hell. But that is false—leaving a mandub is not a sin.
This is the crux of the contradiction.
4
Examine Shafi'i attempts to avoid the contradiction
The text mentions that the Shafi'i might restrict the minor premise to commands without countervailing indications (qara'in), but the Hanafi respond that the proof is general and cannot be restricted.
The Hanafi also note that proving the minor premise from Quranic verses is universal.
5
Conclude the Hanafi refutation
The Hanafi hold that the Shafi'i claim about the mandub being commanded is incompatible with the proof they themselves rely on, thus revealing an internal inconsistency.
The practical result: for Hanafis, the mandub is not ma'mur bih; it is merely talu (required) without threat.

Commentary
This passage presents and analyzes the first proof that the command form (sigha if'al) indicates obligation. The proof is based on two Quranic verses: (1) Musa (AS) addressing Harun (AS): 'Did you disobey my command?' (Quran 20:93), where leaving the command is labeled 'asi' (disobedient), and (2) the general threat against disobedience: 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell' (Quran 72:23). But the author counters that this does not harm the general proof; one can instead use the verse about angels 'They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them' (Quran 66:6) to establish that leaving the commanded act is disobedience. Another objection denies the major premise that every disobedient person is threatened, arguing that 'whoever disobeys Allah' in Quran 72:23 refers specifically to disbelievers (due to the word 'everlasting'). The author rejects this as far-fetched and suggests the better course is to rely on consensus (ijma') that the disobedient person is threatened. The response is that consensus establishes that the form is a command. So the Hanafi punctures the Shafi'i argument as self-defeating. The careful reader must understand the nuance.

Key Terms
المندوب (al-mandub): The recommended act; an act that is demanded but not obligatory; leaving it is not sinful.
القرينة (al-qarina): Countervailing indication that diverts the command form from its default meaning of obligation to another meaning like recommendation or permission.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction; the Hanafi argue the Shafi'i position is self-contradictory when they simultaneously hold the proof for wujub and claim the mandub is commanded.

Refutation of Tawaqquf: The Hanafi Proof for Knowing the Meaning of Amr
Refuting the view that we must suspend judgment on the meaning of amr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the linguistic meaning of amr is knowable through combined rational and transmitted evidence, or through sufficient conjecture (zann) from induction (istiqra') and supporting qara'in.

Core Idea
The meaning of amr is known — through reason, induction, and widely transmitted scholarly practice.

This passage presents the Hanafi refutation of those who claim we must suspend judgment on the meaning of the imperative (amr). The mutawaqqifūn argue that neither reason nor transmission can give us certain knowledge. The Hanafi response demonstrates that knowledge is attainable through multiple avenues: reason can cooperate with transmission, induction provides sufficient conjecture, and the widespread transmission of scholarly usage constitutes practical tawatur. Understanding this refutation is crucial for usul al-fiqh because it defends the very possibility of deriving rulings from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
State the mutawaqqifūn's argument: knowledge of the linguistic assignment must come from reason or transmission; reason has no role; transmission either requires tawatur (which they claim does not exist due to disagreement) or ahad (which gives no certainty); therefore, we cannot know the meaning.
This is the 'suspensionist' position.
2
Analyse the first Hanafi counter-argument
The first response refutes the claim that reason has no role. The commentator says reason can have some entry, though not independently; it can combine with a mutawatir premise to yield certainty.
Reason here is a supplementary tool, not an independent source.
3
Analyse the second Hanafi counter-argument
Even if the transmission is ahad, the Hanafi school accepts that conjecture (zann) through induction (istiqra') is sufficient for linguistic matters and for establishing obligations, especially when supported by other qara'in (circumstantial evidence).
This shows the Hanafi acceptance of zann as a basis for action in practical matters.
4
Analyse the third Hanafi counter-argument
The third response argues that the transmission of the meaning of amr is effectively mutawatir when considering the widespread usage of scholars. The existence of later disagreement does not negate earlier consensus (agreement among early scholars that the imperative indicates obligation).
Tawatur here is understood practically, not merely theoretically.
5
Evaluate the overall tarjih
The Hanafi position prevails: the meaning of amr is knowable through one or a combination of the three arguments. The mutawaqqifūn's demand for absolute certainty is unnecessary; sufficient conjecture is enough for legal reasoning.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(المتوقفون قالوا) لو علم الوضع فإما بالعقل أو النقل والأول باطل، كيف العقل لا مدخل له) في معرفة الأوضاع (وأما النقل فالآحاد منه لا يفيد العلم بالضرورة والتواتر لم يوجد لوجود الاختلاف فيه وأيضاً يكون الخلاف حينئذ بهتاً وإذا بطل التواتر والآحاد بطل العلم بالنقل أيضاً، وهذا الدليل لو تم لدل على أن التوقف بمعنى لا ندري معناه كما مر في النقل الثاني من الأشعري. (قلنا : أوّلاً لا نسلم أن العقل لا مدخل له فيه بل العقل قد يكون له مدخل ما كما مر) في بيان طرق معرفة الوضع، نعم لا يكون له استقلال في المعرفة، فإن قلت : استقلال العقل مسلم ،البطلان فلا بد من النقل، وهو متواتر أو آحاد وكلاهما باطلان، فحينئذ لا توجه لهذا المنع، قلت: لو أريد بالعقل استقلاله ينتقل المنع إلى الشق الأخير، فإنا نقول : يجوز أن يكون نقل مقدمة متواتراً ولم يكن مختلفاً فيه، ثم يستمد بالعقل بانضمام مقدمة عقلية، فيلزم المدعي ويقع الخلاف فيه للخلاف في هذه المقدمة العقلية، أو لعدم اطلاع البعض عليها فافهم (و) قلنا (ثانياً) اخترنا أنه آحاد وسلمنا أنه لا علم بل يكفي الظن بالاستقراء) وهو كاف، في اللغويات، وإثبات الفرائض المقطوعة فانضمام القرائن الأخرى (و) قلنا (ثالثاً) اخترنا أن النقل متواتر، كيف لا تواتر استدلالات العلماء تواتر إنهاله وما ذكرتم من وقوع الاختلاف فلا نسلم تحققه سابقاً، بل إنما يتحقق لاحقاً والاختلاف لاحقاً لا يمنع الاتفاق سابقاً) وأما خلاف مثل
Those who suspend (judgment) said: If the assignment is known, it is either by reason or transmission. The first is invalid, as reason has no entry into knowing assignments. As for transmission, isolated reports do not yield certain knowledge, and mutawatir transmission does not exist because of disagreement about it; also disagreement would then be absurd. Since mutawatir and ahad are invalid, knowledge through transmission is also invalid. This proof, if valid, would indicate that suspension means we do not know the meaning, as passed in the second transmission from al-Ash'ari. We said: First, we do not concede that reason has no entry into it, rather reason can have some entry as passed in the ways of knowing assignment. Indeed, it does not have independence in knowledge. If you say: The independence of reason is conceded to be invalid, so transmission is necessary, and it is either mutawatir or ahad, both invalid. Then this objection would have no direction. I say: If by reason is meant its independence, the objection moves to the last part, for we say: It is possible that the transmission of a premise is mutawatir and there is no disagreement about it, then reason is used by adding a rational premise, so the claim follows, and disagreement occurs due to disagreement about this rational premise, or due to some not being informed of it. So understand. And we said secondly: We choose that it is ahad, and we concede that it does not give knowledge, but conjecture through induction is sufficient. And it is sufficient in linguistic matters and in establishing categorical obligations by adding other qara'in. And we said thirdly: We choose that the transmission is mutawatir. How is it not? The reasonings of scholars are mutawatir in their transmission. As for the occurrence of disagreement you mentioned, we do not concede its actuality earlier; rather it occurs later, and later disagreement does not prevent earlier agreement. As for disagreement like...
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage discusses the argument of those who suspend judgment (mutawaqqifūn) regarding the meaning of the imperative. They argue that knowledge of the linguistic assignment comes either through reason or transmission. Reason has no role; transmission either requires tawatur (which they claim does not exist due to disagreement) or ahad (which does not give certainty). Therefore, we cannot know the meaning. The Hanafi response: First, reason can play a role, though not independently. It can combine with a mutawatir premise to yield certainty. Second, even if the transmission is ahad, conjectural knowledge (zann) through induction is sufficient in linguistics and in establishing obligations when supported by other qara'in. Third, the transmission can be considered mutawatir in the sense that the reasoning of scholars is transmitted widely. Disagreement later does not negate earlier consensus. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
التواتر (al-tawatur): Mass transmission yielding certain knowledge
الآحاد (al-ahad): Single or few transmissions not reaching tawatur
الظن (al-zann): Conjecture or probable knowledge
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Inductive reasoning from numerous instances

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the passage, what is sufficient to establish the linguistic meaning of the imperative?
A) Absolute certainty from mutawatir transmission only
B) Conjecture (zann) through induction (istiqra') along with other qara'in
C) The independent use of reason without any transmission
D) Complete suspension of judgment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school refutes tawaqquf by proving that the meaning of amr is knowable through reason, induction, and the widespread transmission of scholarly practice, with sufficient conjecture being adequate for legal derivation.

Refutation of the Sixth Argument for Fawr from Quranic Verses
Refutation of fawr from Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi critique of the fawr argument from 'Sari'u' and 'Fastabiq' and distinguish between linguistic and legal indications.

Core Idea
Commands do not necessitate immediacy; the Quranic 'hasten' is interpreted as before death or recommendation.

This lesson examines a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: whether commands (awamir) require immediate performance (fawr) or permit delay (tarakhi). The opponent cites Quranic verses commanding haste. The Hanafi school responds with six analytical points, demonstrating that the verses do not establish fawr. Understanding this refutation strengthens your ability to interpret divine commands and appreciate the Hanafi methodology of textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing argument
State the opponent's claim: that 'Sari'u' and 'Fastabiq' command immediate action, proving fawr.
The opponent is likely some Shafi'i scholars who argue that the imperative form implies immediacy.
2
Analyze the first Hanafi response: ta'sis vs ta'kid
If commands already indicated fawr, the verses would be mere confirmation (ta'kid); if not, they establish a new rule (ta'sis). Since ta'sis is preferred, the verses do not support fawr.
This argument uses the principle that establishing a new ruling is better than confirming an existing one.
3
Analyze the second response: haml ala al-nadb
The verses are interpreted as recommendation (nadb), not obligation. The practice of the Companions delaying Zuhr and Fajr supports this.
Imam al-Shafi'i himself reportedly interpreted the verses as recommending haste, not requiring it.
4
Analyze the third response: linguistic vs legal indication
The opponent conflates linguistic and legal indication. Even if the verses prove fawr legally, the discussion is about linguistic default, which cannot be overridden without clear evidence.
The Hanafi position is that commands are linguistically neutral regarding immediacy.
5
Examine the fourth and fifth responses: scope and meaning of 'hasten'
If fawr were proven, all obligations would be immediate, but some (like Zuhr in summer) permit delay. Moreover, 'hasten' means before death, not immediate action, as in other Quranic verses.
The fifth response interprets 'sari'u' as 'perform before death', which is a different sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا سادساً) قال الله تعالى: وَسَارِعُوا إِلَى مَغْفِرَةٍ مِّن رَّبِّكُمْ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۳۳] وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاسْتَبِقُوا الْخَيْرَاتِ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٤٨] والمراد سببهما فإن المسارعة إلى المغفرة غير معقول فأريد سببها الذي هو الواجبات، وكذا الخيرات إن أريد بها خيرات الآخرة من المثوبات فلا بد من تقدير السبب، ويمكن أن يقال الخيرات هي نفس أداء الواجبات، فلا حاجة إلى التقدير، وبعد اللتيا والتي الكريمة تدل على وجوب المسارعة إلى أداء الواجبات، فلزم الفور قلنا أولاً) فحينئذ لو كانت الأوامر للفور يلزم كون هذه الآية تأكيداً لها، وإن لم تكن للفور تكون تأسيساً، و(التأسيس أولى من التأكيد) فلا يكون الأمر للفور (فانقلب) الدليل (عليهم ) ولك أن تقول : هذه الكريمة تدل على وجوب المسارعة، فلو لم تكن الأوامر للفور لزم انتساخها ولو بالزيادة، وهو خلاف الأصل، والتأكيد ليس بتلك المثابة، فيحمل عليه دفعاً للنسخ فالأوامر إما موضوعة للفور أو مستعملة تجوزاً والثاني خلاف الأصل فتعين الأول فتدبر (و) قلنا (ثانياً) هذا الأمر (محمول على الأفضلية والندب وإلا لمن يكن مسارعاً ومستبقاً فإنه لا يقال للآتي بالواجب في وقته إنه مسارع، واعلم أنه نقل هذا التأويل عن الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى، وبه استدل على استحباب تعجيل الفجر وسائر الصلوات وأنت تعلم أنه لا يصلح لما تواتر من الصحابة ومن بعدهم الإسفار في الفجر، وتواتر عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من الإبراد بالظهر، ثم إنه لو تم في معرض الجواب فلا يتم في معرض الاستدلال لاحتمال التأويلات الأخر كما سيظهر بعد إن شاء الله تعالى (و) قلنا (ثالثاً : لو تم الدليل الدل) على الفور شرعاً والكلام في الدلالة لغة) فلا تقريب فيه أنه إن أراد أن الأوامر وردت مطلقة، ثم زيد قيد الفور بعده، فهو نسخ لا يصار إليه بلا باعث وإن أراد الأوامر صارت حقيقة شرعية في الفور فيلزم النقل، وهو خلاف الأصل، مع أنه يتم به المقصود فإنه حينئذ يحمل الأوامر الواردة في كلام الشارع على الفور، هذا فتأمل، وقلنا رابعاً : إنه لو تم لدل على وجوب المسارعة في الواجبات كلها موقتات وغيرها أن منها موسعات جائزة التأخير إلى آخر الوقت، اللهم إلا أن يخصص، ثم إن منها ما هو مع مندوب التأخير كالظهر في الصيف قطعاً وقلنا خامساً : المراد بالمسارعة الإتيان بالواجبات قبل حضور الموت، ولا شك أنه لا يجوز التأخير إلى ما بعد الموت، كما في قوله تعالى ﴿وَأَنفِقُواْ مِن مَّا رَزَقْنَكُم مِّن قَبْلِ أَن يَأْتِيَ أَحَدَكُمُ الْمَوْتُ فَيَقُولَ رَبِّ لَوْلَا أَخَرْتَنِي إِلَى أَجَلٍ قَرِيبٍ [المنافقون: ١٠] كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا التَّوْبَةُ عَلَى اللهِ لِلَّذِينَ يَعْمَلُونَ السُّوءَ بِجَهَالَةٍ ثُمَّ يَتُوبُونَ مِن قَرِيبٍ [النساء: ۱۷] ولو حمل المغفرة على مغفرة جميع الذنوب فسببها الإيمان، فغاية ما لزم كون الإيمان واجباً على الفور ، ولا يلزم كون سائر الأوامر له فتدبر، وقلنا سادساً: سلمنا أن المراد بالمسارعة المبادرة إلى الفعل، فغاية ما يلزم منه المبادرة إلى الفعل الذي هو سبب المغفرة، وقد يكون الأداء على التأخير كما في ظهر الصيف، فلا يدل على الفور أصلاً، ثم الأمر على هذا يكون للندب البتة، فإن من أسباب المغفرة ما هو مندوب فلا يجب المبادرة إليه قطعاً فتدبر
And they said sixthly: Allah said: 'And hasten to forgiveness from your Lord' (Aal Imran 3:133) and His saying: 'So race to good deeds' (al-Baqarah 2:148). The intended meaning is the causes, because hastening to forgiveness is inconceivable, so its cause which is obligations is intended. Similarly, good deeds if what is meant are the rewards of the Hereafter, then estimation of cause is necessary. But it can be said that good deeds are the performance of obligations itself, so no estimation is needed. After all that, the verse indicates the obligation of hastening to perform obligations, so fawr is necessitated. We said firstly: Then if commands are for fawr, this verse would be a confirmation (ta'kid) for them, and if they are not for fawr, it would be an establishment (ta'sis), and establishment is better than confirmation, so the command is not for fawr; thus the proof turns against them. But you may say: this verse indicates obligation of hastening; if commands are not for fawr, then abrogation by increase would occur, which is contrary to principle, and confirmation is not like that, so it is interpreted as confirmation to avoid abrogation. So commands are either set for fawr or used metaphorically, and the latter is contrary to principle, so the first is determined. So ponder. And we said secondly: This command is interpreted as preference and recommendation, otherwise one who performs the obligation in its time would not be called hastening or racing. Know that this interpretation is transmitted from Imam al-Shafi'i, and he used it to deduce the recommendation of expediting Fajr and other prayers, but you know it is not valid due to the mutawatir practice of the Companions and those after them of delaying Fajr until dawn, and the mutawatir from the Prophet of cooling down Zuhr. Moreover, even if it were valid as a response, it is not valid as a proof due to the possibility of other interpretations. And we said thirdly: If the proof indicated fawr legally, and the discussion is about linguistic indication, then there is no approximation. If he means that commands were revealed absolutely, then the restriction of fawr was added later, that is abrogation which is not adopted without cause. If he means commands became a legal reality for fawr, then transmission is required, which is contrary to principle, though it would accomplish the goal, for then the commands in the Lawgiver's speech would be taken for fawr. So ponder. And we said fourthly: If it were valid, it would indicate obligation of hastening in all obligations, timed and untimed, but among them are expansive acts permissible to delay to the end of the time, unless specified. And among them are acts where delay is recommended, such as Zuhr in summer definitely. And we said fifthly: The intended meaning of hastening is performing obligations before death, and there is no doubt that delay beyond death is not allowed, as in His saying: 'And spend from what We have provided you before death comes to one of you...' (al-Munafiqun 10) and His saying: 'Indeed repentance with Allah is for those who do evil in ignorance and then repent soon' (al-Nisa 17). If forgiveness is interpreted as forgiveness of all sins, its cause is faith, so the utmost is that faith is obligatory immediately, not that all other commands are for fawr. So ponder. And we said sixthly: We concede that hastening means early performance; the utmost that follows is early performance of the act which is the cause of forgiveness, and performance can be delayed as in Zuhr in summer, so it does not indicate fawr at all. Then the command on this is definitely for recommendation, for some causes of forgiveness are recommended, so early performance is not obligatory categorically. So ponder.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a dispute: the opponent (likely some Shafi'i scholars) argues that Quranic verses 'Sari'u' and 'Fastabiq' command immediate action (fawr). The Hanafi school responds with six points: 1) If fawr were default, the verse would only confirm it, but establishing a new rule is better; thus it does not prove fawr. 2) The verses are interpreted as recommendation, as shown by the Companion's practice of delaying Zuhr and Fajr. 3) The opponent confuses linguistic and legal indication. 4) If fawr were proven, all obligations would be immediate, but some, like Zuhr in summer, are recommended to delay. 5) 'Hastening' means before death, not immediate action, as in other Quranic verses. 6) Even if it means early action, it is not obligatory because the cause of forgiveness may be recommended. Thus, the verses do not prove fawr.

Key Terms
الفور (al-fawr): Immediate performance of an obligation without delay.
التراخي (al-tarakhi): Permissibility of delaying an obligation to a later time within its window.
المسارعة (al-musara'ah): Hastening to perform an action, here interpreted as before death or as a recommendation.
التأسيس (al-ta'sis): Establishing a new ruling or meaning, considered preferable to confirmation (ta'kid).
التأكيد (al-ta'kid): Confirming an already established ruling or meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fifth response, what does 'hastening' (musara'ah) in the verse mean?
A) Immediate action at the start of the time.
B) Performing obligations before death, not necessarily at the first moment.
C) Doing the act quickly without delay after entering the time.
D) It is a metaphorical expression with no practical implication.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses commanding haste do not prove immediate obligation (fawr) because they can be interpreted as recommendation or as applying before death, consistent with the Hanafi principle that commands are neutral regarding immediacy.
